O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the final one of the science of medicine, by its completion the end is reached, and through its explanations and declarations, it seems to me that I have fulfilled that for you with this treatise, which is a section of surgical practice.
For surgical practice is so neglected in our region and time that the science of it is greatly in danger, and its knowledge is lost; and of it there remain only some small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which have been misinterpreted and led to error and uncertainty, until the understanding of it is obscured and its strength and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I will revitalize it with the organization of this treatise in accordance with the method of explanation, clarification, and summarization; and so that it will contain the forms of cautery tools and other instruments of the craft, as well as the declaration's addition and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled craftsman with his own hands is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first be exercised in the science of anatomy, which considers the body's organs, their shapes, connections, and separations;  and he must have knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, their number, and their arrangement;  and of the pulsating and still veins and the places of their origin.
And for that reason, Hippocrates says that with a name it is little, but in the work it is great, especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already stated this in the introduction to this book, for whoever is not knowledgeable in what we have said about anatomy will inevitably fall into error, leading to the deaths of people, as I have seen many such cases, for some are trained in this science but abandon it without knowledge or experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician open a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some of the neck's arteries so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who attempted to remove a stone from a man who was advanced in years, and the stone was large.  He went at it and pulled out the stone, taking a piece of the bladder with it, so that the man died on the third day.
And I, having already tried to extract it, saw that due to the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew beforehand that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke in our land for surgery, and went to a blackguard who had fractured his thigh; and this physician, through his ignorance, treated the fracture, on the wound, with bandages and splints with excessive tightness, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he loosened it according to his desires, and then he bound it up, and then he left it for several days and commanded that we loosen the bandage.  And it remained like that until the thigh and foot were abscess-ridden, and it was lost.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to loosen the bandage, and calmness was achieved, and thus he was relieved of his pain.
However, corruption had already taken hold in the limb, so I could not stop it, and the corruption continued in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it ulcerated over several days, until its malignancy was greatly aggravated by the patient's condition.
Since cancer is typically caused by melancholic humor, it is not appropriate to treat it with an iron cautery throughout, but rather to treat the affected limb as if everything were to be removed from it.
Therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical procedure be divided into two parts: one is an operation associated with health, and the other is an operation that is dangerous in most situations.
And I have already noted this throughout this book where an operation contains error and fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to avoid this and abandon it, so that mistakes don't find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and God's protection, and your patients with skill and firmness.
And use the best path, by God's grace, seeking health and praiseworthy success, and abandon the terrible illnesses of difficult healing, and remove from your tools what makes you fear, so that your devotion and your name don't fail: for more remains for your glory, and more highly in the world and behind your holiness.
Gualia already said in some of his teachings/instructions/admonitions: Do not treat a bad illness, so that you may not be called bad doctors.
And I have divided this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered from head to foot, regarding the forms of instruments and the tools for cauteries, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, on incision and perforation, and cupping, and sores, and the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, on restoration and dislocation, and the treatment of twisting and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary for me to state the benefits of this cautery and its harms, and in what constitution cautery is forbidden.
Therefore, I say that the discourse on the nature of the benefits of cautery and its harms is a long and verbose discourse, subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, but a small part of their discourses will suffice for me, lest I prolong it.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits every constitution universally, which is with or without matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the opposite, that cautery is not suitable for an illness that is due to heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry illness with something that is hot and dry be cured.
And those who say the opposite claim that cautery is beneficial in all hot and dry illnesses that affect the human body, for when you compare the human body and its humours to the constitution of fire, you find the human body to be cold.
And I say, concerning this matter, that experience has revealed this to me on several occasions.
However, it is not appropriate for someone who has not already been experienced and practiced in the chapter on cautery through previous exercises, and who has not understood the diversity of human constitutions, the nature of illnesses, their causes, the symptoms, and the appropriate time for treatment, to comment on this.
But in other constitutions, there is no doubt, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, for all physicians have agreed on these, and there is no disagreement on the benefit of cautery in those.
And know, children, that the secret of healing through cautery with fire, and its efficacy, is different from cautery with burning medicines, because fire is simple, its action only reaches the part that is cauterized, and does not harm other parts joined with it except with a small amount of harm.
But the action of burning medicines reaches parts further away, and easily causes difficult-to-heal illness and severe pain in the affected part.
However, fire, due to its subtle nature and the goodness of its substance, does not do this except to a minor extent.  We are already familiar with this through experience and long study of the art, understanding the truth of causes.  Because of this, I am excused from a lengthy explanation.  And if it were not that my book already discusses in detail the secret use of fire, and its effect on the body and the expulsion of illnesses, with a philosophical and demonstrable account that would be useful to you, I would dwell on it.
And know, children, that they disagree about the time when cautery is best done, and they consider spring the best time.
And I say that cautery is appropriate at any time, because the harm from the time of the accident is outweighed by the benefit that cautery brings, especially if the cautery is needed for sudden, acute pains, which cannot tolerate delay, for fear of the harm caused by the disease outweighing the small harm caused by the time factor.
And this does not apply to your concerns, children; only the ignorant populace and foolish physicians believe that the illness cured by cautery is not always appropriate, and hold to this belief.
And the reason is not as they suppose, for cautery is merely a method of medicine that alters the complexion and dries the moisture that causes pain.
But nonetheless, cautery is superior to medicine in terms of its power, the effectiveness of its operation, and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, depending on the patient's complexion, the location of the illness, the patient's constitution, and the accumulation of superfluities in the body, as well as what they have ingested in food and similar things; however, if the illness for which cautery is administered is a localized illness with only a small amount of superfluity and moisture, such as toothache and similar cases, it is likely that the pain will not return, but this will happen gradually.
And it is a common saying that cautery is the last resort in medicine, and it is a true statement, not in the way they understand it, for they believe that there is no cure that is as effective as cautery.
And the reason is the opposite; the idea that cautery is the last resort in medicine is only true when we have exhausted other methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those methods do not work, and thus, at the end of the process, cautery is used, and proves effective. Therefore, the statement that cautery is the last resort in medicine is not true according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except because of the equal and superior nature of gold.
And they said that the location of the cautery does not matter, but this is not entirely true, for I have tested this and found that it only works this way in some bodies, not in others, and cautery with gold is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you do cautery with gold, it's not clear to you how much gold is needed, depending on the resistance of the gold, and it prepares to receive heat; and if you apply heat to it, the gold will melt into the fire, and sometimes small pieces of gold will fall into the fire, making it difficult for the craftsman to work with it.
Therefore, cautery with iron is performed more easily and more effectively for the purpose of the operation.
And I have organized this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have organized them from head to feet, so that the seeker may find what they want from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization addresses the dampness and cold affecting the brain, of which there are two causes:  one is the cause of runny noses and a multitude of rheumatic fluxes, called colds, flowing from the head to the eyes and ears, and the multitude of sleepiness, toothache and sore throats; and universally of all illnesses that come from cold, such as paralysis, lethargy, and apoplexy, and similar ailments.
The method of this cauterization is this: first, command the patient to be purged with laxative medicine to cleanse the head, for three or four nights, according to the strength of the patient, their age and their usual condition.
And then command him to shave his head, and then place him on a clean bench, with your hands supporting his feet.
And then place the blade of your palm (presumably a cautery) near the base of his nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark the area with a probe, and then apply the olive cautery, of which this is the method:
And then place that (the cautery) upon the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, while your hand is gradually tightened, and you turn it.
And then gently lift your hand and examine the area; and if you see any amount of bone exposed, about the size of a grain of millet, then lift your hand, and if not, turn your hand with the iron, using another one of the irons, until you see the bone as I have told you.
And then take a small amount of salt and dissolve it over the area, and leave it for three days.
And then place a cloth soaked in broth over it and leave it there until the scab from the fire is destroyed. And then treat the area with four-drug ointment until it is healed.
And if the wound remains open, the better it is to bandage the arm, because it is better and more helpful.
And some of them would say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until a certain part of the bone's substance has been burned.
And then that which has been burned off the bone should be scraped off, and then it should be treated.
And others would say that with the cautery one should penetrate to the back, until it presses strongly on the bone until the bone yields, and this is the way of small cauterization: And they consider that vapors from the head are escaping from that place.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
But I have not seen these two kinds of cauterization in all cases, only in some men, according to their fear; and, in my opinion, it is better to avoid it if possible, because the head is weakened by a break in its continuity just as we have testified in other limbs, and especially when the patient's head is of fragile nature.
And the first kind of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and with that use and method, it is healthier.
Second Chapter.
Of cauterization of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain affects the entire head of a prolonged ill person, and treatment using leeches, cupping, head purges, plasters, poultices, herbal remedies, and similar methods has been used, but none of these things help, then consider that if the patient's head is strong by nature, and not frail, and is experiencing a very severe cold, then cauterize with a second cautery slightly beneath the first one. And then cauterize that area on the side of their head with a cautery until the abscess is broken open. Cut open the abscess and expose the bone to the extent we have described, then cauterize that area on the back of the head in the location called the *vas* (vein) — it is the area where a cupping device is placed. And be careful with your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience very severe pain, different from the pain of other head cauterizations.
And I would say this cauterization is in the right place, for the cautery used on the sides and back of the head needs to be a finer instrument than the one used in the middle of the head; and this is how it should be done.
Third Chapter.
On the cauterization of non-chronic migraines.
When pain in the head is sudden or spreading to the eyes, the head should be cleansed with medicines, and the other treatment, which I have described in the sections on illnesses, should be administered. If this proves ineffective, then cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with a sharp, burning medicine, or with iron.
However, cauterization with a burning medicine is when you take a garlic clove, remove the skin, and cut off the tips of both ends. And then, you carefully cut the painful area on the temple with a sharp lancet until a small, hidden cavity is formed beneath the skin.
Then, press on it with a bandage to constrict the area, and leave it for three hours; and then remove it and discard the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a piece of cloth soaked in broth until the area heals.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, use some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron, according to this method:  use fire for cauterization, and this is the method:
and it is called a "claus clavilhs," because its head is shaped like a nail, with some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small elevation.
Then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand – so that the surrounding flesh is not burned – and turn the small iron slowly but with speed.
And if the amount burned by the intensity of the cautery is as much as half of it, lift your hand so that the artery below is not burned, lest there be a flow of blood.
Then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; then apply cotton moistened with butter, and then treat with ointment until healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the blade of the scalpel, according to the cautery method, and be careful not to cut an artery in this specific, not very ancient, procedure.
Fourth Chapter.
On the cauterization of ancient migraines.
When you treat a migraine with the methods described in the previous treatment and what we've discussed in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not work, and sometimes the illness does not respond to what we've said about the first cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire, then you must heat the scalpel cautery until it is white-hot, then apply it to the painful area in a long line, half the length of your finger, or so; and press your hand down once, and press firmly until you cut through the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be mindful of the continuity of the jawbone, which is stressed by chewing, and that the muscle is not strained, and the nerves which control it are not affected, and that no spasm develops.
And if it concerns the care and caution of the flow of blood from the severed artery, for in its severing there is a fear, and especially for the one who does not understand what they are doing and is not knowledgeable or experienced, and leaves the operation unfinished at the beginning.
And I will bring a reminder of the treatment for accidental blood flow from the artery according to its method, in its proper place in this book.
And if you see that this illness does not respond to this cauterization and you find the patient's condition tolerable, cauterize the area in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it is healed.

Chapter 6.
Of cauterization for tumors.
Tumors that are cured by cauterization are only of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type that arises from dryness or nerve spasm.

And place your cautery on the opposite side of the diseased area, for softening only occurs in the area that appears healthy.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery at the right side of the ear's extremity, a little above the temple of the head, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the index finger.  Hold your cautery there until the amount of the tumor's size is half consumed/burned.

Chapter 16.
Of cautery of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and the eye is inflamed.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that the eye is inflamed, cautery in those cases is in two ways, for cautery is either with burning or with a burning medicine.
If with a burning cautery, command the patient beforehand to cut their hairs as those who groom them, until they are extended and aligned.  And if you burn it near its origin, then you should tightly bind the eyes with a bandage to prevent harm until the hair grows back.
Then, when they have grown back and aligned, place the patient's head on your lap, and gently apply a cautery to the eyelid of their eyes using a probe.  The shape of the cautery should be similar to a leaf of mint, and the beginning of the mark should be close to the hairs.
And then place cotton soaked in albumen of egg or in ptisane poultice on the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize over the shape you have marked, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is shaped like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
The sign of successful treatment is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted, and the hairs raised towards the puncture in the eye.
And then lift your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a border over it until the scab is removed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if, due to any cause, the hair regrows around it, and the eyelid becomes softened, then apply the cautery again in exactly the same way as you did at first.
But if the hair is under the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural position, smoothed, and the hair is no longer irritating the eye.
But the cautery with a burning medicine, which you prescribe to the patient, is to allow the hair to grow out and smooth, and then a sheet of cloth in the shape of a myrtle leaf is to be placed over it.
And then take a known herb and a live coal, or similar, and mix these well, doing this quickly so it doesn't cool down; and then spread this mixture onto the cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf, and place it over the one or both eyes; and place under the eye a cloth soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be resting on your lap.
And place your finger on the medicine and press it gently, and do not remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for the burning sensation is like a fire.
And therefore, when he perceives the burning sensation, then remove the medicine and take it away with your finger; then when the burning ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; then consider if the eyelid has been lifted as if it were released from the constriction with fire or incision, for then it is good; and if not, return to applying the medicine to the area where the medicine wasn't applied and do not let it be black until your treatment is complete and the eye is healed.
And then apply a cloth over it until the burning sensation is gone, and then treat it with a specific ointment or another remedy until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, in the course of your treatment, to guard with vigilant care that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after a few days becomes softened, and some cause of the hairs are in the eye, then repeat your medicine on that part of the eyelid that is softened, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and then continue until it is healed.
And this is the method/procedure for treating an eye injury/problem.
And know that the eyes of men are diverse in size and smallness, and therefore, your treatment is done accordingly.
And the path to recovery will not be hidden from the one who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
Of cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment is not effective, then you must cauterize it in this manner: tell the patient to rest their head on your lap, and have another attendant hold the patient's head between your hands in such a way that it is not moved or agitated by their head.
And then place a cotton pad soaked in albumen (egg white) or in a mucilage (of plant gum) on their eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's wing, at the end used for cauterization.
And if you wish, it should be pointed towards the other end. And if you want, it should be shaped like a probe.
However, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and drain and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
Then place the heated cautery on it, and hold your hand a little away from the cautery near the nostril, so that your hand or the patient is not disturbed.  Then place the cautery on the bottom of the sore and cauterize it.
And so if at the beginning your cautery penetrates to the bone, good.  If not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, then apply a poultice with a bandage and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply an aggressive, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is scraped as appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier doctors said:  apply to the site of the fistula and make a cut there, and then put into that fissure a small amount of melted lead, of the shape it is described: and place it in there, the amount of 1 ounce of melted lead.  And hold it in place with your hand firmly, and so the patient is not moved by the melted lead not reaching the sore;  and it is necessary to put on the wound of the patient a poultice dipped in egg white or in water, for the lead will adhere to the site of the fistula and will heal it with a marvelous cure.

And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs, and placed a cushion under them, and another attendant is on their legs, and another on their chest, who holds their hands.
Then place the cautery over that mark with your hand, using a straight cautery, and hold it there until with it you can reach the bone of the shinbone (or "pentenill," likely a specific part of the leg bone) to the middle of the (the) shin; and repeat with it another time if you haven't succeeded in reaching the bone the first time.
And carefully observe the protrusion of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn that intestine, lest the patient experience death or another serious harm.
And know that if, using the cautery, you do not reach the bone, your operation is not successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be subtle, according to their size and proportions.
And then treat the cauterized area for three days with a bandage until the scar from the burn is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and wound are sealed.
And it is necessary to regulate the patient's nourishment according to the length of their healing time, carefully monitoring their stomach, so that the intestine does not come out due to the effort of healing.
And then, when approximately 40 days have passed, apply a properly made bandage, and let him keep it on for another 40 days.
And he should abstain from strenuous work, overeating, and excessive drinking and shouting, for if he follows this treatment regimen, he will be entirely healed.
And I will now describe the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the abdomen.  If, at the beginning, you want to prevent them from appearing, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, just as the third part of its circumference is cauterized. Then treat it as previously described, and it will be healed.
And some early practitioners would say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, following this shape, since it has been located with a probe:
and a line is drawn along the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; then, apply a wedge-shaped cautery with your hand, but the first, lighter cautery is better.
Chapter 46.
Of cauterization for twisting (or torsion).
When pain persists in some limb due to injury or impact, and that pain persists for a long time and you do not achieve healing with medicinal treatments, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be of a size appropriate to the limb's size and weakness, its location of twisting, and the pain in it.
So, if it is healed by the first cauterization, good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization; for it is customary for such pains to be treated by puncturing the limb near the afflicted one, therefore it is necessary to apply cauterization until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some leprous individuals receive great benefit from cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that, consider if the leprosy is contagious and has been treated with what we have said in the division and is not worsening, and if you fear that the corruption might spread throughout the patient's body and affect their whole constitution, then cauterize the area above the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, a second below that, even with the forehead near the hairline; and two more on either side of the temples; and one at the back of the neck, or in the nape of the neck.
And finally, press the cautery slightly into the bone until a small depression is formed, and the skin is raised, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is relieved; and cauterize on the spleen as required.
And if the leprosy has already spread throughout the patient and is clearly visible, then you must cauterize with the cauteries mentioned for the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on both cheeks, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the hips, and one on the hips, and with two on the ribs, and one on each rib, and with two on the thighs, and with two on the muscles, and with two on the tendons of the feet; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on the joints of their hands.
And be careful of the nerves located on the heel so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the hip bone and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from lancets, because the body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small, depending on the limbs and joints, according to the specifications of the tools.
And then treat the cauterization with flour of herbs cooked with honey and the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of cauterization for numbness.
When a limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate the skin a little. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness is cauterized, which affects the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back, and the branching of the nerves which affect that limb, and the numbness is removed.
But do not presume on this unless one is knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the branching of the nerves throughout the moving body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of a skin disease called morphea.
When the skin disease is longstanding and you do not find any remedy among medical ointments, then you cauterize over it with a cautery, applying it a little, according to the intensity of the condition, until the whiteness and the discoloration are removed, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils, mixed with roasted oil and arnica leaves, and with part of dove's blood or swallow's blood, all mixed together.  Then, apply a linen cloth and place it over the affected area until healed.
Chapter 50.
Of the cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you want to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it is rolled/encircles.
And the learned say it should be cauterized with the back of the cautery in the middle place; but I do not see this, because I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have seen this a few times myself.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 11.
Of cauterization of tumors/growths.
When a tumor has been delayed in maturing, and this is due to excess causing it, or to the patient's age, because they are old and frail, with little blood, or due to time, and you wish to hasten the tumor's maturation, then cauterize around it with many small, puncturing cauteries; and then leave it, for it will hasten in maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the tumor until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place that is lower, so that the arm's condition is alleviated; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
Of the cauterization of corrosive (or: ulcerative/destructive) conditions.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which eats away at it as if a consuming fire eats dry wood.
Therefore, if that corrosive is in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, then you apply small, pointed cauteries, and larger ones as appropriate to the area where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it from every side until you eradicate all the corruption and no trace of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and apply dressings of oil mixed with something to the cauterized areas until the scabs are all gone and all the corruption is eradicated.
And then treat it with flesh-generating ointments.
But if after three days the flesh grows healthy in the area where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes a corrosive medicine is used as a substitute for cautery. However, cautery with fire is a more immediate cure.
And I have already mentioned the treatment with sharp medicine in the division, so take it when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of ulcers and similar conditions.
Many times there are ailments beneath the sick foot called ulcers, a large, gaping wound, which pains the foot; and cauterization for these is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is to ignite a concave cautery resembling a small oven, made of iron according to the size needed to contain the ulcer completely, and it should have fine edges.
Then place that heated cautery on the ulcer, and then rotate your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer. And leave it for three days until it begins to heal. Then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for this will eradicate the ulcer from its roots.
And then treat the area with a healing ointment until it is healed.
And if this doesn't work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you should apply cauterization with water. Take a cannula of brass, iron, or a piece of a vulture's wing and place it over the ulcer. Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand steady, rotating the cannula with the pressure of your hand.  Press the edge of the cannula firmly against the base of the ulcer, and the hot water will reach the root of the ulcer. Let the patient feel the effect of the hot water for an hour, and then remove it, for the ulcer and its roots will be eradicated.
And thus do this to each one and then the other, until you have treated all those that are affected by it on the body.
And then treat those pus since the roots of the ulcers are eradicated with these ointments that cause flesh to regenerate.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers tremor due to cold in the nerve or a cold fever, or another similar cause, then it is necessary to cauterize that person with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one between each vertebra; and with a cautery on the soles of their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive oil cautery.
And the tremor in him/her subsides and hastens the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the cauterization of white spots (or blemishes) on the body.
When white spots appear on the body, and there are pustules or fissures caused by corrupted, thick matter; then, from the beginning when they appear, the affected area at the head of each fistula should be cauterized with a slender cautery made of myrtle wood, the tip of which is heated; or with long roots of herbs; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, a cautery is applied at the beginning of their expulsion, using this method;  and if it is not effective, it will break up the excess causing them, and thus cure them.
However, this must be done after an evacuation with bloodletting.
Chapter LVI.
Of cauterization of an accidental blood flow by archery incision.
Many times, an archery wound that is incised towards the plague, which is outside, or towards the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar cases, is difficult to heal.
Therefore, if this happens to someone, then you should press with your hand on the opening of the archery wound, and place your index finger over it, pressing it repeatedly until the blood flow is stopped under your finger and it does not come out again.
And then place the small and large olive cauteries, numerous in number, on the fire, and blow on them until they are well ignited.
And then take one, either small or large, depending on the location and wound where the artery is ruptured, quickly placing it on that vein after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is cauterized and constricted.
And if the blood is expelled from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and apply the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from the cauteries prepared in the fire, and thus do not cease doing this repeatedly until the blood is constricted.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and that further harm comes to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to constrict it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods: either with cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities of the patient are likely burned and the blood is constricted, or that it be tied with thread or ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines whose virtue is to constrict blood be applied to it; and they be pressed with a cushion with a proper constriction.
However, whoever administers proper constriction with ligatures and tight bandages or by applying cauterizing substances, will only have success after a delay.
But if this happens to someone and the physician is not present nor is medicine available, then proceed with calmly pressing the finger on the opening of the vein as we have described, and attempt to greatly constrict it until the blood is stopped from flowing, and the finger remains on the wound; and over the wound, sprinkle with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and further consider whether cautery or medicine is necessary.
Of incisions, perforations, leeches, phlebotomy, sores, and similar conditions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter all illnesses in which cauterization with fire is appropriate, along with burning medicines, and the occasions and causes for them, and the instruments necessary for various types of cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the supremacy of the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker of this knowledge will be helped in understanding what they have asked.
And before I begin this, children, you must understand that in this chapter, this is a continuation of what is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And therefore great caution is needed, for in the operations of this chapter, often evacuation occurs involving blood, which sustains life, during the opening of a vein, or incision of an abscess, or removal of a suture, or healing of a wound, or the removal of an arrow, or the incision of a stone, and similar procedures. And all of those are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in their occurrence, death often results.
And I warn you that this should not happen to you, for sometimes, you will find yourselves dealing with many different types of sicknesses in many ways. And some of these, because of their illness, approach death easily;  this is because of the severity of their illness, the length of their suffering, and the illness itself, which signifies impending death.
And among those is one who gives you their possessions and declares that they expect health, yet their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to those who come to you with this kind of illness;  let your caution be greater than your desire and your eagerness. Do not presume anything about it, but rather seek certain knowledge that verifies it for you, and to which a commendable outcome will follow.
Employ prognosis and indication of the path to health in the treatment of all your patients, for this will assist you in gaining fame and remembrance, praise, and glory.
O, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold his guidance and protection, for that is in his power.
And I have organized this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, just as the previous chapter on cauteries did, for the treatment of the head to the feet, so that you may find what you are seeking and what you require in it.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water contained within the heads of children.
Know that this illness often comes to children after birth, and when the nurse tightens the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it comes about due to hidden causes that are unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children; and of all those I saw, death followed, and therefore, I leave the work on them.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head of him was always thus swollen until he could no longer support himself due to the size of his head, and that the moisture continued to increase until he died.
And this moisture is either accumulated between the bone and the skin, or is accumulated beneath the bone on the spine.
When the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the abscess is small, then you should make a cut in the middle of the head with a fissure in the width, and the fissure's length should be about two fingers until the moisture runs out. This is the way of a spatum.
And so, if the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures according to this method:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of it is that you see the seams of the head opened from all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press it with your hand on the sides, and this is not hidden from you. You should cut in the middle of the head with three fissures according to this method:
And then remove all the moisture; then bind the fissures with cloths and bandages; then apply wine and oil to it until the fifth day, and then loosen the bandage and treat the wound with bandages and ointments, and bandage the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry foods with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, considering where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or in the right, or in the left; and therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the collection of water appear, and do it as best as possible for you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow and the patient die from that blood flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Second Chapter.
When there is a persistent cold or sores on the eyes or feet, and the usual medical treatments are ineffective, then the final treatment in this case is incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise those, where the patient's illness affects the head, first rub the area with a rough cloth to make the arteries apparent, then bind the patient's neck with a cloth; then consider where the vein pulses; and locate the places, which are near the ears, and press them, and these (arteries) are hidden in some men.
And then gently probe the artery with a probe, and then make an incision along each artery, using a sharp instrument, ensuring the incision goes into the bone, and this should be in line with the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the instrument beneath the artery and sever it at the points above.
And the length of the incision should be about two fingers' width, for when any vein is cut, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which leaps out with frequent spurts.
But if the artery is not apparent by feeling, then you must measure from the ear the amount of space of three fingers, and then probe with a probe, and proceed into the bone.
And the amount of blood to be removed is approximately a cup, and it may be more or less depending on the amount that appears from the patient's constitution and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then separate it so that a harmful abscess does not develop.
And then compress the wound with lint and cloth bandages, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When, in someone, an old migraine or acute headache arises, due to acrid humidities and qualities in the temple tissues, or a similar, old and violent one, and it is treated with the known methods of medicine, and this does not work, then we have tested, in these illnesses, the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or the cauterization of those, as we have shown.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those is that the patient shaves the hair that is on the temples; and look for the artery appearing on the temple because it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden except for a few people due to the violence of the cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or warm the area with hot water until the artery appears clearly to you. And then you take the instrument of which this is the shape: And then you gently scrape the membrane with that until you reach the artery; and then you apply heat there and extract that to its highest point until you extract it from the membrane and uncover the tissues underneath it, on all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the tip of the instrument, and then cut off a portion of it according to the amount by which its extremities are elongated and contracted, so that no blood flow occurs, for if they are not cut, the blood will not rise fully.
And then remove the blood of one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong, double-folded silk thread or strong cords, and not cut it until the wound is consolidated, lest there be a flow of blood.
And then cut the excess that is between the two ligaments; and do this at that time or another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery until the bone is reached, with a knife cautery until its extremities are cut, and it will remain in the same condition.
And it is a better course of action if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot constitution; for cautery is from those things that help eliminate moisture, and therefore is better in a cooling treatment.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the area should be filled with old lint, and powdered medicines should be placed on it, and then it should be treated with drying medicines that cause flesh to grow and with poultices until it is healed.
And if, while working, a flow of blood from the artery occurs again, then you should try to stop the blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the area with lint and pressing your hand until the blood is stopped. And if any of those things are not available to you, then place your finger on the area until the blood has clotted, and apply water of extreme coldness until the pain subsides and it is tightened as necessary.
And the easiest way to extract an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, using two knives, after you have marked the two spots with a probe, and heating each one intensely until they reach the bone and sever the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is elongated, because it is not completely consolidated by this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two knives is an example. However, if there is less sharpness than two knives, it is better, for if it is sharp like two knives close to each other, it will not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is contained within them, and the flesh is cut away easily.
And this work is better than any other work and more effortless.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the care of bodies suffering from acute and acidic ulcers of the eyes.
When the eyes are acidic, and the blood vessels around the forehead are inflamed, and you find no medical cause for this condition, and the patient's face begins to redden, and you feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the eyelids are swollen, moist, and their skin is irritated, and the eyelids are irritated by the acidity of the tears,  the ancient practitioners of medicine used a certain procedure for this condition, and they called that cure "spassa.
And you instruct the patient to shave the hairs on their forehead, and then make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, following the length of the forehead. And if the length of the incision is two fingers, the first incision should be equidistant from the nose along the middle of the forehead, the second approximately the length of the distance from the movement of the muscles near the tear ducts, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to cut the blood vessels that lie on both sides, and keep your hand away from the area connecting both jawbones. And make each incision about three fingers wide.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which you absorb the blood.
And then place the spatula with its two sharp ends, of which this is the form of the incision on it: which is from the temple area to the second incision. Scrape away all the flesh between the two incisions, using the chisel which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument called a scalpel, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of this against the flesh adjacent to the flesh, and its blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision continue in this manner along the flesh, and then do this in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And when a quantity of blood flows, press the spots of congealed blood.
And then place in each fissure a piece of old cloth, and put over these powders mixed in wine, oil, and vinegar, and watch that it doesn't become a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligaments and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basil ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the treatment of tears, and the wounds descending from the parts within the head.
When a patient experiences sharp and continuous tearing, and the wounds on the patient's skin are small and already their care is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their hair is falling out, and there is acute pain and subsequent exhaustion in the front of the head, then know through these symptoms that the matter and tears come only from the many veins of the internal parts of the head.
And the best treatment for this is as follows: instruct the patient to lie down on their forehead, then make a small incision in the middle of their forehead, from the left temple to the right temple,  (beginning with a fissure from the left temple towards the right temple; and let the incision extend to the bone; and keep your hand away from the temples, which are prone to damage from chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the gap between the two edges of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. And then apply a poultice made of powdered herbs mixed with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not form.
And when you remove this and see that the abscess has diminished, then you must clean the bone until flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen to promote the growth of flesh.  This is done by mixing two parts wheat flour with four parts colophony, to make a poultice.  This poultice is used in these wounds to encourage flesh to grow.
Chapter 6.
Of the treatment of that which affects the ears.
Any ailment affecting the ears belongs to one of four types: either it is a mineral, or something similar to a mineral, like iron or glass; or it is from a plant, such as a thistle or a bean; or it is from a fluid substance, such as water, sour wine, and their like; or from an animal.
And therefore, when a stone or similar hard substance is lodged in the ear, and you place the ear in the sun, and it moves the stone, then pour into it some kind of oil (like olive oil or sesame oil), and then use extraction techniques to remove it.  Make the person sneeze with some kind of stimulant, and press the nostrils to help with the sneezing event. Then, place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and stretch the ear in different directions, because many times it is pushed out with these actions.
And if it is not pushed out, then apply extraction with the instrument called a *gesti epicicarios* (a very small, likely surgical, instrument), of which this is the form:
If it is pushed out with the instrument, it is good; and if not, then administer its extraction with a small, thin, spoon-like instrument (a *pontius*).
And therefore, if it is not pushed out by that, then make a copper tube (a cannula) and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal. Seal around the tube with softened wax and oil so that only the tube is open. Then, extract with your hand using strong traction, for often this helps, as we have described.
But if it is not pushed out in this way, then take a lighted cloth or a cloth prepared for this purpose, a small quantity, and place it on the end of the wound. Then carefully wrap the cloth around it. Then gently insert that into the ear canal, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not pushed out by all the methods we have described, then you must cut it open before it becomes a painful abscess or spasm.
And the way to cut it open is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract the blood according to the method appropriate to the patient's condition, then make a small incision between your fingers, turning the ear outward, and make a small incision in the base of the ear, close to the patient's head, in a well-supported position. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until it reaches the stone, and, once the stone is found, remove it if possible with appropriate instruments; then close the incision immediately, and treat it until healed.
And if the cause of the ear affliction is a swelling that has grown and inflamed, then administer the cure and removal of it; but if you do not succeed in this, then you take a subtle spatula in this manner: and use that to remove the swelling that is lodged in the ear.
And do not do this if you think the swelling is already softened by ear moisture, and place it in small pieces, and remove those with tweezers or with a delicate hand, or with a small incision as we have said, because this will ease its removal.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing, and fill the ear with old cloth, and lie on the side with the affected ear, and if the water is thereby expelled, that is good; and if not, then take small, smooth stones, the length of one's finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient should insert one into the ear canal, and step on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone on the stone in the ear. So do this with a stone until all the water is expelled.
So do this with a stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water has already been expelled, then take some paper or linen, place one end in the ear, and hold the other end near a fire until the majority of it is burned.
And then put more linen in, and do this repeatedly until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula, just as described above for removing the stone.
However, in the extraction of a small animal or similar object that has entered the ear, treat it as described in the section on similar cases; but if it is a large object causing a visible swelling, then administer its extraction with tools and forceps, and this condition is easier to treat than any other thing that lodges in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, if you treat it as described in the section on similar cases and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment doesn't work, then you should expose the ear to sunlight.
And therefore, if any sign of worms appears, then extract it with fine forceps or tweezers; but if no sign is visible, then take a cannula, narrow at the bottom and wider at the top, of which this is the form: Insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount the patient can tolerate; and then, repeatedly draw out the worms with forceful suction, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it doesn't respond to this treatment, then you should plug and seal the area around the cannula with wax as previously described.  Then, administer distillations, which the ancients have used in treating worms; you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your application be the introduction of medicinal oils into the ear using this instrument, of which this is the form: Make it of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom, with a small hole, and a flat top.
And thus, it should be like a funnel, situated within the cavity of the cannula wherever needed. And if you wish, take a reed, wrapping its end neatly with cloth.  Then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or the juice of any herb, or whatever medicinal substance you desire, into the cannula – and that is within the ear.  Then carefully insert the reed wrapped in cloth, and press gently with your hand until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the ear, and the patient feels the oil inside.
And whatever is inserted into the ear should be warmed slightly with fire. And be careful that the substance you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear remedies cannot tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Of the treatment of ear blockages.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' wombs, and this also occurs in some adults, a blockage in the ear canal caused by pus or some flesh growing there; and this blockage sometimes occurs at the entrance to the ear canal, preventing the passage of sound, and sometimes is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the entrance to it, preventing the passage of sound, is in the most difficult cases to cure.
And in those cases where you can hear sound, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And so if a blockage is apparent, open it with a very small instrument, which has this shape:
If its tip has a small width, one side sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin ends so that it doesn't damage the ear.
And if the blockage is due to flesh grown in the ear or its canal, then remove it with fine forceps, and cut away that flesh vigorously until all of it is removed.
And if the blockage is in the ear canal, then take a thin, pointed stick and heat it slightly with fire, then carefully place it into the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have sensed a blockage that the patient finds difficult to hear through, ease the pressure gently, and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a cloth, sized according to the width of the hole you've created, dipped in Egyptian ointment, over the ear.  Do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing, or take some crushed and powdered herbs, and use them.  If the ear is already inflamed, then you must remove the herb and replace it with a new herb wrapped in a compress made from warmed olive oil, until the inflammation subsides. Then continue treatment until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cool water and apply it to the ear, and use other treatments to stop the bleeding, as described in the distillation tract.
Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of a swelling that forms on the eyelids.
Swellings that appear on the eyelids sometimes are moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and cut them away from their roots, and then apply a compress to the area.
But if pain with a flow of blood occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is close to the incisions, and many times they return when some cause remains of their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often in the eyelids of the eyes some cause, which is similar to a swelling in its strength and hardness, and therefore swelling is called.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the operation for them is that you should then consider whether the swelling is arising from something visible on the eyelid and is spreading in all directions; and its cause is slight.
Then make a cut over that with a scalpel, and then slice it from all sides easily until it opens, and then suspend it with a forceps and cut it off.
And if the figure (the swelling) is large, then you should close it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not encounter difficulty because the ointment will restore it and cause it to heal.
And if the size is extending towards the inside of the eyelid, towards the inside surface, then lift the eyelid, suspend the swelling with forceps, so that you can make an incision, and press it from all sides. If the eyelid is incised and spread open, this does not matter.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with whatever promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a tumour on the eyelid above the eye.
A tumour is a growth that develops in the layers of the eyelid above the eye, and is more common in children.  It aggravates the eyes of those afflicted and causes difficulty in seeing clearly in the morning.  They cannot look at the sunlight because the tears are drawn to it.
And because of this, you often see them lying on their faces at all times, and sleeping on their faces; and their eyelids below the eyebrows are damp, already covered in swelling, and the appearance of a tumor is evident in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the tumor within them.
And a certain method of operation is that the patient rests their head on your own, and you then place a cloth over them and warm it.  You then make a circle according to the size containing the tumor on all sides, and place this over it and press it with your fingers so that the tumor is contained within the middle of the circle.  Then, in the centre of that moisture, you create a small incision with a scalpel, just as in the incision made in a phlebotomy.
However, in the process, you must cut through the skin completely until you reach the tumor, for in most cases, the tumor forms a white, greasy substance through a fissure.
Then remove that with cloths that you have wrapped between your fingers on the outside and rub back and forth with your right and left hands until it disappears.
And be careful not to force the incision, so that with the scalpel you do not pierce the eye and damage it.
However, if the tumor does not appear at the beginning of the incision, but you must gently push into the incision until the tumor emerges; and then remove it as we have said; and then soak cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them on the affected area and compress them with powders.
And some rub salt and place it in the cavity of the incision, so that this may remove any remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if there is a festering boil in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.

Chapter 12.
Of raising painful skin in the eye with a needle when the skin is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough hairs that prick the eyes, then the operation for them is that you take a fine needle and place it in that fine thread of silk and linen, and then adjust the ends of it, twisting each one very subtly. And then you add another fine thread underneath that quickly, and tie the ends of that one if you want to.

Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a wound which occurs on the upper eyelid.
When a wound occurs in the eye, and there is a contraction of the eyelid when it is drawn up, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and it is called "leporine" (harelip). This wound, or ulcer, is either natural or accidental, and is caused by poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauteries, or by similar poorly treated injuries.
The method of treatment in such a case is to break apart that scar tissue and separate the edges of the wound, and place a strip of linen between them, and tie them together until they are healed.
And you should not use treatments that are drying or constricting; for if you do this, the wound will return worse than it was before the injury.
Use remedies that soften it, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice with water in which herbs and their seeds are steeped, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil. Soak the linen strip in it and treat with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you should break apart any unhealed scar tissue, restoring it to the shape it originally had.
And this operation, which we have described, is simply a rectification of the scar tissue, restoring the area to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a scar that is on the lower eyelid.
This scar on the lower eyelid, properly called a scar, can be either a natural or an accidental one.
But an accidental scar is caused by a wound, or a cut, or cautery, or other similar injuries.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with a double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and make it pass from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then spread the flesh apart with the needle and stitch it with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. And so if the eyelid shape returns as it should, good.  If not, take the thread and place it in the cleft/gap, and with that draw the eyelid together, and close the two fissures (splits) on the lower eyelid.
And let the extremities of the two fissures be two angles of the incision, which you have made until they meet, and let there be an angle formed by their meeting.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then hold back the flesh, to the extent that it is the sharp edge below it, which is the eyelid.
And then bring the separated parts together and bind them with wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices we mentioned until healed.
And if a raising occurs because of a fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you dissect with a flat incision on the eyelid skin, according to what has been previously described. And then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have said.
In summary, the treatment for a raised swelling, whether above or below, should follow the operation, according to the type of swelling, and this is in a variety of forms.

Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
