Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various fleshy growths appear in the nose, some of which are caused by something resembling a scorpion's stinger, or a fish-like polyp; and this illness is called polyps because it resembles that depicted fish. And of it is cancerous flesh, leprous, dark coloured; and of it is flesh like a lion, not dark in colour.
Therefore, in those fleshy growths that are not fraudulent nor cancerous, it is necessary that you place the patient between your hands, in the sun, and clean their nostrils, and pierce those growths with your lancet. And then pull that outwards, and then cut away what you have grasped of those with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed;  if any part of it remains from which further excision is possible, then cut that with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing more remains. But if it is swollen with blood or a hot abscess, then you apply what is appropriate; or if it is a malignant abscess, then you cauterize it until the blood is drained and all the cavities are cleared.
And then put vinegar and water, or wine, in the nose near the incision. But if the nose is open and moisture flows from it to the palate, then you know it is healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is still corrupted flesh, or excess pus, on the bones of the nose to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, then it is necessary to introduce a piece of lint to which some thickness is added, and place several pieces, placing the quantity of a finger or less between each piece.  Then, you should gently insert one end of the lint into the nose with a probe or other suitable instrument.
And after that is done, pull it [the lint] with its scent until it reaches the passages of the nose, and it comes out on his throat, for many times children in schools do this same thing, and it is an easy thing for whoever wishes to do it.
Then, place the two ends of the lint, one end coming out of the nose, and the other remaining inside the nose.  Then gently scrape away any flesh with the pieces of lint on the lint, and do so until you are sure all flesh is removed from the lint.
Then, remove the lint and place a poultice [or a compress] over the nose, near the area where the blood was removed, and do this for three days or longer until the poultice corrodes any remaining flesh.
And finally, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a quicker cure, use this one.
And this is the form of the instrument for nose-blowing with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose:
And make this of silver or brass, which should resemble a small, open lamp or a small shell, and the opening should be similar.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the nose-blowing instrument should be flat, uncovered; to which there should be a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when heating oil or water or other desired juices and subtle substances in it.
Chapter 25.
Of the ulcers found in the extremities of the nose.
Often a sore appears on the extremities of the nose, and it grows larger and worsens over several days until its condition is very noticeable.
And therefore, you should cut it out at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then apply either a cautery or a burning medicine to the area.
However, if its removal is delayed until it has grown large, then consider if it is hardened, rough, dark-colored, and insensitive.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a stubborn abscess; because often the one who cuts into this abscess may cause great harm to the person who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not dark-colored, and if cutting into it is possible everywhere, then evacuate the patient and cut into it without fear, and treat the area with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suture of the nose, lips, and ear, when the continuity is broken by plague or similar illnesses.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you accomplish little in that operation unless you are a skilled practitioner.
Therefore, when you encounter any of these causes, consider if it is fresh plague with its blood, and if so, you should bring the two lips of the wound together with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the break in continuity are already separated, and each is made whole, then you should scrape each part of its visible surface until blood comes out. And then bring the two edges together with sutures and tighten them, and sprinkle blood of dragon and crushed incense over them, and place a piece of diafinicon plaster, or another of the consolidating plasters, over the powder, and leave it bound for two or three days, and then remove it and replace the plaster, and leave it until the pus is separated, and then treat with ointment until healed.
But the method of suturing is that you join the break in continuity either with needles as we have recounted in the suturing of the abdomen, or join the break in continuity with glue as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the removal of those things that come between the two lips in the inner parts.
Often, hard abscesses resembling grains of corn appear on the inner parts of some men's lips, and some of these are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, you must turn back the lips and cut away each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then cauterize the area with a sharp cautery until the bleeding is stopped, and afterward wash the area with vinegar and treat it with something astringent until healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, flesh adheres to the gums, which the ancients called albulus.  Then you must detach it with a forceps or scissors, and cut it off at its base, allowing the pus or blood to drain.
And then apply a sharp cautery or other styptic or drying powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again, and cauterize it, for the cautery will now prevent its return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with an iron instrument.
When on the surface of the teeth, both on the inner and outer portions, and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes dark, yellowish or greenish scabs form, and the gums are affected by this corruption, the teeth become loose.
And therefore, you should have the patient sit between your hands, place their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth where such scabs and similar gritty substances appear until nothing of them remains, removing the blackness, yellowing, and other discolorations until they are cleansed.
So if you have removed what was initially present in the process, good.  But if not, then you repeat the procedure on them the next day, and the second, and the third, until they reach the desired state.
And know that teeth require many different tools and shapes, according to what your procedure requires. And therefore, the scraper used to scrape the inner portion of the tooth is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, in other forms, and these forms of scrapers must be prepared by you, which you will see soon.
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You should treat the tooth's pain with all care and take your time in its complete eradication, for there is no cause that can replace its use when it is removed, for it is a valuable substance until it is extracted in the procedure of eradication.
However, when the patient needs that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is causing the pain, because many times the pain deceives the patient, suggesting it's in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one.  Then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have seen this through the practice of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which tooth is painful, and knowing this, you should scarify the area around the tooth with a substance that has some medicinal virtue, until the tooth's connection to the gum is dissolved all around.  Then, carefully extract it with your fingers or with very fine scissors, little by little, until the tooth is loosened.
Then, if necessary use strong pliers for good grip or a strong lever, and place the patient's head between your knees to firmly hold them so they don't move. Then carefully pull out the tooth, in a straight line, in such a way as not to break it, and to not cause the patient any further pain.
And if it is not easily removed, then take one of those instruments, and place it beneath the tooth from all sides with ease, and apply force to remove it, as was done initially.
However, if the tooth is fractured or decayed, then you should cover the fracture with cloth and tightly wrap it with a thin piece of string so that it doesn't break when you apply the pliers.
And you must carefully scarify the area around the gum all around, carefully controlling your strength so you don't break it, and leaving a part of it attached.  Return to the patient carefully, so that the pain is not worse than the initial pain.
Beware that you do not do as some ignorant barbers do in their recklessness and audacity regarding extractions, without following what we have explained.  For many times they cause serious illnesses in people, which are harder to cure than if they were extracted correctly, with the roots of the tooth remaining whole, or some of the roots, or by pulling it out with parts of the jawbone, as we have witnessed many times.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be rinsed with warm wine or wine mixed with salt.
And if blood flows from this, as often happens, then place a small amount of alum and sprinkle it on the wound, and if that doesn't stop it, cauterize.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers, with which the tooth is initially grasped, should be long at the extremities, with a short and thick handle, so that they don't bend when using them.
And they should be like a thick handle so that when you tighten them, they don't bend, but have short extremities.
And they should be made of well-forged Indian iron with straight extremities, and on the ends of these are teeth that interlock and grip firmly with a strong constriction.  Sometimes the ends of these are shaped like the beak of a stork (or crane), that is, hooked, and are a strong grip.
Chapter 31.

The ends of them (the forceps/pliers) are made smooth like a file or a rasp.

Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural alignment, and their form is unsightly, especially in women, and particularly in venerable young women; then you must consider: if a tooth is protruding against another tooth, and removal or binding is not possible, then remove it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you cut it with this instrument, whose shape is like a pig's snout; and it is made of Indian iron, with a very sharp point.
And make your incision so that you cut through the tooth's hardness over several days, and that it is not joined to the other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file, of which this is the form:
And it should be entirely of Indian iron, easily sharpened; and it should have very small points, like the file used to make needles, with which the tooth should be filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is joined and broken. And then bind it at the end, and remove its root with some razors.
And if a tooth has a broken part that cuts the tongue, then you must file it until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not corrupted.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver fillings.
When, due to a blow or impact, the teeth break or are displaced, and the patient cannot hold the missing portion, and your treatment with astringent medicines has not been successful, then, in order to fix those that are fractured, use gold fillings, and not silver, because silver turns green and begins to decay in a few days, whereas gold never changes.
And let the filling be of such fineness and thickness as is appropriate to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of application is that you take the filling and place double its size between two sound teeth, and then you shape two curvatures of the filling between the fractured teeth, whether one or several, until you reach the sound tooth on the opposite side. Then repeat the placement until you reach the point where you started, and hold your hand with care and skill until it is secure.
And let your grip, with which you hold the filling against the tooth roots, be firm but not excessive.
Then cut off the excess ends of the filling with forceps or scissors, and place them in each gap with care, and shape them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the fractured tooth so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it like that, tightly secured.
However, if it is loose or broken, secure it or them with another filling in such a way for all time.
And this is the method for teeth and the procedure for binding two healthy teeth and two broken ones, just as these are sometimes joined:
And when one or two teeth are returned to their proper place in the socket, and they are restored as we have described, and remain fixed.
And only skilled and knowledgeable artisans can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is prepared, shaped like a tooth, and placed in the socket where the tooth is missing, secured as we said, and remains in place and serves in that way for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Of the inflammation of the ligament which affects the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which sometimes forms below the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, arising from a wound that has already healed.
And the treatment for it is to open the patient's mouth, their head resting on your lap; raise their tongue, and then cut the nerve ligament along its width until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting, and this is due to a healed wound, then you use a forceps and cut it along its width until you have severed the ligament and the knot is undone.
And be careful not to make a fissure in the flesh and to cut so deeply that bleeding occurs.
And then wash the incision site with water or vinegar or cold water; and then place a linen pad under the tongue; this is a compress that the patient must keep under their tongue every night so it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place powdered vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the area with a blunt cautery appropriate for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the extraction of a ranula (a cyst) under the tongue.
Sometimes a boil resembling a small grape develops under the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, sitting them facing the sun, and examining the boil.
And if you see it to be a dark colour, or black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that area, for it is cancer.
And if it is leaning towards white, in which there is moisture, then you place a lancet on it, and you cut it with a fine scalpel, and you sever it from all sides; and if blood comes out during your procedure, then you put crushed alum, or vitriol, over it until the blood stops.
And then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it has been washed with sour wine and salt; and treat it with the further treatment for this until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses from the ears, and of those that are in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When there is an abscess in the throat, these are glands that have come together, which have broken out and are called the tonsils; when you treat them with what we have said in the division and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, dark-colored, and not very sensitive, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is a reddish color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for the sake of the flow of blood; before, let it ripen, and then either you will open it, or it will break open on its own.
And if it is white, smooth, and its root is subtle, then it is the one that should be incised.
And the procedure for it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already rested it with complete rest or if it has decreased with any reduction; and then make the patient sit facing the sun, and their head should be in your view, and open their mouth, and the attendant, taking between their hands, presses the patient's tongue down with a very noble instrument of which this is the form:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be thin like a knife.

Furthermore, when you pull out any cause of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be of hardened iron.
But if this instrument is not present for you, then cut it with a scalpel of which this is the form: And it should be sharp on one side, blunt on the other side.
And after you have incised one opening, cut the other according to this same method of incision, and then the patient will recover from cold water.
However, if there is a flow of blood, treat it with water decocted from pomegranate rind or myrtle leaf and similar astringent remedies until the blood flow is reduced, and then care for it until healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear near the throat or near those two ears, and cut them according to what I have said about the incision of two openings just now.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat, an abscess tending towards a dark color, with little sensitivity, which had already constricted her throat, and she was breathing with a constricted airway. She had already been forbidden from eating and drinking water, and she would have died if it had continued for another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so much that it had produced two ridges before it burst through two openings.
Therefore, I treated it surgically and placed a lancet on one of the openings, and pulled it out, and a large portion was pulled out.  Then I incised the opening that I knew connected to the other wound, and then opened the patient's mouth, and held her tongue down, and placed the lancet into the abscess.
And then I cut away a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely healed, and she immediately began drinking water, and then eating.  So, I did not stop cutting away from that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in that area, which I incised until it finally resolved.
Therefore, I managed to heal and cauterize the woman's throat, thus bringing about a cure; and then I wandered about and did not know what God had done through me on that occasion.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is suppurating.
When a sore throat affects the uvula, and it is suppurating and long, then it is called a vertical sore throat; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a boil.
However, when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and you do not confirm your cure, and sometimes the boil that has already been treated and is small, then you should cut the one that is swollen and round; and if it is long, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and is not painful, then you should test the incision, for in that there is fear for the patient.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, you should have the patient sit down and press his tongue with an instrument that shows its shape, and then put the incision in the new one, and pull it down and cut it with one of the two instruments that are mentioned in the incision of two boils.
And you should only cut that which is joined to the natural boundary without addition, for if you cut more, you will injure the patient's speech.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two boils, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use cauterization without fear, and the method of cauterization is only with sharp water.  You should position the patient's head on your lap, then press the tongue with the instrument we've described, and take the water, as described in the cauterization chapter, and apply it with a live ember.  Put it neither thick nor thin, and fill the concavity of the instrument, which has this shape:
The end of it should be concave, like the bowl of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula.  The patient should lie on their side, so that saliva or mucus from the mouth is drawn by the medicine, so that no cause descends down the throat and damages it.
Then, apply your hand with the medicine and press it onto the uvula for half an hour until you see it blackened and settled by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and soak the cotton in the medicine.  Then, insert the spoon with the cotton into a tube until the cotton adheres to the tube.  Do this repeatedly until you achieve the desired cauterization effect on the uvula.  Then allow it to dry and fall off after approximately three to four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and then, after cauterization, cleanse the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in broth.  Clean away any remnants of the medicine.  Wash with cold water, and treat externally with an embrocation and internally with remedies until healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incision and less prone to fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with something more subtle than cauterization or incision, for it is treated with fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, Arabic cicadas (likely a misspelling of "cicuta," a poisonous plant), chamomile, borage, and similar herbs, and mix all or some of them in a pot, then submerge them in sour wine and let them boil.  The pot should be securely sealed with a suitable lid, and a hollow, concave metal instrument should be placed in the middle of the pot, shaped in this manner; it should be made of silver or brass.
And place the end of the instrument into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the instrument, until the uvula is significantly clouded.
And then repeat this many times until it's dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the start of an abscess, as it would only spread the abscess, and you shouldn't do this without the diagnosis of a mature abscess.
But if this instrument is not available, then take a reed and attach a hard-boiled egg shell to its end, so the patient's mouth is protected, because the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and things adhering to the throat and other such causes.
Often thorns or fishbones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Then you should extract whatever of these is visible, upon which you focus your sight, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in front of a light, so that what is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which is not visible and hidden in the throat, you should make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for if the cause stuck to the throat will be forcefully expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or let the patient take a piece of dry sea sponge, tie it tightly with a cloth, and then have the patient swallow it, for as it reaches the place where the thorns are, it will be expelled. And further, take an eel and drink it with a cloth tied around it, and let it be placed inside and removed quickly; or take a leek and quickly put it in and quickly take it out.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
And let it be larger than a fingernail, and at its end be a cavity which you can easily insert into their throat.
And have them hold their head up, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not hurt them, and with that push the bone or thorn, or have the physician do it with their hand.
But rather that the sick person themselves insert it, because of their knowledge of the location of the cause and push it downward, and pull their hand with the instrument upwards, all according to the amount which is prepared for them, until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When a bloodsucker is treated with what we have described in the section on treatment with medicine and not with remedies, then consider the bloodsucker in the patient's throat toward the sunlight, after you have compressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have described.
Therefore, if you see the bloodsucker, extract it with a small instrument or with subtle, skillful gestures.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this repeatedly (until the bloodsucker is dead and detached from the throat) and keep the patient away from water all day.
And then take a vessel full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth and rinse the mouth with it; and let the patient not swallow anything and hold the water for another hour with your hand, because the bloodsucker, as soon as it feels the water, falls off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with incense or asafoetida using the instrument we have described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it falls off.
And another way of operating in the fumigation of that, is that you take a pan in which there are burning coals, and the pan is covered with a cover in the middle of which there is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then let the fumigation go; and let the patient place their mouth on the end of the cannula, and close their mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the bloodsucker, for the bloodsucker will fall off as soon as it is reached.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and let them eat salty things and other foods, and do not let them drink water; and thus it is necessary that with this treatment it be expelled.
And this is the shape of the instrument with which the bloodsucker is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps, in a similar way, except that it has some curvature. Which is placed on the bloodsucker, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is covered, on which is filed, so that when we grasp something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discussion on abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are of many kinds, in many ways, according to their appearance, each one discussed in this book.
And they are diverse in their puncturing and incision in two ways, of which one is through the nature of the abscess itself; and by what we grasp of the humors.
And the second way is according to the locations on the body where they occur, for an abscess in the head is different from an abscess in the buttocks; and an abscess in fleshy tissue is different from an abscess in a joint; and each has its own judgment for treatment.
And moreover, some abscesses should not be punctured until the pus has matured within them or they have reached fullness.
Some other abscesses should be lanced while they are inflamed and have not matured to their fullness, such as abscesses near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its presence prolongs until that which is in its vicinity has spoiled, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are strongly corrupted, thus causing a contraction of that limb; or it is an abscess near a vital limb, and if you delay puncturing it until it has matured, it might harm the vital limb; or it is near the anus, therefore puncture it while unripe; and one should not pierce an unripe, incompletely digested one, but rather one near the anus, so as not to injure its lining and cause it to extend into the rectum, creating a fistula, or causing it to become permanently diseased. However, you must know the right time to pierce the abscess, and that is when it has matured.
And you know it has matured when there is a cessation of pain and fever, and a decrease in swelling and throbbing, and the sharpness of the abscess head, and the other signs.
And you must know that the incision should be made in the location of the pus, below the abscess and from the pus resulting from the healing of the elevation.
And the incision should always be made along the muscle or body tissue if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the location of tendons, ligaments, nerves, or arteries; and, in general, in all places where there is no reflection; for in those areas that are reflective, use incision according to that particular limb.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to let it be until the digestion of the area, according to the completion, has been confirmed, as we have said.  For if you incise it before, you prolong the course of its suffering and increase the amount of foul matter, and likely make the abscess walls harder and deeper.
But some abscesses are incised according to the width of the flesh or body tissue, due to necessity, or according to the requirements of the affected limb.
And you should administer a small incision for small abscesses, and a large, wide incision or multiple incisions for large abscesses, according to the varying size of the abscesses.
And others are abscesses where the pus pocket needs to be scooped out and incised, when done as described and the abscess is mature, like those occurring in clusters of boils and carbuncles.
And some are incised with an incision having three angles; and some are incised like a myrtle leaf shape, like abscesses of the armpits.
And some are treated with a circular incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones.
But those that are not like that, such as superficial lion-like abscesses, require you to pierce them with a superficial, or flat, incision only.
And when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, you should pierce it and not be afraid to remove all the pus at once; after removing a portion, close the abscess until the next day, then remove a larger portion, and do this repeatedly according to a schedule until it is completely emptied.
Especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an elderly, frail person, because the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient is easily killed, and you open [the abscess] for them.
Observe this chapter with great care, and when you make the incision to open these abscesses, you must clean the wounds and then consider if the abscess wound is small, or a simple flat incision, then apply ointments of oil or old cloth; but if the abscess is large and the incisions are many, you must put a medicament in each hole until they meet together.
And if a part of the abscess wall has been cut away or hollowed out around its edges, you must fill it with old cloth or linen strips, as when you pull out bread dough or roots, without moisture; and keep that in place until the third day; and then, treat and heal it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
But if you encounter a blood flow during your operation, then apply cool water and vinegar on a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must apply the treatment and remedies that have been described in many parts of this book, and in its divisions.
But if it is wintertime, and the location of the abscess is close to many nerves, then you must mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply that to the affected areas.
And if it is summertime, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil, or with wine and oil, and keep it cool until the third day, as we have said. Then you must open the abscess, cleanse it, and apply the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary to know about the treatment of abscesses in general; however, according to the method of distinguishing each type of abscess, I have already described the specific treatment method for each in individual chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur on the nape of the neck.
Small abscesses, and even some kinds of nodes, form on the nape of the neck, which contain tunics around them; and their various types are various.
And some of them contain pus, and some of that contain moisture similar to lime. And some contain moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, as the extraction of those does not cause contrary harm.
The procedure for incising those is that you probe them with an instrument shaped like this, which is called a trocar, until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then you cut it with an incision along the length, by a superficial incision, in this way:
And begin the incision at line B to line G.  Therefore, once the moisture is evacuated, then you scrape the skin that contained that moisture, and do not leave behind any cause for it, because it will often return if any cause remains.
Then soak cotton in Egyptian ointment, if available, and otherwise in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, because with this you corrode any remaining bits of that skin.
Then repeat applying Egyptian ointment to that cotton a second and third time, if needed, until you are confident that no remnants of the skin remain; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you cut it in this way: with an incision having the shape of a cross, and place hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane that contains it.
But if your effort encounters resistance, then do what I previously told you.
And similarly, do the incision and treatment for the calcified abscess as we have told you; and incision on a calcified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses, which are on the head in some men, in which there is no moisture at all.  The reason for this is that I once incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess to be like hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if someone had struck it with that, it would have hurt them.
But in other abscesses that occur on the head besides this one, this is not the topic, like the abscesses that come to the heads of children and near the roots of the ears, but the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation in those from the lower part of them, so that the body is relieved of the pus towards the lower parts.
And then treat them with whatever is appropriate for their treatment.
Chapter 42.
Of the incision of scrofulas which occur on the neck.
Often these abscesses appear on the neck, under the arms, and in the armpits; and sometimes they occur in other parts of the body.
And those which occur on the neck, and many of them are generated among themselves, and every scrofula of them is within its own proper tunic, and thus, as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the types of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain moisture, and some of them are malignant which do not yield to treatment.
Therefore, in that which you find to be well-disposed upon touch, whose manifest colour is similar to the colour of the skin, and is soft throughout, and is not attached to the neck's nerve, nor to the veins, nor to the arteries, and is not deep, you should incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along the line from G to B, and drain it on every side, and spread the flaps of the skin with forceps, or with many forceps if necessary, as we have described for abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is more important that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And if the pus is not very sharp, so that you don't use your hand with incision, or that it disturbs the patient, and that you do not cut what is not necessary to cut; for if you cut a vein or artery and your operation prohibits it, then put powdered vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood on the wound, and constrict the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides and the wound softens and begins to suppurate. When the blood is separated (or removed).
Therefore, return to your operation until you have completed it.

And then fill the wound with old wool, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then heal it.
And if you cut the entire swelling/sore, then you should bring together the two edges of the wound and stitch it as soon as there is no excess fluid left.
But if you see that there is remaining excess fluid due to the size of the swelling/sore, then you should understand that you need to cut the root of that, and remove that and administer stitches and the other things that were said.
And for those swellings containing moisture, pierce it with a superficial incision where you see the place of maturation, and place the incision where the pus descends from the body, as we have said.
And then administer a poultice, some with ointments and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, you should heal it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Of the incision of the boil of an abscess that has occurred near the windpipe.
The ancients said this incision should be made on the boil, and no one in our land has seen it done.
And this is the written record of their teachings.
However, in people with back pain, the extraction of the boil should be avoided, as it will not be beneficial because all the veins and major blood vessels of the patient are located there.
However, in those who have hot abscesses in the mouth, windpipe, or ears, when the disease is not in the throat, one should use the incision of the boil to avoid the damage caused by cauterization.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the boil below the three or four circles of the boil's surrounding skin, with a small incision in the space between two circles.  The incision should be made in a circular fashion, not in the cartilage, as this spot is suitable for the incision because it lacks flesh and its blood vessels are further away.
And if the physician is cautious, then he should gently extend the membrane of the windpipe with a probe, and then cut the membrane until he reaches the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
And then cut the boil circularly as we described; and the incision on the canal is signaled by the pus that flows out from the ruptured area, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
Therefore, when the time has come in which the perforation is feared, bring together the two edges of the membrane of the wound and stitch them together, excluding the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the procedure we have described, the explanation, is nothing more than to observe whether some of these abscesses have already blocked the patient's throat, and the patient has succumbed to death, and their breath is cut off, and some will then cut the epiglottis so that the patient breathes over the site of the wound for some respiration and so be saved from death.
And for that reason, I command leaving the wound open until the corruption of the illness is consumed; and this corruption lasts for three days; and then, at that time, I will command closing the wound, and treating it until it is healed.
And what I myself have seen is that a servant took a knife and inserted it into her throat and cut a part of her lung's airway with it, and I was called to treat her, and I found her breathing like a dying person, and I uncovered the wound and saw that little blood had come from the wound.
Therefore, I was told that she had not cut a vein or artery, and the wind escaped from the wound, and I treated her until she was healed.
And it did not happen to the servant if she did not have trouble with her voice, and after a few days, she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that there is no fear in the treatment of epilepsy.
Chapter 44.
Of the incision of an abscess which occurs in the throat outside, and is called a hernia of the throat.
This abscess, called a hernia of the throat, is a large abscess according to the color of the body, and is common in women.

And in the natural case, there is no cure.
And the accidental case is in two ways, of which one is similar to fatty lumps, and the other way is similar to an abscess caused by the blockage of an artery, and in that case there is fear (of serious consequences). Chapter 45.
Of the incision of the types of lumps.
There are many types of lumps, and I have already mentioned all the types of them in the division.
And it is necessary that I recount the difference between a lump and a fistula, as there is doubt.
Therefore, I say that with a fistula, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity is calmed; and when the putrefaction is complete, the fever, pain, and heat cease.
However, with a lump, there is no heat, fever, or pain, and it resembles that particular lion which is in a proper pouch.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a boil and is made like a bladder, whether larger or smaller, and there are two types, either fatty and containing moisture, and the colours of moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore, when you reach the treatment of a lump, you should investigate it from the start with the instrument called an *intromes*, as its form will be in the chapter following this one.
And it is a method of investigating abscesses and all lumps, and it is that you take this instrument and insert it into the moistest spot you find in the abscess, and you will rotate your fingers with it little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the membrane.
And then, place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then remove the *intromes* and consider what has come out on its surface.
And if the running moisture, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said in other abscesses.
And if there is no moisture on the surface where the *intromes* touched, then know that it is fatty (or greasy).
And dry it with an incision in the shape of a cross, following this form: In the manner I have shown you, and suspend it with hooks, and carefully remove the skin from all sides, and if possible, keep the skin or sac over it and extract the pus with the forceps.
But if it breaks during the operation, it's not possible to extract the pus completely, and often this happens. Then you extract it piece by piece until nothing remains.
For if any part, small or large, remains, the forceps will return to the pus.
And if you see any small part remaining, then fill the wound with some sharp, corrosive powders, and tightly close the wound, and apply a poultice of hot, cauterizing substance, and heal it with the other remedies until it is cured.
And if it's a large wound, then you stitch the two edges together and treat it with what promotes healing.
But if there is a pulsating vein there, or one that isn't pulsating, then you cauterize and fill the area with crushed vitriol, and leave it there for two or three days until the wound might calm down and the blood's boiling subsides.  Then return to probing the wound.

Chapter 49.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur from arrow wounds or vein wounds.
When an arrow wound is inflicted, and the surrounding tissue has become consolidated, often an abscess develops.  And the signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or a vein: for when the abscess is from an arrow wound, it is long and drawn out along the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a creaking sensation there.
And the one caused by a vein wound is round and apparent on the body's surface.
And incision on this abscess is a mistake, especially if it is beneath the armpits, in the joints, in the neck, and in many other places on the body; and it is very dangerous, so its cure with iron should be avoided, and those on the extremities or head must be avoided.
But for those abscesses caused by inflammation of the arrow wound's opening, make a longitudinal incision into the surrounding tissue; and then open the incision with hooks and remove the arrow, and clear away the tissues until it is exposed.
And then insert a needle and make it penetrate to the other side, and pull the arrow out with a double-threaded cord in two places—which I learned from a teacher in the extraction of two arrows from Templars.
And then prick the area between the two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood is drawn out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment where pus is generated until the ligaments fall away; and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the abscess is due to a cut vein, then you should hold with your hand whatever part of the abscess is accessible from the surrounding area. Then, insert a needle below the area you are holding, and make it penetrate; and use a double-thread through it, until you extract it from the other side. Then, tie up the abscess with a ligature, as I have taught you regarding the grape-vine ligature, in two places with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear pulling out the thread, then insert the thread with the needle below the entire abscess, beneath the initial incision of the first needle, and tighten your thread in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of the affected area until what's within it is released; then you cut away the excess flesh, and leave what's there bound.
And then place a poultice, soaked in wine and oil, on the area, and then administer the treatment made with herbs and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess that occurs due to nerve twisting.
Thus, abscesses occur in the arteries and veins, and similarly, abscesses occur in the nerves when they are injured by impact or excessive strain, and similar causes.
And it is most likely to occur in the area of the tendons or heel, or in any place where the joints are strained.
And it is a serious abscess, and it is similar in color to the rest of the body, and it is in the majority of cases without specific symptoms, and when it is severe and intense, the patient experiences a sensation of numbness.
And it is not an abscess spreading through the body, and therefore it is contained, and it is painful throughout and does not extend to the front or back parts of the body.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be lanced with an iron instrument, for this will easily cause contraction.
However, that which is on the head or forehead can be lanced with a lance. But if the abscess is small, then cut it out with forceps and remove it from the roots.
And if it is large, then suspend it with hooks, and scrape it, and then remove it; and close the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the incision of an abscess that occurs in the part that corresponds to the belly.
Often, in the abdomens of certain individuals, and in other parts of their bodies, there are growths that are called fungi by resemblance to fungi, whose roots are subtle and whose heads are large; and their edges are bent inwards, and they vary in size, being sometimes small, sometimes extremely large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two growths that were more like fungi, and there was no difference between them, both being white with fine roots.
And their edges were inverted and split open, and moisture flowed from them constantly.
Therefore, I incised them and found one to be eighteen inches in length and the other six inches.

Have cauterizing irons nearby, heated in the fire, because often much blood is released during the cutting. If you notice blood flow, cauterize the area.
And if the patient is fearful and anxious, avoid incising with iron; instead, take freshly made lead solder and bind the growth with it, in the manner previously described. Leave it on for two days.
And then, tighten the lead thread firmly; thus, do not cease tightening the lead on the growth until it is severed and falls away of its own accord without effort.
But if the growth has thick roots, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and anxiety, especially in one located in the abdomen.
However, it is possible to cut it in half, and then cauterize the area to prevent it from returning.
And then treat it until the wound is healed.
And be careful not to presume to cut a growth that is dark in color, with little feeling, and of slow growth, for that is possibly cancerous.
And I will bring a remedy for cancer to address this.
Chapter 52.
Of the treatment of swelling of the navel.
Swelling of the navel is caused by various things; either by fissures in the diaphragm which is over the abdomen, and from which tissue or intestine protrudes as in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or by trapped wind within it.
However, if it is due to a fissure in the diaphragm and the protrusion of tissue, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and it is painless, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the extrusion of the intestine, then its location differs greatly from what we have described, and when you press it with your fingers it is concealed, and then returns; and it is very light in that location, and is noticeably elevated above the entrance to the bath and causes considerable distress.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is painless and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor is it swollen.
And if it is due to blood, then the abscess appears with the characteristic sign of a darkening.
But if it is due to dead flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, and hard, and is lodged according to a certain quantity.
But if it is due to wind, then the touch of it is painless.
And the treatment in such a case is to consider whether the swelling near the navel is due to blood from an artery, or a vein, or wind; in which case you should avoid treating it, for in that there is fear and dread, just as you have been taught in the chapter where I discussed abscesses caused by an artery and a vein.
And if the swelling at the navel is due to the intestine or the mesentery, then you should instruct the patient to hold their breath and be standing upright; and then feel around the entire navel with a probe, and then direct them to lie on their back between your hands, and then encircle the navel with a wide spatula around the area marked by the probe; and then elevate the half of the abscess upward with a large forceps; and then bind around the circumference with strong thread or linen in a proper manner of ligation.
And then open the middle of the exposed abscess over the ligament and introduce your instrument into that opening and search for the intestine; and if you have found and held the intestine by the ligament, then you extract it gently and push the intestine back into the abdominal cavity.
And if you have found the mesentery, then take it with forceps; and cut away the excess tissue.
But if it is an artery or vein, then encircle that spot, and then return to your procedure; and take two needles and put strong threads in them and insert the two needles into the circumference you made around the abscess in the form of crosses, making them penetrate; and then compress the abscess in four sections over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and allow the exposed flesh to soften and fall away on its own; or cut away that portion once it has softened, and then treat the area with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the swelling around the navel is due to flesh protruding or moisture, then you must open the abscess as we have described and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, and then treat it with appropriate remedies to consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
Of the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division, the types of cancer and how it is treated with medicine, and that iron is inappropriate for treatment because it does not heal the ulcer; and we have discussed cancer that arises in the mother and the precautions in treating it.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where it is possible to eradicate it, like cancer that is in the breasts or the groin, and similar growths located on the limbs, especially when it is small and in the beginning stages.
But when it is old and severe, it is not advisable to try to remove it, for I have not been able to cure it in any way, nor have I seen another who has achieved this.
And the operation, when it is located as we have said, is that you should first let the patient rest and cleanse them of black bile sometimes, and then bleed them if a noticeable congestion is found.
And then prepare the patient with the preparation in which the affected area is located for surgery, and then introduce hooks appropriate to the location; and then carefully scoop out with a spoon-like instrument to thoroughly search until no root of the cancer remains. And allow the blood to flow, and do not cut it lightly, and express the places, and wipe away the thick blood with your hand or an instrument that is at your disposal.
But if you encounter difficulty with thick blood, from a vein or artery incision, then cauterize it until the blood flow stops, and then treat it with other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 148.
Of the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of water dropsy, and the symptoms of each type, and the treatment of them with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the type of "althitas" (a type of dropsy), alone, and it is not treated with iron for the type of "timpanistres" (another type of dropsy) or the type of "yposarcha" (another type of dropsy), for that is fatal.
And when you have treated this type of tympanistres dropsy with medicines and your treatment doesn't improve it, then consider that if the patient is already weakened, or has another illness, such as another type of dropsy, or has a chest or abdominal illness, or similar to those, then be careful not to treat it with iron, for there is a fear of that.
But if you see the patient with an exuberant condition, and it is only dropsy and not another illness, and the patient is not a child nor an old person, then the method of treating them is that you raise the patient in your arms, and have an assistant press the patient's belly with their hand, expelling the water from below the ribs, and then apply a pointed instrument in this way:
It should be two-pronged, sharp, with a sharp tip like a lance, but nonetheless, let it be somewhat small and gradually shorter so that when you use it in the procedure, you do not pierce the intestines, and injure the patient.
And then consider if the dropsy originates from the intestines, for if so, you must, with the incision, move away from the navel by three fingers to the lower regions, to the right side over the ribs.
But if the dropsy originates from a liver ailment, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right side.
And your incision should not be on the side where the patient will lie, lest the excess fluids run to that constricted area.
And then pierce the entire membrane with the instrument, and then insert into that incision, and lift your hand with a spatula between the membrane and the flesh, and scrape that; and if [the thickness is] the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then pierce the flesh until the spatula reaches the empty space, and that is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the shape: and it resembles a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or brass or lead, on which, at its lower part, is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes: two on one side and one on the other side; and its tip is sharpened in the manner of a feather, as you see.
And at its uppermost end is a little stopper, for when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately closes over the instrument, so as to drain a medium amount of water, for if you drain more than is necessary at that time, the patient is strongly likely to die from the resolution of their vital spirit, or they will suffer a stroke leading near to death.
But drain it according to the amount indicated by its power and according to the indications given to you by the patient's condition, the strength of their pulse, and their general well-being.
And then remove the instrument, and retain the water – the reason for this is that it is immediately retained by the stopper that closes the hole above the part of which I have instructed you to puncture in this manner.
And then repeat the procedure the following day, if the patient tolerates it, and drain a small amount of water, and do this for several days with caution, and be careful of mistakes until only a little water remains.
But if you fear for the patient, and leave a large amount of water, then cure it by burying [the patient] in hot sand, and inducing sweating through bathing, and waiting in the sun for a long time; and cure it with drying medicines until it is healed.
And cauterize on the stomach, the spleen, and the liver after the draining of the water, as I have described in what precedes.

Chapter 56.
Of the whitening which occurs on the foreskin on the head of the penis, and of its corruption, blackness, and adhesion to the head of the penis.

Chapter 62.
Of surgery on a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing more than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which lies below the groin on the side containing the testicles, and it is called the scrotum.
And sometimes it is in the natural sheath that the body has prepared in the area of the testicle, until there is a care that there is already another testicle; and it is the groin area between the white scrotum which we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a swelling.

And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or blood-red, or red, or black and foul-smelling, or watery and white, and that is the most common.
And the signs by which one knows where this collection of water is are these: if it is in the white scrotum we mentioned, then the swelling is round, sloping slightly in length, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle is hidden, because the moisture surrounds it entirely.
And if the moisture is in the proper sheath, then the swelling is round with a portion like the testicle, and for this reason one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the groin of the testicles and the white scrotum, then it is felt deep down.

And scrape away that area, and observe carefully, and be careful not to cut into it; and your scraping, with which you scrape away a part, should be as gentle and subtle as possible, scraping away in accordance with the quantity as possible for you.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a cut that occurs in the weakened area above the abdomen, on the side of the abdominal cavity's edges, thus releasing the intestines from that rupture and letting them fall into the abdominal cavity.
And this rupture is either of the weakened area or of a stretching of it, and these two types happen due to various reasons: either a blow, or a jump, or a loud noise, or the lifting of a heavy object, or similar actions.
And the sign of this, when it is due to a stretching of the weakened area, is that it occurs gradually over a long period of time, and not suddenly, and it swells inward towards the groin because the weakened area is compressing the intestines.
And the sign of it [the hernia] when it is due to a cut in the weakened area is that it initially causes sudden, intense pain; and a different kind of swelling appears under the area near the groin, and this is due to the protruding intestine and the body of the intestine being pushed out of the weakened area.
And sometimes, the intestine drags the membrane along with it, and this is called a circumferential intestinal hernia.
And sometimes it is caused by wind, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and it is trapped there, and this causes significant harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.

And check with your fingers to see if there is any cause of the intestine being wrapped within the hard white tissue. But if you find any cause of this, then you push it back into the abdominal cavity.
