And treat the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that occurs in the joints.
Sometimes, a rupture occurs in the joints as we have said, and you should examine the area.
And do not leave any cause of the intestines in the groin; and if they do, it is little; and it returns at all times.
However, if the time is prolonged with this, a fissure is formed in the groin, until the intestine descends into the groin; and this happens due to the stretching of the groin in the joints, as we have said, which is why the groin is stretched, and soon softened and the area is inflamed and you examine it.
And the cure for it is with cautery, as was previously described.
And sometimes it is cured with an iron according to this method:  the patient lies on their back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture and abscesses, examining it with a cut across the width for about three days.
And then, press on the white groin that is under the hip until it is exposed.
And then take the probe and place it over the prominent area of the groin, and press it against the front of the abdomen.
And then stitch the two prominent areas on the probe's extremity of the groin, and join one to the other with a suture.
And then remove the probe's extremity, and do not cut through the groin completely, nor extend the wound, nor any other cause like this that I have to tell you about in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment of wounds, and as you dress the sores, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is healed; for the groin is contracted and the rupture's prominence is not corrected.
And cauterization is better in this location, for it is closer to healing.
Chapter 68.
Of softening the hardness of the testicles.
Often the hardness of the testicles in some men is softened because the tissues around them are softened, and this is why it is helpful to watch them.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one who is tasked with their care to have the patient lie according to their side, and to cut away all the hardened tissue which is on top of the natural tissue; and then to stitch together the two edges using sutures.
And if you wish, sew the excess of the softened tissue at the beginning with three or four stitches, and with firm sutures.
And then you make incisions between the sutures; and then treat with a treatment for other wounds until it is healed and the pus subsides.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our law, is forbidden, but by reason of necessity, it is permitted to me to mention it in this book; and its mention is not necessary except in two ways.  One is so that a physician, when questioned about it, may understand the treatment of any such injury; and the other way is that we frequently have need of castrating other animals for our benefit, as with oxen, rams, pigs, and animals similar to them.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by a fissure or incision.
However, in the method done by crushing, the procedure is to immerse the beast in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hanging. And then grasp them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear beneath the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, you must hold the beast and press down on the scrotum with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and make an incision on each testicle, until both testicles are separated.
And then cut them further, once you have scraped each one. And do not leave behind any cause from the scrotum, but only the subtle membrane that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration, which is performed by attrition, is better, because strong attrition leaves no cause in the testicles for which the beast desires coition.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men comes in two ways: one is when there appears in the space in the scrotum, between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs.
And sometimes urine flows from that opening in the scrotum.
But in women it is a single type: and it is when the woman's vulva is like two testicles of a man somewhat hidden on the outer part, similar to a man's foreskin, and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: for two types of men and one type for women. And it is necessary that you incise those adhering flesh until the swelling subsides, and then treat those with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type of man, for whom urine is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of lentigo and protruding flesh caused by female conditions.
Lentigo, a mild swelling, is usually caused by natural factors until it becomes tight and the care is successful; and sometimes, in some women, it becomes so large that it spreads out like in men and extends even to the area around the genitals.
Then you should remove the excess lentigo with your hand or with a surgical instrument and cut it away.
And do not overdo the cutting, especially on the root of the tissue, so that you do not cause excessive bleeding.
And then treat that with the treatment for sores until it is healed.
However, flesh that has developed within the womb of the mother until it fills it, and has come out in a localized manner in the form of a mass (for this reason, some earlier practitioners called it "a disease of the mass"); therefore, it is necessary to cut it like we cut lentigo and to treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Concerning the treatment of elephantiasis.
Elephantiasis is a condition where the vagina of a woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental elephantiasis is caused by a preceding illness, which is either due to improperly formed flesh or a subtle or thick cyst.
And it occurs either in the mother's womb, or on her sides, or on the topmost part of her body, or the lowest, and you forbid cooking, contact, and childbirth, and especially strongly forbid menstruation.
And the diagnosis is made by observing the midwife, if the obstruction is apparent, promptly; but if it is not apparent, then you search for it with your words or with the instrument.
But if the obstruction is due to a subtle cyst, and is close to the two lips, then you should heat and break it open.
And you should place powders on both lips, and then place your index fingers of both hands gently on top.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend both lips until that subtle cyst is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool and soak it in oil, and place it over the area, and let her rest with a man that day so that the incision site does not become consolidated.
But if the cyst is large and thick, then it is necessary for you to cut it with a sharp blade or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to flesh, then suspend that with a hook, and cut it.
And have with you the remedies that stop blood flow without causing bites, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and cinquefoil, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep that for several days and apply a poultice of them.
And then let it be cured with other healing treatments until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the major nativity of other flesh, and then it is necessary to incise according to this method. But if this is due to a cranial abscess, no iron should be used in any way against the cranial abscess.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of the sore and red swelling that happens in the vagina of women.
Hemorrhoids are a collection of many small veins from which much blood flows continuously; and when hemorrhoids are longstanding and painful, they are considered to be 'theils' (possibly a type of ulcer or painful lump).
And I have already discussed the types and signs of these [ailments], and will now describe those that are curable and those that are not curable.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and painful lumps, when they are deep inside and not apparent to the touch, should not be treated with iron.
But those which are at the opening of the anus and can be felt, are those which can be cured.
And you must place the woman in a cool place and then carefully remove the [painful] lump with scissors, or a rough cloth, and cut them off at their roots.
And then sprinkle on them the flow of blood from one of the incised powders, without biting, like acacia and similar things.
And then soak their feet for an hour, and then place that [affected area] in cool water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make that [affected area] sit in a decoction of pomegranate rind, quince rind, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a poultice with this and place it on their back.
And if the blood is stopped, it is good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without incision.
And then take wool, and soak it in coriander juice, or juice of arnagloza, or juice of guarravier mixed with diluted wine, and apply it to the affected area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of an accidental abscess in the breast.
Many kinds of abscesses accidentally occur in the breast, such as cancerous ones, stony abscesses, ulcers, boils, carbuncles, fistulas, hemorrhoids, sores, ulcers, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types, their signs, and their treatments in the division.
And it is necessary for me to discuss in this treatise the hot abscess that arises in the breast when it is one of the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know how the operation is performed on it with an iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with throbbing, phlegm, and fever, and sometimes redness, and if, when pressing on it, you feel sensitivity, then do not proceed with puncturing it, but treat it with whatever aids maturation until the sharpness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you must have the woman lie down on a table with two supports;  and she should lie on her back and position her feet towards the lower part of the abdomen, and her thighs should be spread apart, and place her arms under her elbows, and bind each one with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife, standing on the right side, administers the instrument with which the opening in the breast is made, the shape of which will be evident in the subsequent procedures.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you must insert, with the handle, the nipple of the woman's breast, and what enters the breast from the instrument should be no more than the nipple itself, and ease the patient's suffering. But if the instrument is larger than the nipple, then you must place powders on the texture of the nipple to prevent any instrument from entering the nipple.
And you must place the liquid that is on the instrument's handle on top, and let the practitioner hold the instrument and turn the liquid until the breast opening is revealed.
And when the discharge appears, and it is touched by the hand and is leathery and moist, then you must dry that area around the head with a linen cloth.
And when the discharge is evacuated, then you must place a piece of cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil (in which some kind of medicinal substance is present) on the affected area and apply a compress on the section of the nipple.
And place outside the breast, and over the areola, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And it should be treated for approximately two days with an ointment suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And sometimes the breast and the affected area are washed in a mixture of water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled, and subsequently returned to the ointment treatment.
And if the affected area is hidden deep within the breast, then you must refrain from treating it with iron, instead treating it with what we have discussed in the division [of treatments].
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat stillborn babies when their condition deviates from the natural order.
It is necessary for the midwife to recognize the natural birth pattern from its signs.  When you see the woman pressing downwards and eager to breathe air, and she is relieved by the weight of the birth, and hastens the delivery of the baby, then know that this birth is following a natural pattern.  The delivery will be of the baby's head first, and the placenta with it either hanging or suspended from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, the woman's abdomen should be compressed to allow the baby to descend easily, as when it descends headfirst, it releases the placenta, and it is cleansed of those superfluities with proper care.
However, a birth that differs from this is unnatural and undesirable, for when the baby emerges feet first and its body exits before its head and feet, or one hand, or the foot is foremost, or the head emerges, and the hand, or belly and the whole body is turned unnaturally to the side.  These are considered bad signs.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and perceptive in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what kind of ingenuity is in them, so that it can be understood and what has been observed.
When the birth occurs headfirst in a natural manner, and the process is difficult for the woman, and the woman's strength is already weakened, then apply this treatment, or what women should have, and vaporize her brow with fennel seeds and moist oils.
And then the midwife should insert her finger between the baby's shoulders and crack the afterbirth, or cut it with her nail until that moisture within is expelled. And press the woman's abdomen until the afterbirth comes down.
But if it does not descend, then the woman needs to be treated with a poultice of ptilium and a poultice of fennel seed with cumin.
And then command her, or the attendant, to be pressed and make her sneeze with a concoction; and cover her mouth and nose for an hour, for the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
And if the two halves of the afterbirth are expelled at the beginning, then you must gently reduce them, little by little. But if you do not succeed in the process, then place the woman on a stool and raise her feet up, and then lower the stool to the ground; and the woman must be held so that she does not fall during the movement.
And if the halves are not returned and the afterbirth remains, then cut them and extract the remaining part of the afterbirth, or bind the body part to the woman's hand with a cloth and extract it gently, for that will be expelled.
When the afterbirth is on its feet, then you must push it up, and then turn the afterbirth gently, and then swallow it, and then take one of its legs and extend it gently.
But when it has emerged into the thighs, then you command him to strain, and make him sneeze with a condition, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then you return the afterbirth gently until you put it back in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with ease.
And if it is not expelled by all of that which we have recounted, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, and pound them well in a mortar, then apply this mixture to the vulva of the woman, at the lower part of her abdomen. And then apply this compress in lukewarm water, until it reaches the hypochondria.
And when the parts below him are soothed, then apply a fumigation of myrtle and then place it over him. And when the fumigation has been in place for an hour, then you place him on a stool, and make him sneeze and hold his mouth shut, and remove the compress, and press the lower part of his abdomen gently, for the afterbirth will be expelled soon.
The expulsion of the afterbirth, hanging lengthwise by one of her hands. Be clever in reducing the hand, but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her go.
And if she cannot go, then make her lie down on the bed, and beat her thighs with forceful blows, and then administer bandages which I and recommend made of mucilage.
And if labor has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then study the reduction of the hand, and pull the afterbirth in a natural manner, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to push until it is expelled.
The afterbirth is on the mother's bottom, with her hands spread out above it, and her face is towards her mother's. The midwife should hold the hand of the afterbirth and gradually turn it, and remove it with ease.
And when it is pulled out, then place on her navel what we have recommended of oil and mucilage.
Then instruct her to move slightly, and when you have done this, have her sit on a stool and tell her to lean on her right side, and then tell her to press; and make her sneeze with some condition. The prepared fetus (or foetus) is placed on its side.
When the mother is delivered of the afterbirth in this manner, consider, and then you cut the placenta with your fingernail until the moisture is drained away.
And if the fetus is hanging by the face and neck, then you cut the umbilical cord so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the fetus into the parts inside until you have placed it where it should be, and tell the woman to press herself a few times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born frequently, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and quickly dies; however, this is for reasons outside of nature, and not to live.
And, count them, whether they are numerous, one by one, and separate them by separating the ligaments at the navel of each, and sometimes they are expelled in the same way as a single fetus, easily, and sometimes in a less praiseworthy way, as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver, and arrive at the point we have discussed about unnatural figures, then you treat them in the same way as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with slowness, and is ingenious according to every possible figure, so as to prepare the way for health.
A recollection of what is formed in the womb from embryos and [what is] aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and even ten are formed in the mother's womb, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted six, and another ten, all fully formed.  The cause of this was determined by measurements of the glorious wise men.
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, for it is very easy for her to recognize this slight resemblance, and therefore she should proceed with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with what we have discussed in the division of medicine, and it has not been expelled, and you are forced to use manual procedures, then you must consider whether the woman is of sound constitution and is not suffering from any of the conditions that lead to fear of her demise.
Then the woman should be positioned on the bed on her side, with her head lowered and her thighs raised.
And the attendant should hold her in two places or bind her to the bed so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
And then, moisten the mother's mouth with soothing oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then, the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingers onto the fetus.
And in suitable places: then consider if the fetus is ready to descend headfirst, and then hook your fingers into its neck, or head, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or in its armpits, or at the sides, or under the ribs.
But if the descent is feet-first, then in the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And the midwife should hold the fingers with her right hand, and rest their curve in the place between the fingers of her left hand, and gently insert her hand and hook her fingers into one of those places we mentioned, until reaching the empty space.
And then place and insert another finger, or a third if necessary, in opposition to the first, so that there is a drawing-in, and not a pulling-out only according to its own resistance.
And this should be done gently on all sides, so that the delivery is eased as is done when a tooth is pulled out.
And it is necessary in the area between them that the stretching should be softened; and if any part of it is held back, then the midwife should anoint some of her fingers with oil and insert these carefully and roll with those fingers that which is held back.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the fingers should be moved to the location higher up; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
However, if one hand emerges before the other, and it is not possible to return it by compressing it, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until the whole is expelled, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary to do the same for the other hand and the two feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or you encounter resistance in the expulsion, or water is accumulated on its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the spinal bones and to break the head open and drain the water, or to break it with an instrument called "alundat," the form of which will be found with the other instruments in the chapter near this one.
And similarly, do this if the fetus has a large head by nature.  Then it is necessary to cut it with a scalpel, or to extract it with an alundat as we have said; and then remove the bones with forceps.
And if the head is emerged and is constricted towards the pubic area, then it should be split open until the moisture which is in the feet is expelled.
But if it is not complicated, nor resisting the expulsion, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the belly is swollen with pus or is hydrops, then you must pierce it until the moisture inside it drains out.
But if the fetus is positioned on its feet, then the pulling of it is gentle and its direction toward the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is constricted towards the belly and the feet, then you must extract it with a cloth that you will have wrapped around your hand, and you must cut the belly and the feet until whatever is in those areas is discharged.
However, if the other limbs have been removed and the head remains, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she should insert her hand into the vagina, and seek the head with it, and pull it with the fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then insert a finger or two into it and pull it with those.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted by a hot abscess that has developed inside, then it should not be poked at; instead, it is necessary to administer an application of fatty, moist substances beforehand. And administer poultices; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisturize.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned; and what we have said about a living fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and removed.
And it is necessary that you leave no cause within the afterbirth.
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment of abscesses that occur in the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then you should place it in astringent water and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose womb a stillbirth had been made, and then again pregnant, and a stillbirth followed. An abscess occurred on his navel a long time ago; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and treated him for a long time, and the wound was not healed.
Then I applied some strong-drawing plasters to him, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones in the belly.
And then I thought they were bones from the stillborn child.
So I cleaned the wound, and removed many bones from him, the woman remaining in her usual condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, recovering a little from the pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather that this is knowledge and helpful in what the skilled doctor administers with their hands for healing.
Of the forms of instruments necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of vertigo with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of constriction in which the books are bound, with two twists on the two ends of two cords. However, this twist should be more subtle than a constriction, and be made either of linen or of wood.
And the width of each cord is about two fingers, and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle of the two cords there are two attachments of the same cord, already fixed, whose length is half a palm plus a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two attachments are inserted into the mother's mouth so that it is opened with them when you rotate the two prescribed twists.
Another form, similar to the more subtle and lighter one:
Be made of linen or wood, in the manner of forceps. However, at its extremity, there are two attachments, just as before, and the length of these attachments is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the mother using these, then you make the woman lie on her back, her feet hanging down, opening the space between her thighs. Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the patient to hold their breath, and then make them sneeze forcefully, and press your hand over their mouth and nose, and if by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then take a bowl, pierce a hole in its cover, and place in it herbs such as mugwort, rue, anise, chamomile, arabic cicely, cassia wood, and yarrow.  Place it over the mother's open mouth, as a compress. All these herbs, or some of them, should be immersed in water in the bowl, and place that bowl over the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's cavity.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.

Once found, gently pull it out slowly.  However, if it is adhered to the mother's womb, insert her hand as previously described.  When the afterbirth has been located, gently remove it bit by bit, as instructed, so that the mother is not subjected to excessive traction. Previously, it is necessary that it be moved easily from side to side.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment using sneezing and medicating with herbal infusions.
However, if it has not been expelled by all the methods we have described, be careful not to tire yourself out by trying to remove it further.  Instead, whatever has come out of it into the mother's mouth, place it on the woman's thigh. Then, apply ointment and a four-ingredient medicine to it, as this will cause it to decompose over a few days, dissolve, and be expelled.
But when it decomposes, a foul odor rises from the head and stomach, making the patient ill.  Then, fumigations and appropriate remedies must be administered.
However, some of the ancients already tested fumigation with burnt mint and dried figs.
Method of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for retained menses and afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or earthenware.
And place the narrow end in the container and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation is placed over the flames of the fire.
And let it remain until the fumigation is consumed, and then renew another.
Concerning the treatment of an unperforated child's anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which are very narrow and closed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrowness with her finger; and if not, to perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the intestines.
And then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil; and treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is blocked, then place a tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to pass stool.
And blockage of the anus sometimes occurs due to the consolidation of a plague or an abscess; then it is necessary to incise that consolidation, and then treat it with what we have mentioned about the treatment and the lead.
Of the treatment of the fistulas that then develop below.
The fistulas that develop in the lower part, swollen and thick, which appear near the anus or in the space between the parts.
And it is a fistula or many.
And when that swelling is old and comes from moist, whitish moisture or subtle putrefaction, and sometimes these fistulas are even penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because of what comes out of them through discharge and windiness; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and often worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of these penetrating ones reach the hip joint and even the marrow of the thigh bone.
And the way to know if a fistula is penetrating into the anus and not penetrating is that you put your index finger into the anus, and gently insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula, if there is no twisting.
But if there is twisting in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or the hair on your finger.
And if you feel nothing at all, and no discharge, worms, or windiness proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the urethra, then the sign of it is the discharge of urine from that place and prohibition of it, so that the area may be healed with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the thigh or into the bone, and the surrounding area is painful, then the sign is that the probe passes directly into the bone, if there is no twisting within the bone, and pain in the surrounding area; and that it may remain so for a long time; and that medicine is of no value there, nor will it be healed with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of those is the work and futility of foolish physicians, upon which you should be wary.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected, as I will tell you, and what I have already tested.
And it is that the patient lies on their back with their hands under their sides, and raise their legs up, with their thighs inclined towards their belly, and then insert a lead probe, or an iron one if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know the location where the probe passes through.
And if that touches the patient around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe itself penetrating through your finger, or something else besides the flesh, then know for certain that it is penetrating; and then do not work on it, for there is no cure for it, as we have said.
But nevertheless, some might say that it is healed in some men slowly.
And the treatment by which healing is expected in this case is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as was previously described, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the flesh around the fistula that resembles a bird's feather quill has been cauterized.
And then treat it with something wrapped in a bandage until all that burned flesh is healed, and then treat it with flesh-generating and strengthening remedies, and if it is healed, good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate right through your finger into the anus, and between it and the probe is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula is in the area where the surface of the anus is, then cut the anus at the beginning of the fistula, and you shall cut it with the probe, and place it within the fistula until the incision reaches the point where the probe's end reaches, and through the probe, it is released and falls out.
And then cleanse that fistula-ridden flesh, which is like a bird's feather quill, and do not leave any cause behind.
But if a flow of blood often occurs during your procedure when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire provides two good habits: that is, the burning of those tissues, and the cutting off of the blood and excess moisture.
And then treat it with dressings wrapped in bandages or lint until the wound heals, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and strengthen tough flesh.
And those medicines contain astringency and excision; when it is thus restored, it is already healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
But if the fistula has already reached the anus and is distant from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And if you find the opening and between you and it there is a veil of cicatrix or flesh, and it is close to the penetration, then there is no surgery there but rather a way of trust and hope.
This condition can be treated in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or excise it until you reach its cavity, so that you can insert remedies and treatment is readily available; and do not excessively excise, lest you injure the underlying tissue, and the patient suffer from involuntary pain; and then treat it with what we have said, for a strong remedy will quickly heal; or, extend that veil, and cleanse the pus, removing it towards the anus as we have said, either with a probe or with another instrument with a sharp end, and subsequently treat those fistulous tissues resulting from the incision, in a way which allows you to use a crust; then, endeavor to restore the entire wound by excision, and if the opening above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the anal region, it will be more easily tolerated by the patient. And sometimes the fistula ends in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is situated in the folds of the anus around the body's surface and on the edge of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like a needle for preparing the skin in this manner:
and insert it into that fold, wrapped in five or around those folds; and then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates the edge of the anus near the interior, then you remove the thread from that hole as you would insert your finger into the anus; and you pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, tightening each one, and leave it for a day or two.  And whenever you cut the thread into the flesh, firmly tighten it until the tissues that lie between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever manner you have prepared, but not too much on the perineum, for you should not do this so as not to cut the sphincter; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the spatula with which the fistula is opened: one curve is very sharp, and the other part is blunt, so that it does not cut what is unnecessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are between the buttocks like bunches of grapes, or they are like bunches of raisins. And they are small or large, and whether blood flows from them constantly or is single, or multiple, and are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the times of little moisture, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with a continuous substance, and according to the color of the substance.
And the treatment of those inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and press until the anus is stretched and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp and lift them with forceps or nails, and then cut them off at their roots.
But if the forceps are not able to hold them due to their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or cotton or wool floss, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them and sprinkle some hot powders onto the cuts, to be instead of cautery; or cauterize them according to what is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not yield to evacuation, then prick the patient with a cautery in which there is a small bite, and wash it with what is in the anus, so that it yields to evacuation easily when the patient is straining.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause is slight, and you should grasp them with your finger, or forceps, cut them, and then treat them as we have said until they are healed.
And whoever has made incisions, must stitch them in this manner: take a thin thread and insert it into a needle, and then pull the hemorrhoids apart on both sides, and make the needle pierce into the other side, and tie both ends of the thread to the lower section of the needle, and do it horizontally; and tightly constrict the hemorrhoid with the stitch, and then knot the thread and pull out the needle.
Do this to all of them, and leave one, do not stitch it, so that the excess blood flows from that one.
Then place a cloth soaked in warmed oil or ointment on the anus; put it on the area and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it is set; and when it has set, treat them with ointments or other remedies until healed.
And if they are old and no longer responding to that medicine, then you must scrape them with the tip of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the scab of pus on top is removed, which prevents them from healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then you scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first until much blood and they are scraped; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a certain ailment, this is important, and it is not anything other than a reddish spot according to the color of the body, like a nail head, and it is found throughout the body, but especially in the parts below the feet and fingers, and is frequently followed by pain due to its presence.
And then it is necessary to cut what is in the surrounding area of the ailment, and hold it with shears or tweezers, and uproot it from its root; and then treat that area.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is prescribed in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round elevation on the surface of the body, and it often appears on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to scrape and cut it, or to squeeze it with a file or a needle until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
However, a pimple is a small, ulcerated or fistulous, large elevation on the body's surface, often found on the face.  When the cold touches it, a sensation similar to an ant bite is felt, and it is often distributed around the body.
And its treatment is to take a hollow quill from a bird of prey (like a hawk or a vulture), a strong one, and place it over the pimple, until the affected area is contained within the quill's circumference;  then rotate your hand with the quill until you have cut through the material surrounding the pimple, and place it on the skin, and remove the pimple from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a quill of iron or steel, according to this method:
The part of the quill above the opening should be thin, closed (meaning not perforated), wrapped so that the grip on it is easy and the rotation is smooth.
And if you wish to cauterize the pimple, do so as described earlier in the section on cauterizing ulcers in the chapter on cauterization.
Concerning the instruments that are used for giving treatment.
Sometimes treatment is done with silver or brass or molten or hammered iron; and sometimes it is done with those instruments, small or large, depending on who is receiving treatment, for the one used in treating small children should be small, and those for whose bottoms are sore or painful, the treatment should be applied with small tools.
The form of large and small treatment (or instruments):
The head of the cannula, on top of which the pouch is tied, should be wide in this way; and should have a distinct area where the pouch is tied, just like before.
And the lower end, which is inserted into the rectum, should be completely closed (i.e., not open) very finely tapered; and on one side there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the holes should be according to the thickness of the rays and slightly bigger ones are smaller.
And the pouch, in which the bladder medicines or sheepskin is placed, should be a palm's width in length.
And it should be perforated with many holes, in the same way it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, thick bile, perhaps ten parts, or around that; and attach the pouch to that.  And when the pouch is in place, tighten the head of the container tightly between two dividers with strong constriction, and then fasten each hole with that.  However, the manner of fastening with it is that you fill the pouch with the medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then have the patient lie on their back, raise their legs, and place a cloth or a sheet under them; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or something similar; and then insert that into the rectum with ease.  Then the attendant takes the pouch and squeezes it with both hands forcefully until all that is in the container is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the container, and the patient lowers their legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces the movement this way, keep it in place as long as possible; and if they sleep with it for an entire night, it is more effective in providing relief.
However, if you use this method for ailments of loose bowels and expulsion from the body, then you should repeatedly use the cautery until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the rectum, then the container should be very small and thin, like the containers used for children's treatments.
Concerning the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I thus state the treatment of those ailments with hands and with medicines according to their completion.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on how the plagues are caused and depending on the location on which the plagues manifest.
And the causes for which plagues are often caused are like the throwing of stones, or the cutting with a sword or knife, or a blow with a spear, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those from many causes.
However, the plagues that occur in various parts of the body are like a plague that strikes the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the liver, and similar to those affecting other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues so that you might apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recollection of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a thrown stone or similar, and the skull is merely cracked and is a serious wound, and you fear the development of a festering boil, then you should quickly lance and bleed the cephalic vein immediately.  Do not delay; and remove from him the blood according to his strength, if there is no other reason forbidding bloodletting, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is little.
And the bloodletting should be on the opposite side of the wounded area; and keep the patient from overeating and consuming heavy foods and drinks.
But if some reason prohibits the bloodletting, then the diet should be lessened, and he should avoid meat and wine.
And if a festering boil develops on the wound, apply a compress soaked in heated, perfumed oil or wine with astringents.
But if a festering boil is certain, then apply to the wound, if it's recent, its own blood, and if the air hasn't altered the powder which I'll describe shortly.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointments until it heals.
But if the wound is large, or caused by a cut or similar injury, and the edges aren't brought together with stitches, then bring them together with stitches, as I will describe concerning their suture.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and sinew, and there's no retention of the bone but a small suspension, then cut that away and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh to its proper place, and command the patient to eat foods rich in strength: heads of various kinds of vegetables and similar items until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it is not adhering to the bone, then you cut away all that which is rotten and discard it, and then treat it further.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow, and it is not severed by medicine, then you search for the artery, and if you find it unsevered, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it off; and if necessity compels you that what we have said is not appropriate, then you cauterize it until the blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is slight;  so that you may powder those wounds with this powder, which will solidify them before the air alters them.
And the method of the powder is this: take one part of oil of rosemary, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime;  have them all finely ground and sifted, and apply the powder to the wound, and bind it tightly until it adheres well, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it with bandages and leave it, and do not remove them until it is no longer inflamed, for the medicine is not eradicated from there until it is healed.
And sometimes it does this alone, when you do not have rosemary and dragon's blood readily available.
And you do this same procedure on large wounds when it is necessary to apply it and bandage them, and on fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air hasn't yet altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially in summer, then this powder application is not appropriate. Instead, treat them with what we have already mentioned, or apply digestive ointments, or dust them with flour of barley mixed with water and honey until a scab forms; then continue with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a wound happens to have a fractured bone, and it is small, then you extract it with a gesture.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds to the neck.
But if the wound is on the neck, there is no difference in treatment between a wound to the head when it is simple.
But if the neck wound involves an incised nerve or artery, if it is a nerve, there is no good treatment for it other than avoiding any treatment that causes severe astringency, like using zinc and vitriol, and things similar, for they harm the nerves and contract them. And do not apply any cold substance to the wound, for the substance of the nerves is cold, and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if this is treated with mild medications, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or unperfumed oil, and  using poultices when washed with fresh water and other softening stones according to this method, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or close its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep, or if the injury is in the lower part of the flesh, then pierce it in the most depressed location.
But if an artery is already cut in the wound, and blood is flowing, then separate or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the entire neck have been cut, and the veins of the neck are preserved, then you should close the edges of the wound with sutures on the neck's bone and do not touch it; first align it and return it to its natural form, then strengthen it with appropriate binding, and leave it for some days; then treat it with ointments that are astringent and stimulating until it is healed.
But if the wound has affected the bone, then you should clean it, and if there are bone fragments, remove as many as possible; and what you are unable to remove, leave it until the wound begins to suppurate, and is separated, and the discharge is eased.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle powder on it, the powder that has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what the recollection will provide in its relevant chapter.
If it is a blow from a spear or a knife, and you assess its depth, then consider if there is windiness [or gas] when the patient breathes; if so, know that the wound is fatal.
And if there is no depth to it, and the wound is fresh with its own blood, then you put powder on it in the first hour, and do not press it so that the blood is not trapped in its depths and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but instead, apply a drawing ointment, and apply it again twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then put old wool in the opening of the wound so that it absorbs any moisture; and have the patient lie on the wound so that whatever is concentrated there may drain out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound, and you observe no spasms, tremors of the heart, or constriction of the windpipe, and other good conditions are present, then know that the wound is salvageable; and then treat it with cauterization and other remedies until it is healed.
But if healing is not possible, and the wound is permanently open, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore, treat it according to the appropriate chapter [of medical procedures].
And if the wound is from a cut with a scalpel or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with sutures if it is large, or with powders if it is small.
But if it has already penetrated the bone, and you have already cut out pieces of it, then you should probe the wound and try to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound can heal, because their removal is made easier.
And for other wounds occurring on other limbs, the method of treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of wounds of the belly.
The ailment which afflicts the belly is either severe, or slight, or moderate.
A small rupture is difficult to mend by another method, and if the reduction of the protruded parts is not done immediately, the process becomes more difficult, because the opening enlarges and the return of the parts becomes difficult.
Therefore, a moderate rupture is best, for in it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, unlike the two types previously mentioned.
And know that the suturing of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common and appropriate for the suturing of other parts of the body.
And two other sutures are specific to other parts of the body, yet they are particularly suitable for suturing the abdomen.
And I will explain each to you with explanation and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to return it, then this is in one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture, as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you should warm it in such a way that you immerse a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water alone, or that you gather water with mint, cyperus, and spikenard, and apply that to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, it works better with some astringent added, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And therefore, when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallows and althea have been boiled, for by this the inside of it is relieved with little difficulty.
But if the inside is not adequately relieved by this treatment, then you should carefully cut the rupture slightly with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is suitable, rather than incision, and there should be no attempt to dry it out when there is need and deficiency.
Of the instrument, take note of its shape:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the tip is thin, and it should not be as thin as a spatula; rather, it should be slightly blunt.
And therefore, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its return must be according to its natural shape, into its proper place, if possible, for this is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the belly, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the belly, then you place their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then constantly focus your attention and consideration on this, so that the part where the wound is higher than the other.
And thus you must administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant hold the rupture entirely with their hand and bring the edges together, and then determine whether there is a suture or some other remedy needed.
And this is the common method of suturing: take a needle, or several needles depending on the size of the wound; and then insert it at the edge of the rupture near the ring finger, and carefully push the needle through, and, without putting pressure on the two ends of the thread, together with the two ends of the stitching thread beneath the thread of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate from that side; and you have already brought together two ends of the thread and two ends of the stitching thread, and this makes four sutures.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle, sometimes from two sides at once, and then bring the edges and the wound together in a proper manner; and then leave the thickness of the finger and insert another needle into it, and then tie it with thread as you did with the first needle; and do not cease to do this with needles if necessary until you have completely closed the wound.
And the needles should be medium in size, neither too thin nor too thick, because thin needles easily pierce the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the thread, and therefore they should be medium in thickness and fineness.
And the needle should not be inserted into both ends of the thread too close together, so that the flesh is not easily pierced and the wound breaks open before it has healed.
And the sutures should not be too long, so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they don't bother the patient while they are sleeping; and place powdered substances spread around, covering the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound has already healed.
And this type of suturing with needles is most suitable for small wounds, for in their suture, one or two, or around that number of needles suffice.
However, the second suture is more common:  and it is that you suture the four ends (of the thread) and two ends of the bandage, all at once, with a needle that is finely wrapped around it in terms of both fineness and thickness.
And then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, pull the needle back from the same place you started, so that the thread is retracted from the most superior part of the wound,  so that the suture is like a cloth stitch, with the cloths being tightly bound.
And place the thickness of a little finger between each stitch.
And this suture is known to men (or, more literally and perhaps more accurately: This suture is known by men).
And with this suture, I closed the wound that had occurred on a man's belly, which had been wounded with a knife.  The wound was a disruption greater than a palm, and about two palms of the intestine had protruded from the middle area, and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I returned that which had been outside the wound (the intestine) after it had been outside for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was consolidated within fifteen days or thereabouts, and I healed him, and he is healthy; and after that he lived for many years, and he remained in his proper disposition (or: recovered his normal state).
And the other physicians had judged that he would not recover; and by a miracle it was, for I did not heal him with an ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or medicine was found, but I placed old wool on the wound twice a day and diligently washed it with honey water until he was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of sutures, appropriate according to the narration of Paraula.
And it is that you begin with a suture of the cord (or thread), and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And thus, as the needle penetrates the cord (or thread) and in that same laceration, according to its shape and length throughout the body, and leaves the end of the thread where you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate from the inside to the outside at the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again in that same wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this wound, and insert the needle at the other end from the inside to the outside.
And make that penetration with your penetration, which you make into the thread at the wound's end, which is on its side, until all of that is penetrated.
And then, starting from this same side, sew it with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the thread which you penetrated, and then return it to that thread and sew the end of the thread that is on the other side with this end of the wound; and remove that from the thread that is on that side.
And do this once and again, and do this once more and again until you sew everything on the outside according to that example.
And the sum of this way of sewing is that you sew with a skin stitch, with which you sew skin, in such a way that you sew the thread once from the end of the thread, and you leave it another time until you have completed it.
However, the second type of appropriate suture, which you rightly call "Gualia" – and this is according to his and for him recounted advice – is that you stitch it according to a similar method by which some healers stitch it, in such a way that they join each part to its corresponding part appropriate to its nature; and they apply the end of one to the end of the wound, and this is done as I have instructed you.
You must put the needle into the end of the wound close to the external part, and make it penetrate to the inner part in that single place, and leave both ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in both ends of the thread.
And then turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the inside to the outside in two other ends of the wound, on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the ordinary superficial suture, and is the suture of which a previous recollection has been made.
This is the teaching of Gualia.
And moreover, some might say that the suture of the abdomen is rectified with two needles, meaning that you insert a thread into each of them, and begin with the insertion of the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who know how to sew skins equally.
And know that when a rupture is in the middle part of the belly, then the suture there is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment of plague is a judgment like that of other plagues.
Which means that when you get the recent plague with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestines and sew it up and treat it properly, then you should place the flesh-promoting powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then place on it some poultices that are available to you until they form a hard crust, and the pus falls out, and are healed firmly and watched over; then treat it similarly to the treatment of other plagues until it is healed.
But if the medicines are not available to you, then apply it immediately, after it starts to form pus, a thick cloth, and change that twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you will not need other medicine in many situations when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague might spread to the main limbs, then you should soak lion's wool in tempered olive oil, or in rubbed olive oil, and place it around the areas between the armpits and under the armpits.
And if you feel pain or putrefaction in his bowels, for this often happens, then treat that with strong, black wine, and especially if the pus has reached the intestines and has made a penetrating exit into their cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is easier to heal than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal, for the intestine called "jejunum" does not receive healing from the plague that strikes it at all; and this is due to the large multitude of veins within it and the magnitude of those within, and the subtlety of its structure, and because of its proximity to the nature of the nerves.
But if what is proceeding from the plague is the lining of the intestines, and you find it recently affected, reduce it according to your method for reducing the intestines as well.
But if it has spread to the surrounding areas and is already green or black, then you should apply a strong pressure with a cloth over the discolored area, so that blood flows, because the tissue contains veins and arteries.
And then, cut what is beyond that ligament, and suspend the two ends of the cloth from below the affected area, so that you relieve the pull from the affected area away from the tissue damage and the spreading of the plague.
A reminder of the plagues when they occur in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine, and it is small, it is possible that it might be restored in some cases.  Therefore, I witnessed a man who had been afflicted in his belly by a lance thrust, and the plague was on the right side of the stomach, and the plague had become an established fistula, and it expelled gas and wind.
Then I considered the treatment of him, although I wasn't confident in his recovery, but I still tried to carefully tend to the wound until it healed and the area was consolidated.
And after I saw the area had healed, I considered the possibility of further illness affecting his belly; however, no further illness occurred and he remained in his normal state, eating and drinking, and afterward, he went bathing and resumed his activities.
And some experienced people have said that when a small rupture occurs in the intestine, it should be bound in this way: you should take large ants; then you should bring together the two edges of the wound and place one of those ants with its mouth open over the two edges of the wound; so, when it bites them and closes its mouth, you should sever the ant's head, as it has adhered and will not be dislodged.
And then place another ant next to the first, and continue doing this with ants, one next to another, according to the size of the entire wound.
Then you reduce and bind the wound; and those ant heads remain attached to the intestine until it is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.
And it is also possible to bind the intestine with a fine thread made from the intestine of an animal, once it has been put on a needle; you should take the end of this thread, which should be very thin, and tie the end of the thread to the thread, wrapping it.  Then, push that thread through needles and bind the intestine with that thread, returning it to its place within the tear/rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only by way of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is on one of the delicate intestines, because then it is only a refinement, and not a complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it ages and becomes chronic, and is an ulcer that does not heal and continues to discharge pus constantly and is not excised, then, in any limb, is universally called a fistula, and we call it an ulcer in this case.
And a fistula, according to truth, is a hard, white, fibrous growth, without pain, and having a cavity similar to the hollow of a bird's beak; and for this reason it is called a "knotty ulcer", that is, a "beak".
And in some people it is moist, which discharges pus; and often the moisture is easily excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes thin, and sometimes large, and sometimes subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their treatment with medicine in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you understand the treatment of a fistula, which is done through surgery with a hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for there are fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spinal cord, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to some of the vertebrae of the spine, or to any of the compound joints such as those of the hand and foot; and those that are close to the main limb and similar parts.
Therefore, those fistulas that occur in this manner are among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, and they do not receive a cure, and attempting treatment is labor and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas has a depth that does not reach the places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe or a iron rod, and if the fistula is going in a straight direction, you will find it with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, due to its lightness, is easily bent towards the winding path.
And if the fistula is very deep, it is not possible for you to determine its location with a probe.
Then irrigate the openings through their openings, for the moisture you irrigate will flow towards the other openings and run from them.
And then inquire thoroughly, in whatever way is possible for you to understand if there are bones or nerves present, and if the fistula's cavity is deep or close, or if it is a fistula with many openings and that all is present there with the full extent of your strength, through your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your pressing with your hand on the affected area, and noting the signs and the accidental causes of an abscess.
And when you have considered all these factors with true understanding, then proceed to the treatment according to your judgment:  and consider whether the fistula is an apparent near-surface one, or is in a saline area, far from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the locations that I have told you about.
Then you dry the fistula as previously described, removing the filtering material and the corrupted flesh, and whatever has grown within the opening of the flesh, or tissue, or similar things, and heal it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of deep origin, and as is appropriate, then it is necessary to cut it open as much as possible, and then clean it thoroughly of all its corrupt flesh; and then apply some bandages containing sharp medicines, and introduce them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have opened with an instrument.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine has corroded everything that remains within the fistula cavity from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have described.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you have confirmed this, then you should examine and dry it as we have described, if you do not find any vein, nerve, or major limb impediment as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then heal it with something that produces flesh until it is cured.
And if it is not healed and still discharges pus, then know that you have not completely eradicated the corruption; and then, you should expose it again thoroughly, perform a further scraping and cleaning of it using your strongest tools, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, well and good; but if not, then know that that corruption is in a deep, remote part of the body, which has not been reached, and then there is no remedy for you but to leave it be.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to pull it out, then you should pull it out with delicate forceps suitable for this purpose.
And if there are many bones, then thoroughly remove all of them, and leave no part of them behind using your strength.
But if you encounter any difficulty due to the humors, and in their incision there is no fear or error, then cut through those ligaments or flesh if they are present, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then apply something to the wound that might close it, and leave it until it is surrounded by those bones, flesh, and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, and make sure the incision you made in your operation is constricted.
Furthermore, if you fear this, apply to it cotton soaked in crushed oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian unguents, or in other green ointments, for when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the removal, and the pulling of them will be eased.
However, if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone, or similar to these, and only the surface of it is corrupted, then scrape that with a scraper only from behind until that blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted portion is large, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut that entirely, severing it down to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat that until it is healed.
And I will tell you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, about 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh.  The internal parts of the body were affected by the matter spreading throughout, so that the matter had reached the thigh, and it was a large abscess, an internal abscess, and it was not an external cause.
And then the time was prolonged by the error of the physicians, until the abscess opened, and a lot of matter flowed out, and his healing was delayed until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were created in it, which all discharged pus and bodily fluids.
Then, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were skilled in the art, until I was called in.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings in large quantities; and the man's body was already emaciated, and his complexion was yellow.
Then, I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.
And then I examined all the openings and found them extending throughout the leg from all directions.
Then I probed and incised one of those openings until I exposed part of the bone, and found it corrupted, already rotten and blackened; and a part of the leg had already become corrupted, having penetrated to the marrow.
Then I cauterized what I had discovered of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it was not in the bone itself, but that corruption that I had excised and cauterized, and I had already cauterized it.
And then I applied restorative medicine to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I probed again and discovered, on the second layer of the previous discovery, further corruption along with the bone.
And I cauterized what appeared to me to be that corruption, and then I was forced to perform the healing and restoration of it, but it has not been restored.
And then I discovered it again, and I did not cease to cut the small bone little by little, seeking its restoration, and it was not restored until I cut about a palm's width of it, and I removed it with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicine, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And no further probing or cutting is needed in its operation, except for the weakness of the area, its little tolerance, and its fear of death, for it had seizures throughout the hours due to a surplus of a bad fainting-type evacuation.
Therefore, it is healed completely, and nothing is in the place of bone but hard flesh; and its condition in its body is good, and its strength has returned; and it has been restored to its functions, and I did not find any impediment in its walking or harm in any way.
However, if a prominent bone is located in a previously injured part of the body, then you should cut it in this way:  You should take a ligament and tighten it at the extremity of that prominent bone, and order someone to pull on it; then place a thicker wool ligament over the first one; and then tie that over the flesh beneath the bone, pulling the extremity so that you draw the flesh back, revealing the area you want to cut so that the cut doesn't harm the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the saw is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the sawing should be done on the corrupted area little by little, for if the corruption is in the cavity of the bone and does not appear in its visible parts, then the sawing must be repeated.
And if the bone is corrupted but not prominent, with one part continuing to the other, and the corruption is in the middle or a part of it, then you must uncover all the surrounding flesh; and then place wood underneath, and saw from one side to the other where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous on the other side.
And the sawing should be done along the area of the corruption a little at a time, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not a rational problem, but rather a deceitful one.
And if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and that you should expose and cleanse it according to any possible disposition, and by any means possible, so long as it does not affect any vein or nerve.
And know that the tools for cutting and sawing these bones vary according to the bones' location, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, size, and smallness, and their hardness and rarity.  Therefore, you must prepare suitable instruments for each type of operation.
And know that this operation indicates to you the type of instrument you need; when great study, long experience, and knowledge of the methods of this art, and observation of illnesses, are yours, then you will know what instruments are appropriate for each illness yourself.
