Of the cutting edges and the sawing of bones.
The extremities can be affected either by intrinsic or extrinsic causes, or if your treatment with those medicines fails to cure the corruption, or if the corruption extends to a limb unaffected by any apparent cause, then you must cut into that limb until the corruption is exposed, so that the patient escapes death or more serious harm to the limb.
And the sign of the affected area is that the limb becomes black, as if fire has already burned it, or the blackness will spread until the hardness that has penetrated the limb and spread throughout the body; and therefore cut into it with a cutting instrument.
And similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the sting of some poisonous creature, such as a scorpion, a viper, or a toad, and the like.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger and do not allow the corruption to spread into the hand.
And similarly, if it affects the hand, then you cut it from the base of the forearm, and do not allow the corruption to spread into the arm's forearm.
And if instead, it affects that area, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond the elbow, then in that case, death is the patient's fate; and administering any other remedy for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, for the foot, when corruption affects the toe, then you cut it down to one of the joints.
And if it takes hold of the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no cure, only resignation, and leaving the patient to death.
And the method of cutting or severing the limb is that you tighten the ligament in the area just below the part you want to cut, and tighten another ligament above the area; and the minister extends a ligament to the parts below, and the minister extends another ligament above; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a sharp instrument until all the flesh is exposed; and then it is cut or severed.
And it is necessary that a cloth be placed on all sides so that the area does not touch anything, and that the patient receives a carefully applied wound dressing and a cautious abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while working, then you cauterize the area thoroughly, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with suitable bandages, and treat it until it is healed.
And I relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, this same accident which I and you have recounted, which is that blackening occurred on his foot due to a burn similar to a fire's combustion.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it spread to his entire foot.
And then that man prepared himself, knowing that the treatment would affect the limb with the intensity of the pain and burn he experienced, and he incised that foot down to the joint, and the burn healed.
For a long time, that same corruption occurred in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I was compelled to address that excess with what I placed upon his hand, as part of cleansing his body; and the excess was not dispersed, and it began to affect the other fingers until it spread throughout the hand.
And he urged me to cut open his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the dispersal of that excess, and fearing that the hand would die because of the incision, for the patient's strength was failing.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and later I heard that that man prepared himself and cut his entire hand open, and it healed.
And I have related this story only so that it might help in understanding this illness, and to signify how it should be understood and treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicinal substances.
When an abscess occurs on any fleshy part of the body, and the space of the abscess enlarges until it collects pus, and then it bursts and is pierced, and the pus is released, and the area remains empty like a vessel, and the skin over it is thin like a fine cloth, and it has not progressed to a deeper corruption, and it does not affect the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the space continues to enlarge until the corruption penetrates some body part, then it is called a fistula, and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to cut away that entire skin, just as it has receded, especially when it is thin and like a cloth, and to ensure that it does not adhere to the area because of its corruption.
But if you suspect the skin will adhere and that the corruption hasn't yet reached deeper tissues, and the skin has become inflamed on the surface, then treat it with an enema.
And if you are considering a sinus, and if it is large and the discharge has a foul odor, then inject that with an Egyptian medicine, which is of oil, sour wine, and vinegar in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and simmered over fire until the medicine turns to a reddish color and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then take what is necessary of this, dissolve it in water and honey; and inject it into the sinus with that, and compress the opening, leaving the medicine there for two hours.
Then, remove it with gentle pressure; and do this for as many days as it takes until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.
And sometimes it's injected with water from ashes, when this medicine isn't available to you; and you should take the ashes of wood, and place them in water; and then strain that and inject the sinus with it until you are sure the sinus is now healed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then inject it with water and honey, and vinegar finely ground, and inject it with wine and honey mixed, because honey's property is to cleanse and wash, and wine helps adhere the sinus, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure the sinus is healed, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or you should inject it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, so the enema tube cannot be inserted, then you should widen it little by little with a small iron instrument, and apply a poultice wrapped in Egyptian or basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the lips together with sutures and leave it of such a size that the enema tube enters without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the branch [of the sinus] extends is elevated to the upper parts, then you should lower it to the lowest point possible so that the branch runs downwards, because when the branch is constricted at the depth of the sinus, flesh will grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to lower the sinus to the vicinity of the lowest bone as desired, then you must prepare the member with a preparation that allows the branch to run easily according to your equipment, and that no cause retains the branch within it.
But the one over the sinus's opening, which remains in place at all times to prevent anything that is joined to the branch from coming out. And so that you may know if what flows from the branch is much or little, or is digested or not digested.
And by this, you consider the location of that sinus; whether the patient feels pain there, and if it is an abscess.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged over the sinus opening has already adhered, or is about to adhere, then on the third day, you remove the adhesion.  When you remove the scab, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle, spreading virus emerges, then do not despair of the adhesion, for its healing will happen eventually.  For there are some people in whom the generation of flesh takes a long time in their wounds, and for others, the opposite is true.
And if from the sinus, undigested matter has been discharged for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the sinus's adhesion is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you must adjust the drying nature of the medicines.  If the medicines are humid in their substance and virtue, they should be dried, like a diaphanous ointment, when the excess of the added substance has been adjusted.
The final step in the treatment is to take diaphanous ointment, dissolve it in rosemary oil, and add to it aged wine, until its substance is infused. Then combine these ingredients and use the resulting mixture.
And you can do something similar when there is no pus—using diaphanous ointment, honey when it is thick enough, and apply it; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind everything into a powder and mix it with honey that has been heated until it is thick.  Then apply this mixture to a cloth and compress it over the sinus; or apply the thickened honey, powdered and mixed with a cloth, and compress it on the sinus.
Sometimes, aristolochia root and the roots of white lilies (and they are white lilies), and the flour of eryngo and centaury are administered with it. Grind these herbs and strain them, powdering them over the previously described honey; then use the mixture.
And if the sinus persists, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone, or in other hard parts or ligaments; then treat it with a fistulous treatment, as described above.
Concerning the treatment of whitlows, damaged nails, the incision of a swollen finger, and its subsequent sectioning.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of a finger of the hand or foot, though it frequently arises on other fingers.  When it persists and its treatment is less successful, or it becomes an abscess from a hot abscess, or is corrupted, it produces pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and often corrupts the entire nail.  The corruption often spreads to the bone, producing a foul odor, and causing the fingertip to become blackened, its color turning dark.
And therefore, when you have done as we have said in the division and your treatment is not effective, then you should cut away all the excess remains of the nail with an iron, and then cauterize the wound, for a cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail bed is already pressing against the flesh underneath, causing it to become inflamed and sore, then you should place a thin piece of a sharp instrument underneath the corner of the nail that is pressing on the flesh, and pull that flesh upwards, and cut that flesh with ease.  Then apply the remaining flesh to the corrosives and burning medicines until it is all removed; then treat that with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into the bone and remove it, for a corrupted bone will never heal.
And if the corrosion and corruption spread to the joint, then you should cut it open at one of the joints, according to its expected recovery, and then treat it until it is healed.
However, if the nail has suffered impact or abrasion, and there is intense pain, then you should initially bleed the patient, and then carefully trim the nail with a sharp instrument in a crosswise cut from top to bottom.  Be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and it will lead to the growth of excessive flesh in the area; then treat the area with whatever alleviates the pain.
But a swollen joint, which is frequently found in the hands of some men, is easily fleshy, and easily located on the bones, or easily located on the nails.
And some of them arise at the base of the joint, or their origin is in certain joints of the fingers. However, that which arises in certain joints of the finger is not severe, and that which arises in the joints is easily severe.
And of those that are fleshy growths, the incision is gentle, and you should cut it away from its base with a broad blade.
But for that which arises at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, be cautious of its removal.
But for that which is grown on the finger near the joints, you must first cut the flesh with a circular incision until you reach the bone, then cut it off with one of the appropriate saws for this, and then treat it until healed.
However, if a hardening occurs on the fingers, either frequently or due to the person's birth, or from a wound, burn, or similar cause, then you must remove this hardening until the fingers return to their natural shape.
Then place between them cloths soaked in rubbed oil, so they don't heal too quickly, and separate them or put a thin lead sheet between them until they heal correctly.
And similarly, if hardening occurs on any finger with the palm, then you remove this hardening according to its preparation and the member's proper shape.
Of incisions of veins, these are varicose veins.
Veins are varicose veins, and they are swollen, thick veins full of melancholic superfluities that appear in many parts of the body, and mostly in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer purgation of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But the cure for them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is raised and entirely extracted.
The section of it is done in this way: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, phlegmy blood from the leg is resolved, then tightly bind the patient's leg from the upper thigh area down to the area below the knee with a bandage.  Then, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upward and downwards, until the amount of blood you desire has been extracted, which will remove the effects of the illness.
And then tie it up, and command him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are filled and this is noticed by the patient.
But the raising of them is done in this manner: you raise the patient's leg, if there are many hairs on it, then put him in a bath and soak his leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent; or, if a bath is not available, exercise it by strenuous exercise until the limb is heated.
And then dry the skin directly over the vein by cutting it lengthwise or behind the knee, or at the very bottom of the leg below the ankle.
And then open the skin with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears, and it is towards the appearance that you will see that redness turns to blackness.
And then, as you squeeze, from the skin you see a white thread-like thing.
And then you place a poultice under it until it is raised and pulled out of the skin, and suspend it with a hook or a lion's claw.
And then make another incision near that incision every three days, and then scrape the skin above the vein until it appears.
And then lift that [affected part] and cut it in a second incision towards the heel, and then pull it out and lift it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out from the incision above that.
Do this until you remove that [affected part] from the incision that is highest or most prominent of all the incisions, until it is all out; and then cut it open.
And if you are unable to extract and lift it, then insert a needle with a strong, double-edged blade, and cut it, and pull it out, or insert a poultice under it and move your hand in all directions until it is thrown out. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it is difficult for you to lift that much and it has become a harm to the patient because of that injury.
And when that has been completely removed, then place on the affected area wool soaked in wine and oiled and unoiled, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having twists where it is wrapped around parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then you must cut across those twists and wrapped parts, and then suspend it with hooks until you lift it all up.
And be careful in your cutting not to cut the vein and not to harm it, for it will be difficult for you to lift it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not so sharp as the hooks, nor does it have a sharp tip so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of strong flexibility, like a lion's, for if it were delicate, it would cut the vein due to its delicacy; therefore, it has an unnecessary thickness, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in the land of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, with little moisture, and in which no grass or tree grows.
And it is readily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its formation is from putrefaction arising from under the skin, like serpents, worms, and maggots that breed between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is a vehement inflammation in the leg; then the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the area of the blister, appearing like the root of a plant.
And then, when the extremity of it appears, you should wrap a lead weight around it, of which the weight is one drachma, or two drachmas, and bind it, leaving the lead weight suspended from the leg.
And whenever any cause of it is found externally, wrap that in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut off a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not cut into its root until it has come out completely, for if you cut into it, it will retract and enter the flesh, causing abscesses and possibly a severe ulcer in the area.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled from behind it, and no part of it remains within the body.
And from this vein, there have been cases in some men where it has reached a length of five palms and ten fingers, and I have been informed of one case reaching twenty palms.
And if it is incised during its treatment, then insert the instrument into its opening and pierce through it with a long instrument, until everything contained within the material is evacuated.  Then, treat the site for a few days with medicines to address the putrefaction, and subsequently heal it like any other abscess.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot itself, and emerges from many openings, with each opening having a branch.
Therefore, treat it in the same way as we have described in the division and with the previously mentioned methods.
Of the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This illness is called "bovine disease" in some lands near us, because it often affects cattle, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh, and travels throughout the body, ascending and descending, and is sensed moving from limb to limb until it bursts through the skin and comes out.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humours, in the same way that worms, serpents, earthworms, and ascarids are produced in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it travels through the body and reaches the head, and arrives at the eye, it then often causes intense inflammation, enters and destroys the eye, and this happens frequently.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is only done by following its movement and appearance as sensed, and then you must tightly bind and constrict that which is above and below it, and then carefully cut around it and extract it.
And if it is embedded in the flesh and you cannot find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies, and the greatest feared consequence is its corruption, which corrupts the area as we have said.
And if you see that being near the eye, then tightly bind the area above the eyebrow with a firm constriction, and then carefully cut around it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use remedies to cleanse their body of the bad putrid humours, and guard themselves from foods that cause putrefaction.
Of the section on the illness of the nose.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and is a pain that comes to some limbs, and then moves from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that as I told you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm, and after I stayed for an hour, I saw that swelling move about like a worm climbing, but more swiftly than a worm might, like a bit of living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain went to that spot and settled in the muscle, and then I stayed there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other limb until it reached the other arm.
And then it continued to move around her whole body as I watched; and I was very amazed by its movement from one limb to another and its speed. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in that manner, but I have seen many instances where pain moves from one limb to another. But I did not see it with my eyes in such a way as in the woman.
And I did not think it was anything else, except that the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in this condition the movement of the air became apparent to the senses.
And it is necessary that this kind of reaction not appear in those who are delicate, with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to the eye as we have said.  Then you should gently and subtly press on it until the trapped air is released, and cauterize the affected area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with an emetic to expel the air, and use remedies like foul-smelling pills, or pills of serpentine and similar things.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and the parts of the body they are aimed at, for their variety is such that some have large points, some small, some concave, and some blunt; others have three angles; and some have tongues; and some have barbs.
And the signs of the brain injury, when an arrow strikes it and pierces the skull, are sudden violent pain, and dizziness, vertigo, pain in the eyes, inflammation and redness of the tongue, spasms and confusion of the mind, vomiting of bile.  The ability to speak is lost and the voice is destroyed.  A white, powdery moisture similar to pus leaks from the wound, and the area around it has the appearance of rotting flesh.
Therefore, if you see these signs, then refrain from treatment and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is protruding from the body.
However, the signs of an arrow striking the heart, especially if it's near the left breast, are that the arrow feels firmly lodged in a solid place, not a hollow space; and the movement of the arrow is readily felt, similar to the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound.  The extremities feel cold, the body sweats cold, and there are fainting spells.  Therefore, know that death will certainly follow.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the outflow of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck become inflamed.  The patient's complexion changes, they breathe heavily, and crave cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane around the foot, the pain is near the small ribs.  There is a significant tremor with intense pain and sighing, and all the muscles of the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then food will soon come out of the wound, undigested.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is lodged there, there will be some opening in the wound, or the tissue or intestines will be torn.  There is no hope of a cure without removing the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine comes out from the wound, and some cause for the external parts is present, and pain is soon upon the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, the other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, the extremities, the vertebrae of the back, the collarbone, the hip, and the leg and their similar members, are saved according to the greater provisions if the arrow does not strike a vein or artery, and it is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you this, which I have seen concerning these arrows, so that you may use it in your own treatment.
Which is, because in a man an arrow struck the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose, and I extracted it from the other side, below the ear, and he was healed, and no harm occurred to his eye.

Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which there are a customary thirty, are mentioned in chapter sixteen.  These include two pulsating veins near the ears, called *alhasisain*; two veins in the temples, *apparentz*; two veins in the tear ducts (of two eyes), called *aspicientz*; the vein in the middle of the forehead; the vein at the tip of the nose; two veins at the neck; two veins in the upper lip; two veins in the lower lip;  and—these are the veins called *algelic*— two veins beneath the tongue. However, the veins that are to be bled in the arms are five. One is the cephalic vein, located on the outer side of the arm, and is called "the head vein" by the common people.  It's superficial, a middle vein, composed of a branch from the basilic vein and another branch from the cephalic. The people call this "the vein of the whole body." The basilic vein is located on the inner side of the arm, and is called "the vein of the stomach" or "citillar" by the common people; it runs along the inside of the arm, near the elbow; and the vein that is bled from (and which appears prominently).  There's also the "salvatella," the vein between the thumb and the little finger, and it's located in two folds.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins. One of these is located below the bend of the outer knee; the second is the *sophena*, located beneath the inner side of the leg; and the sciatic vein, located beneath the outer side of the leg.  There are other similar veins in the leg.
The bleeding of veins near the ears is for headaches, migraines, fever, and old, bad sores of the head.
And the method of bleeding those veins, as I recommend to you, is as follows:  place a cold cloth on the patient's head, and rub the patient's head in the occipital region where both veins lie, with a wide cloth, applying firm pressure.  Press the patient's neck firmly with a bandage until the two veins and their locations near the ears, in two indentations of the head, are visible; feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then proceed with the lancet.
Now take the lancet, which is the sharp instrument, and insert it under the vein into the fold until the bleeding reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and the fold upward and cut the vein with a sharp incision. And the length of the incision should be about the width of two fingers; let the blood flow out as much as you want.
And then apply powders and leave it until it is healed.
And the incision of those and their cauterization have already been previously described at the beginning of the book.

And the benefit of phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for ailments in the lower part of the body, such as uterine illnesses, and retention of menstrual blood, and ailments of the kidneys and old sores and ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments.
And the procedure for phlebotomy involves the patient immersing their foot in warm water and applying friction until the veins appear clearly; then bind the joint of the foot with a strap; and the location of the vein, clearly visible near the said point, and from that point many branches extend along the surface of the foot.
And therefore, phlebotomize the widest vein, either below the leg towards the heel or at its junction, for that is better and more beneficial.
And if you phlebotomize that on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that are beneath it on the foot's surface, and make your incision with the lancet across it, as if you were intending to cut it through.
And let the phlebotomy be done skillfully.
But if bleeding is prohibited, then you should re-immerse the patient's foot in warm water and allow the blood to flow from it until it is evacuated.
And if the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then repeat the phlebotomy a little further along, for the area is safe; and do not fear harming him, but rather be careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly, do the same for the sole of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, beneath the bone of the outer side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy for him is in relieving hip pain when caused by hot blood.
And the method of phlebotomy for him is that the patient enters a bath, and quickly and tightly binds his thigh from the groin area upward to above the heel with a long, thin bandage for four days, as it is only apparent through this.
And then, when it appears, you perform phlebotomy on that area, according to whatever method is possible for you and appropriate to the vein, preferably; either incise it with an incision, or excise it through a resection, because the area for him is healed.
And it is very hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it doesn't appear in all its entirety, then phlebotomize a branch of it, and this is the one that appears on the back of the foot, between the so-called auricular and annular veins.
And be careful of the nerves, and draw off the amount of blood you desire.
And then loosen the constriction and place a poultice on the phlebotomy site and bind the area tightly, for it will heal quickly.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping is of horns, or of clay, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping that is administered with scarification and blood extraction, there are fourteen locations on the body.  One is at the back of the head; another is in the middle of the neck; cupping on the chin, below the jaw; cupping below the mouth; on both shoulder blades; on the elbow; on the forearms (or "two arm-pits"); on the two kneecaps; and on the two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from major veins, but rather from smaller veins scattered in the flesh.  Therefore, it does not deplete the body's vital forces as bloodletting does.  Likewise, cupping with or without fire should not be done for any illness caused by fullness until the entire body has been evacuated.
But if need compels cupping for an illness or a continual condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of the month, and in the middle of the month.
Therefore, when any of these conditions occur, especially in the middle or third of the month, we recommend cupping be done after two or three hours have passed.
Cupping the neck is beneficial when it alleviates heaviness in the head and that which obscures the eyes; however, it must be part of a general evacuation of the entire body.
And this cupping is in the location of a phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not recommended for use by those with a cold complexion, or those with a catarrh, as it would cause significant harm to them.
Therefore, it is not appropriate for use by the elderly, or those with cold illnesses in their heads.
However, for those who are continually suffering from this, the treatment is ineffective; therefore, you should instruct the cupper to place their hand slightly below the cupping glass on the lower parts, to avoid the generation of oblivion.
But cupping with alcohol is performed at the location of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica veins: and therefore it is beneficial for asthma, and for the constriction of the lungs and the splitting of the lung tissue, and for coughs and fullness.
And the cupping glass with alcohol should be raised slightly, for if it is placed too low it will generate weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* is beneficial for accidental head pains, and for ophthalmia, and migraine, and fever, and toothache; and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press their hand with the scarification tool, so that they do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping under the chin is beneficial for inflammation in the mouth, and for corruption of the gums, and for similar mouth ailments, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the veins located on the two lips.
But cupping on both shoulder blades is beneficial for *caphatan*, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two areas inside the armpits is beneficial as a substitute for the phlebotomy of three veins, namely the basilica, cephalic, and basilic, because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and those small veins draw from the larger veins until the drawing is directed toward the three named veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply with the scarification, for the area is thin on flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping that is applied to the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids and ulcers in the lower part of the anus.
And it is necessary to instruct the cupper to use a large, bronze cupping, for the area requires strong suction; and small, delicate cupping is ineffective and should only be scarified with a specific scarification technique.
However, cupping of the legs is less effective due to its obvious limitations, as it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for old pains in the kidneys, bladder and uterus; it induces menstruation and benefits boils and tumors; and it serves as a substitute for the phlebotomy of two or more superficial veins and veins.
However, it greatly weakens the whole body and causes fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping to the corresponding areas is similar to the benefit of cupping on both legs.
And the proper application of cupping is to place the empty cup at first and create moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cups, but place them gently, and remove them carefully, allowing the humors to return to the area naturally. And do not stop repeating this process until the area becomes red and swollen, and the redness of the blood appears.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the condition of the body.
For that person with delicate skin, and prone to fear, you should make incisions only once, so that the area is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to increase the incisions slightly, and moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then you should make incisions twice, for in the first instance, you open the path for the blood and its moisture to be drawn out. But in the second, to further investigate the removal of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then a third incision should be made so that the final removal is achieved.
And finally, when we want to remove the blood slowly or a little bit, a single incision is enough for us.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know that the blood is thick, then we should make deep incisions.
And the term for incision in which the depth is only the length of a fingernail.
And if the affected area has thick, hard, dark skin, and its pores are tight, the places where the cupping vessels are to be placed should be treated with softening, opening, and resolving ointments.  If it is summer, use almond oil, violet oil, gentle chamomile oil, or prickly poppy seed oil; and if it is winter, use narcissus oil, lily oil, camomile oil, or bloodroot oil, and similar preparations.
And if the excess is thick and cold, then the oil should be arnica oil, balm oil, almond oil, dill oil, and similar oils.
But if the area to be cupped has large pores and soft flesh, then oil should be avoided.  Instead, the areas around the cups should be washed with herbs, or with cold spring water, or with poppy, or with borage water, and similar preparations.
And if the person's blood is of a moist nature, wash their cupped areas with sour wine, or with water mixed with myrtle and sumac, and similar liquids.
And for those whose excesses are thick, wash the cupped areas with old wine, or with water infused with yarrow (which is marjoram), or a decoction of dill or chamomile, or similar preparations.
And the cupping should be done before a bath; if done afterwards, it should be administered shortly after the bath for an hour or two, and the patient should rest/sleep after the cupping.
What is it that should be regulated before cupping or bloodletting?
One must consider at the outset: if the person being cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp, inflamed blood, then they should take cooling things, such as sour milk, endive in sour wine, lettuce, secanabin, julep, and similar remedies.
And give them food like barley, and the flesh of small birds, and foods seasoned with vinegar, and similar things.
And for the one whose constitution is cold, they should drink almiara syrup, or alsecanabin seed syrup, and sumac, and moderately sweet wine;  and let it be recommended to them to use little food and drink, and let their food be barley, hemp, small birds, and pigeons.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more plentiful than the food.
And it is necessary to give certain men some theriac, or musk-based remedies, or alcelita, before the cupping or bloodletting, or shortly afterward, so that the main parts of the body are strengthened and the blood is refined; and they should not take those remedies when they are hot.
But the cupping, without scarification, should be placed over the liver, the spleen, gout, the navel, the knee areas, and the kidneys, for these parts of the body do not tolerate scarification on their surface.
And it is sought in these cases not for the drawing of blood from a part of the body, just as we place a cupping glass on a breast in a disease of blood from the nose; but we use this to dissolve the cold, viscous air from the part of the body, like when we place the cupping glass on the abdomen and navel; for it rarefies the part, warms it, and alleviates the pain, because it resolves the air.
And it is placed upon the temples when there is obstruction or phlegm in those parts, for by the virtue of strong suction, it easily opens the blockage and eradicates the phlegm from its location.
And similarly, it works when placed upon the liver and spleen to relieve air trapped within them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with suction alone; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and made larger, either with warm water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
The form of cupping administered with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have shaped it; and let the quantity of the heating be half a palm; and let it be on the patient's back, in the area that is in the center of the back; the hole should be small, just enough for a needle to enter.
Let it be made of a red or citrus wood, with thick edges,  lion, and rounded and smooth surfaces so that it does not damage the patient's limb, preserving its position on the body part.
And there should be a transverse rod of wood or iron in the middle, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping should be larger than the one previously described; and the smaller one should be smaller, adjusted to the illnesses and age of the user.
And the cupping for children and the weakened are different from the cupping for young people and those with strong bodies.
And the method of applying this cupping with fire to the limb is to light a fire with good wood,  using a small beeswax candle, and placing it on the middle of the transverse rod in the center of the cupping device, so the fire is ignited around the lower part, preventing the patient's body from being burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be on the opening we mentioned, until, when we have the cupping devices and we do not want the finger to show and the vapor to escape through that opening, the cupping will quickly be dissolved.
And relight the fire according to its method, and repeat it if necessary.
But a cupping used for the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or opening, and is not administered except to be filled with water and placed on the limb alone.
And this is the manner of it:  The larger this cupping, the better, so that it can receive a greater quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many members, but only to those members where cupping is not possible, due to their small size, like the foot and gums, and similar parts; or to a limb devoid of flesh, like the finger and nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using those leeches that you have found in the clear, shallow waters of muddy areas.
And then they [the leeches] should be left in fresh water day and night until they are hungry, and no food remains in their bellies.  And then, initially, blood should be evacuated through phlebotomy and cupping; and then, the patient's limb should be rubbed until it reddens; and then, the leeches should be placed on it.
And when they [the leeches] are full and have fallen off.
And if suction from the area with a cupping vessel is possible, it is better afterwards for healing.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water; and it should be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood from the area around the leeches' bite is prolonged, and is oozing, then you should dip a cloth in cold water and place it over the area until the oozing is stopped.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle powdered vitriol, or other similar astringent substances, over the area until the bleeding stops; or place the roasted halves of beans on the affected area and leave them until the beans adhere to the area, and the bleeding is stopped.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when possible to change them.
And if the use of leeches is forbidden or suspended, then the area should be rubbed with fresh blood or a needle should be inserted into the area until blood flows; and then [the leeches] should be placed, because they adhere to the area as soon as they feel blood.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little powdered alum, or salt, or ashes, over them, and they fall off quickly.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish and vulgar physicians, and those who have never consulted the books of the Ancients nor read a single letter from them, boast about this chapter.
And for this reason, this manner of science in our land is private; and I have not forgotten this at any time entirely; and I have not adopted this that I have learned, but by the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying their meaning, until I extracted the science from them.
And I have devoted myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experience has passed; since I have summarized this for you and freed you from the labor of elongation; and I have abridged it at the end of the abridgement; and have demonstrated it at the end of the declaration; and have formed in this many forms of the instruments that are administered in it, as is done by adding a declaration, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the nature of bone fractures, and the necessary preliminaries before we begin to remember broken limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us state at the beginning of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters whose understanding is initially required for the craftsman and to stand on the truth of those; this is you and those who are ready to learn this noble art, either themselves or from you.
And therefore I say that when an accident occurs such as a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or blow, then it is necessary for you to begin with bloodletting or to reduce the swelling, or both, if no cause forbids it, such as weakness of the body, or if the person affected is a child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, or if the time is of intense heat or intense cold.
And then, let their diet consist of cool herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and forbid fatty meats and overfilling with food, and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain of a hot abscess, and if you do not fear a discharge of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to their initial state of health, with which they were accustomed.
And therefore, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary for the patient to eat nutritious foods, thick, heavy, and viscous foods, such as foods made from boiled wheat with water, and rice, and dumplings, and the belly and insides of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar to these, and with this regimen the fracture heals more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and the decrepit, in their first injury, cannot be healed and made whole according to their nature, due to the drying up of their limbs.
But nevertheless, nature adjusts and brings about a glue-like substance, thick and adhering, around the broken bone, until it is completely joined and bound together, until it reaches the point of strength and firmness, just as with fire at the beginning until no external force hinders its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient consumes foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the head bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all the other limbs have different types of fractures.
And I will now come to a recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is a diversity of fractures in themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is a curved fracture, and one that occurs in star-like patterns; and sometimes a fracture runs along the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the bone, jagged, splintered, and proceeding or non-proceeding, just as is the case with the pen when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a groove and rupture in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type there is a specific treatment or remedy in restoring it, as will be recalled distinctly in their proper places.
A sign of a bone fracture. And by this a fracture of the bone is known: it is the twisting of it, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and this is felt when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location, nor is it felt by your touch when you touch the bone with pressure and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a twisting or a small crack, so it is not necessary for you to examine it with your hand and with all the pressure; instead, place upon it the remedies of which the memory is relevant to that moment, the appropriate ones to the location, and then tightly bind it with a subtle bandage.
And know that when the location is bent but not split in two or otherwise damaged in such a way as to prevent proper alignment, when the whole part of its shape is restored, then you must begin immediately to straighten it, lest there be a harmful abscess.
And if there is a harmful abscess there, then leave it for several days until the abscess subsides; and then heal it by any means you can with gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of the soft tissues is easier than that of the bone, which has sustained damage.
And then bind it according to the memory of what is required.
But if the bone is fractured, then the fractured limb (whether hand or foot) needs to be extended by two parts, or with your hand if the limb is small, or with two ropes or one rope and your hand.
And be sure to position the limb in the correct natural shape, while the parts of the fractured limb are being stretched, then you must endeavor to return those additions to their places by skill and any means possible.
And be careful, through your treatment, not to cause pain or injury to the patient.  Strive to your utmost to put one bone back together with its fellow in their best alignment.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch it with your hand, and if you encounter any unusual cause, correct it and make it whole to the best of your ability.
Avoid excessive stretching and compression, as some foolish doctors often cause abscesses or contractions in the limb through their treatment, as we have seen in their many instances.
The proper support for a calmed limb is what I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs vary in their size, their breadth, and in the manner in which they are broken.
For those that are small, such as an arm, finger, or similar parts, then a wrapping of fine, soft cloth is necessary.
However, for larger limbs such as the thigh or leg, a firm, strong wrapping is necessary, for a firm binding connects the large limb tightly and firmly, so that no swelling occurs.
Then begin, after your treatment, to apply a suitable bandage to the fracture site, using three or four wraps, depending on the limb's needs; and gently tighten the bandage.
And then, with that, move to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten the bandage less than you did on the fracture site itself. Then slowly loosen the bandage on the fracture site a little, and gradually relax the tightening, until you have reduced the swelling by a large amount.
Then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site, and then move the wraps to the lower part of the fracture; and your procedure should involve tightening and loosening the wrapping in the same way as described in the first wrapping above.
Then place between the wraps a linen or cloth pad to support the irregularity of the fracture, if there is any; if not, do not place anything there.
Then place another bandage over these neatly wrapped layers immediately, if there is no inflammation or hot abscess in the area.  If there is, place on it something that will relieve the abscess and reduce the inflammation. Leave that in place for several days, and then tighten the layers over it.
Let the splints be of half-cane, or of fine, well-formed wood, or of palm ribs; and let the width of each splint be according to this shape and form:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly larger and thicker than the others, and its length should correspond to the size and smallness of the limb; then, tighten the splints with another bandage, following your initial method of tightening.  Finally, tie it securely with thread, as previously described in terms of tightening.  The tightness should be greatest over the fracture site, and the tightness should diminish the further away from the fracture you go.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fine, and made of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operations of some foolish people using twisted rope for threads, that is a major error, for it will disrupt the proper tightness.
Likewise, excessively fine threads are not suitable, for you will not achieve the desired tightness with them.  There should be at least a finger's width between each splint.
And if the patient complains of the edges of the splints pressing on their sensitive areas during the tightening, then place padding of soft wool or linen under those areas to prevent injury.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a dislocation occurs in the joint.  I will address this in the second chapter on unique cases.
And know that not every fractured limb is immediately fastened with splints; the reason is that when the limb is large, you should not apply splints until about five or seven days, or longer, depending on your assessment of whether a suppurating abscess might form.
This is the method of plasters with which the ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and twists.
A method of a common plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many constitutions, especially women and children, as it does not produce heat or cold: take pulverized flour, specifically the pith of the flour that remains on the walls of the mill after grinding.
And mix it like this, but sift it with egg white, and make the mixture neither too thick nor too thin; and then apply it.
Another method of plaster for treating fractures, dislocations, and twists: take asafoetida, opoponax, acacia, enula, and wild chamomile root (all of which should be a drachma each), myrrh and aloe (five drachmas each), aloes (twenty drachmas), all ground, sifted, and mixed with warm water or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; then apply this plaster, as it is a good composition and heals slightly fractured bones, and is helpful to many people.
Another method of plaster helpful for fractures and twists: take wild chamomile and white marshmallow root (each a drachma), myrrh and aloe (five drachmas each), acacia (six drachmas), and Armenian bole (twenty drachmas).
All are ground in a mortar with thorough grinding, sifted, mixed with water or egg white, and applied.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their place, and for accidental pain there, and for fissures and fractures: take wool, soak it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but rather relieves hot abscesses and expels pain effectively.
Another method of plaster to restore a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; grind them together and make a plaster from them.
A quick plaster for the restoration of a broken bone and when you want resolution of a remaining abscess: take roots of marsh mallow, chamomile, violet flowers, and elderberry flour; grind them all into a powder; mix it with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix it with damp coriander water or with water and use it.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving a stubborn abscess that hinders the limb's restoration: take roots of marshmallow, linseed, elderberry, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile; mix them into a paste; all ground into a powder and mixed with willow water, or fresh water, or wine, depending on the quality of the limb and the color's condition.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that a plaster should be placed on a broken limb for its restoration, but rather a cerate made of wax and oil, and nothing else; and he recommended it to be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
But in Gaul, it is observed that plasters are placed on a broken limb to aid its restoration, and in cases of desiccation with a slight warmth, such as myrrh, incense, and similar things to the ones previously mentioned.
A plaster for wounds and pain: take equal parts of honey, human skin (or the skin of an animal), marshmallow root, and salt; grind them into a powder, sieve it, and form a plaster.
However, the length of time the bandage should remain in place, and when it should be removed, depends on whether pain, inflammation, or a bone fracture or dislocation occurs.  If none of these occur, then do not remove it for several days. And if severe pain, inflammation, or swelling occurs at the site, then you should loosen or remove it immediately and remove the plaster.
And take a clean cloth and a moistened marine sponge, and immerse it in lukewarm water, and use it to wash the area until the inflammation subsides and the pain is relieved; then let the limb rest for an hour.  Then place a piece of wool soaked in vinegar and olive oil (or just warmed olive oil) over it, and bandage it overnight, ensuring that any potential inflammation or pain is reduced or resolved, and that the swelling of the limb subsides.
And then apply a thin, small plaster, not as tightly as your initial application, and use it until the area is healed.
And if, at times, the abscess, swelling, pain, inflammation, and all the other symptoms are completely gone, and you no longer require the plaster and bandage, then you should apply another one, in the same way as you did initially.
And if no symptoms from those we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not remove the bandage for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes it is left for twenty days, as indicated by the treatment of the limb, as we have stated, until the fracture heals and the flesh around it solidifies.
Then, make the bandage tighter than your first one, and increase the patient's intake of nourishing food, as dictated by the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fractured area already drying out and weakening more than necessary, understand that nourishment cannot reach it.  Therefore, bathe that area with lukewarm water every time you remove the bandage, and do so for three days.  Loosen the bandage slightly, and through this procedure, nourishment flows to the limb, facilitating quick healing.
However, what those foolish bone-setters do, restoring the limb fracture a second time when it was not initially set correctly, and setting it crookedly, is an error in their procedure and a great danger. If they were correct, the ancients would have written about this in their books and would have used this approach.
And I have not seen anyone of them do that, which makes it more reasonable that it should not be done.
Second Chapter: Concerning fractures when the head is injured.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow with a blunt object, and the blow crushes the entire bone up to the spinal cord, which is beneath the bone, just as it does to a tree trunk; and therefore, this type is called a comminuted fracture; or the blunt object cuts a part of the bone and cuts the flesh and does not penetrate to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the severity of these two types of fractures can be great or small, and the fracture may be a crushing or a grinding type; and the cause is a blow with a rock or a fall on a stone, and similar causes.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two types of fractures is either great or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the smallness of a hair's breadth, and therefore this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.
And there is a fracture that is caused either by a fall or a blow from a stone, and similar things, that causes a portion of the bone to be pushed inward, and it is made in the concave area, just as happens to the ribs when they are hit, and a portion of them is driven inward.
And this happens more often in the soft bones of infants' heads.
And in all these types of fractures, there are fragments, like separate pieces of a broken star. And I shall come with a remedy for all of this, returning it to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fracture by examination of those areas and by investigating them with probes and examination of the corrupt flesh on them.
A particular type of fracture is recognized because it is exposed on the bone, and is scraped clean and probed, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms, and if you see through the symptoms that they signify a dangerous condition, such as vomiting, diarrhea, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, and swelling and inflammation of the limbs, and similar symptoms in the most serious cases, without a doubt, it is not going to be a successful recovery.
And if you see symptoms that are not severe, you hope for recovery, then you begin the treatment.
The reason for this is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning of the illness, as when afflicted during the winter days, then you must study the condition of the bone by the fourteenth day, according to the overall prognosis.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must try to set the bone by the seventh day, before the area under the bone is damaged by the tissue, and the symptoms we have described occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the tissue covering the brain, and is with small pieces and attrition, then you must cut away the broken and damaged pieces, as I will explain to you.
And you are to shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and method as best you can, according to the nature of the injury, and be gentle with the patient.
And if you encounter a detection of bone, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must counter it with what is necessary, and you should fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rubbed oil, and let the injury rest until the abscess is calmed and the blood flow ceases; and then begin with the circulation of the bone and the removal of it.
And this will be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow scalpel, and this is its form: And then apply another scalpel to it, a little further out than that one; and this is its form:
And then apply a scalpel further out than the second one.
And you need to have several different scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with their sharpened ends at the back, and made of tempered or hardened iron.
And use them with care in percussion on the scalpel so that it does not strike the head and break it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to drill a hole around it before applying the scalpels with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not called this way except because those do not reach the surface of the skull bone near it, because the drill has a round end or edge on that which is under the head, sharp like an edge or a small circle, which prevents being submerged and going through the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate that you take a large number of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand in all cases of the skull a tip that is sharp, the length and shortness are according to the thickness of each skull.
And this is the method for three types of drills:  The method for drilling around the fractured bone is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is already penetrated; then, change the drill to another location, and place that which is in between each hole at the thickness of the ray or approximately that.
Then, cut with scalpels what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as possible, so as to achieve a lightness, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other means using tools you have prepared for this, like pliers and delicate forceps.
And you must take great care that the scalpel does not touch any part of the spinal column.
And therefore, after you have drilled around the bone, and if it is connected to the spinal column, separate it, and remove it.  You should then rasp and smooth the rough edges of where it was attached to the bone, using a tool similar to a scalpel, but it must be thinner and finer than the other scalpels.
And if any small pieces of bone remain and are loose, then remove them easily with the tools you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
Another method of operation is gentle, free of fear, which, as the great Galen said, is beneficial and worthy of praise.  This is his advice:  For you to begin, it is necessary to uncover the portion of the bone at the most severe and obvious fracture point, until you have exposed that section and placed the tip of a slow and light scalpel, in this manner...
Its blade is shaped like a lentil, and does not cut anything, while the sharp edge of the blade has two sides extending lengthwise, as you see, in such a way that the lentil-shaped part of the blade fits against the spinal column, with the sharp edge of the blade in contact with the bone.
Then strike the scalpel at that point with a small mallet until you easily cut the entire bone as it is turned.  Be certain of the safety and that there will be no accident that you fear in any way.  And if the physician were the most foolish of all men, and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were asleep.
Then, if any part remains attached to the bone, in any part of the bone, cut it from the bone with the lentil-shaped tip of the scalpel and remove it easily, for it is detached without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate the flesh, and what is cut off or removed from the bone is the surface of it, and there remain roughnesses and tiny irregularities, then it is necessary to smooth out those roughnesses and eliminate those irregularities with the fine scalpels you have prepared, of various sizes so you can apply the most suitable ones to every part and place them correctly depending on the position and the shape of the broken bone and its fracture.
And you must use the widest of those scalpels in your initial scraping of the bone, and then progressively use the finer ones, and do not stop doing this until you arrive at using the finest and most delicate of them all.
However, for each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle breaks, you must provide treatment according to what you find necessary for their repair.
And this is a cause that is not hidden from all who study this art. And this is what we have written about in large fractures.
And if the skin is pulled back when you expose the bone, then you must take a cloth of linen the size of the wound, and soak it in warm oil, and place it over the wound opening; and then take another cloth, folded double or triple, and soak it in wine and warm oil, and place that over the linen cloth, and do this as gently as possible so that you do not press too hard on the flesh; and then apply a thick bandage, and do not tighten it except for the amount necessary to simply hold the wound closed.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure there is no festering; then remove it and administer medicines to counteract any dryness, such as linen roots and flour of herbs, and powders of fennel and aristolochia, and similar things.
And you should make a powder from these medicines, pulverized and dry, and sprinkle it on the wound.  In general, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse but not to cause irritation.
And be careful, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and do not allow any suppuration or other corruption to build up there; nor allow the virulence to increase; for when it is concentrated on the skin of the brain, it corrupts and poisons it, and this will cause serious harm to the patient.
And it causes harm to the flesh of the head, especially when the bone is exposed and the treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if you observe in the patient the symptoms we have described, know that it is dangerous without a doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the medicine applied, but rather a result of the medicine's properties, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rubbed oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, and place it on the flesh; and then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two separate, supremely important parts, or even in one in some cases, because there are two bones in it, for the lower part is cartilaginous and is not fractured.  It is only bruised, twisted, and bent.
However, if one of the two parts is fractured, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and push that fracture inwards with your index finger, and the thumb outwards, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this easily, and be careful not to cause further pain to the afflicted person by your actions.
And if the fracture is in the most important part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it must be straightened with the tip of a tool with a small amount of thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do likewise.
And strive for the patient's restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the 7th or 10th day, apply a cautious poultice.
And then insert a piece of linen cloth into the tip of the nose, if the fracture is in one part, or place two bandages, if the fracture is in two parts, and the thickness of the bandage should be sufficient to fill the nasal cavity.
And some of the ancient healers say that the bandages should be soaked in water and changed daily, but I do not see the need for that; rather, I say that the cloth should be soaked in a mixture of egg white and powdered mortar; and leave the bandage in place until the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a bandage, a small amount of bird's down wrapped in linen is inserted into the nose; this is appropriate if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do it this way, and if you wish, do it with bandages.
And if there is another abscess or inflammation, plaster the nose with cerot or cotton soaked in sour wine and oil of roses, or with diachylon.
But if there is no inflammation or abscess, then you must plaster the exterior with sesame flour and powdered incense mixed with egg white; then place a linen bandage over it, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are fractured or broken into small pieces, then you must dissect them, and remove them with appropriate instruments. Afterward, close the fracture and heal it with things that cause healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if there is a wound inside the nose, then you must treat it with bandages and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, observe if the fracture is only displaced, and not broken in two but bent inward, then its repositioning is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you must insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of your right hand; and with that, push the displaced part of the fracture gently outward, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; with that, gently reposition it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to apply traction to both parts along a straight line until the repositioning of the parts is possible.
And if you encounter tooth damage along with a break, then tie those teeth which you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a compress on the broken jaw, then place a doubled cloth over it, and then place a large, properly made bandage, or a single piece of gauze equal to the length of the jaw; and then bind it as your bandage application allows, until it is no longer damaged.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him nourishing, easily digestible food.
And if you estimate that the shape has already been altered in some way, then you attend to it and loosen it on the third day; and then you rectify whatever is altered and cover it with powdered chalk, egg white, or ground flaxseed, after removing the compress, and place a soft wool bandage over the plaster.
And then, when that plaster is firmly fixed and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture is strong; for often this fracture is mended in three weeks.
And if another time a painful boil arises, then administer what we have often said for its calming until it is healed, and the boil is gone.
And if the fracture has a wound, then consider if it is a piece of bone separated or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with whatever is appropriate for their removal from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you enlarge it with leeches according to the amount you need.
And when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the suitable plasters on it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the furcula when it is broken.
Many times the furcula is broken in the part before the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters, it is easy to restore; or it is such that splinters are present, and its restoration is difficult; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the fractured furcula and the other extends the collar towards that area.
And then approximate the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and is neither overly prominent nor concave.
And if you need more extension, then you must place under the patient's armpit a pad of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your needs; and extend and elevate the furcula and compress the pad with your hand until the fracture is approximated as needed.
And if you cannot pull the fracture's extremities outward, because it has become stuck (or impaled?), then the patient must be laid on their back, and place a cushion of moderate size on their muscles.
And the assistant presses the muscles underneath to elevate the furcula bone that is stuck upwards, and then you straighten the fracture and approximate it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the furcula and they are sharp, then you must cut over them and remove those splinters easily.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you create an incision there with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after you have placed the tool that serves to hold the furcula underneath; and this tool is of wood or iron, of which this is its form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to your need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
However, its length should be as possible for the operation; it should have two ends like this, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound you have incised when extracting the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure of a suppurating boil, then you should bring the two edges of the wound together with stitches.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a suppurating boil, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's opening.
And if a suppurating boil occurs, then soak a cloth in vinegar, olive oil, and wine, and place it over it.
And make sure the bandage does not move off the broken bone.  Therefore, you must examine the patient daily, and when you see the ligament has softened or the bandage has slipped, then you should adjust it and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient's head on their neck, and place a small cushion under the patient's chin to elevate the upper arm from the side, thus elevating the fracture of the collarbone, and so the patient's arm is tied to the neck.
Do not loosen the bandage if a suppurating boil or similar swelling occurs in the area, for up to twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen a change in the affected area, and adjust the tightness and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the collarbone has been restored and has coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month, in 28 days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fractured shoulder.
The shoulder blade is broken in its lateral location, and it is not broken *from* him/her, but rather, the edges are broken.
And therefore, when the location is broken—and it is broken in the middle of the location—and it is only known through touch, then, according to the figure of the fracture, prepare it and reduce it to its natural shape by every manner of ingenuity possible.
And then apply powdered mastic with egg white and stop-leon; and then apply a folded cloth; and then place a thin, flat piece of lead over it, equal to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or slightly larger.
But if, beneath the lead, there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then fill that hollow with stop-leon, and then raise the lead to an equal level.
And then hold it firmly in place with a long bandage, with proper constriction, and with the firmness of the lead until it is no longer displaced from its position.
And observe the bandage every day; and every time it has softened, bandage again and tighten it, and adjust the lead if it has been displaced from its position.
And let the patient lie on their sound side.
And the shoulder blade will be restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and be sure that the connection is secure between the bones, so that their disunion is not feared.
