And if you proceed from the location of the injured bone and place a splint under it, then you should cut over it, remove it, and act as we have described in the fracture of the furcula, or treatment of a boil, or if an abscess occurs there.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this is often severe; but its extremities are often more severely crushed and splintered.
And the consequences of the fracture in the middle of the foot include deviation to the sides, great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, severe weakness, and vomiting of blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is revealed, not hidden from the feeling/perception.
And the restoration of the foot involves laying the patient on their back, placing a cushion between their shoulders; then pressing on their muscles, and adjusting the bones with the hands on both sides, carefully and meticulously in the operation and according to every possible and suitable way, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then apply a poultice and bandage and place a thin sheet of willow or reed or similar lightweight material over that, wrapping it in cloth; then carefully bind it around the broken bone so it doesn't move; and tie it around the back in a circular manner sometimes, and tighten it properly; and then check the bandage frequently, and when softened, tighten it further.
And if the bandage causes ulceration, pus, pain, or an abscess in the area, then you loosen and remove it and remove the poultice and rectify what was caused by it with the proper treatment; and then you reapply the poultice, or if necessary, the dressing; and maintain the tightness until it heals.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of broken bones.
Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in the thick parts, which are the middle sections; and on the outer edges, or front parts, damage is not caused in the same way, as those sections are cartilaginous. And knowledge of this is not hidden from the feeling of it through examining it with words.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is brought together with the fingers in the most possible way, until it is held together as necessary, and then apply a poultice and bind the broken bone with a bandage, if needed.
And if the fracture of the ribs is situated towards the internal parts, then the patient experiences severe pain and stabbing, similar to the stabbing that comes with pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and it causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were ingenious in dealing with that with many clever methods.
And of their methods, they say:  it is necessary to withhold foods from the patient that engender inflation and windiness, so that the belly remains inflated and stretched, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we would avoid this, as it is not a light matter to generate a hot abscess, if it doesn't happen; and if it does happen, then we'll address it.
And some of them would say that a cupping glass should be placed over it and forcefully drawn; and it seems a reasonable approach, but nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the area due to weakness.
And some of them would say:  the area should be covered with wool that has been immersed in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so the dressing fits the rounded shape; and then the patient should be treated with pleurisy cures, both dietary and medicinal.
And if a severe cause afflicts the patient, who cannot bear it, and the bone pierces the membrane with a harmful puncture, we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the area, expose the broken rib, and then place an instrument on it to hold the fractured piece in place, a procedure preceded by a memory/rehearsal, and cut the bone with ease, removing it.
And then, if the wound is large, we should bring together the two sides of the wound with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and the patient should be treated with whatever is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured vertebra of the back and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and sometimes their attrition, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone, and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you repeatedly touch their muscles, which are already softened, numb, dead, and cannot be moved, stretched, or tightened, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they do not feel any pain, then know that they are not healed, and in this state of affairs they are in mortal danger.
And if you move them and feel constriction with your fingers and pricks, then know that the bone's integrity is preserved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the same signs in the neck vertebrae and want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, similar to what happened in the muscles, and when they lie on their back, they have involuntary wind and bowel movements, and sometimes they are lying on their back wanting to urinate and cannot, then know that it is fatal, and therefore do not bother with their treatment.
But if you do not see any of those symptoms, the cause is better and more easily treatable.
And the treatment for this is to apply a poultice to the inflamed vertebra, using either oiled or roasted herbs, or even boiled egg whites.  Apply this several times a day until the inflamed area subsides.
And then apply a comforting and soothing plaster, and bind it firmly.  Order the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe signs of bone breakage or some other separation from the surrounding tissue, then it is necessary to dissect the skin over the bone and remove the displaced bone; then rejoin the two edges of the wound, with sutures if it is large.  Then treat it with some kind of healing ointment until it is healed.
But if the bone at the back of the hip is fractured, and it is at the end of the thigh bone, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the buttock, and align the fractured bone with the other hand as much as possible by aligning the ends; then apply a plaster and bandage, if necessary, and firmly bind it.
And if you find any broken pieces in it, then you should remove them, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] width/breadth.
The bones of the hips are often fractured, and if they break, then the fracture of them is only a communication at their extremities, and they are cut lengthwise and deviate inwards; and the patient experiences pain and a puncture in the area, and the leg is numb when fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you should extend your hand over it until it is upon the fracture, which is its form.
And if the fracture is only at one end, then you should bind it in accordance with the instrument that you have prepared for aligning it until it is restored to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along the length or already deviates inwards, then you should have the patient lie on their stomach until you are prepared to restore that fracture. And then, once you have bound it, apply a poultice, and afterwards place a splint of wood or bone, and apply pressure firmly, so that you do not fear the piercing of the fracture or the movement of the splint.
And bind the hollow with that which fills it, so that it maintains a firm hold equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a painful abscess arises, then you should cease the extension and restoration until the painful abscess is soothed, and apply whatever soothes it as previously described.  Then return to the restoration and bind it as necessary.
And if splinters or fragments of bone arise, then you should remove them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured limb.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the shoulder (joint).
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness in this way:
And two ligaments are tied to its two ends, and then it is suspended from a raised position.
And have the patient lie on a stretcher; and then place the patient's broken arm on the wood until the bend of the arm is in the middle of the curve of the wood.
And then place some heavy object on it, or the attendant extends it downwards; and then the doctor holds the fracture with his hands together until the fracture returns as is proper.
The other method is for the patient to lie on their back, and tie the arm of the patient's neck with a bandage.  Then, instruct two attendants that one of them should hold what's above the fracture with their hands; and the other hold what's below it; and each should pull in their direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten the bandage over the fracture and another bandage on top of that, and each of the two attendants pulls in their respective direction.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half the bandage be placed on the bend and the other below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that spot and over the elbow itself.
Apply it easily and without effort, and then, once it's bandaged correctly and applied well, tighten it if there is risk of a suppurating boil.
But if a hot abscess arises, then leave the tightness until the seventh day, and place wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil over it until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten it is to place a plaster over the fracture, then place a wrap of clean cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm over that support, and place their open hand over their own muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the support on the arm so that the arm is in its proper position, unless some aspect of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And you should place on the fracture the splint that is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the tightening method I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And ensure your tightening on the fracture site is more tight. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the tightening should be.
And thus adjust the positioning of the splints and the tightening as we have described, at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do so.
And if you fear a suppurative abscess, then leave the tightening and splints in place for seven days, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament in all parts for three days, so that no bruising or inflammation occurs in the area; or food should be withheld from the limb due to excess tightening; and therefore, correct all of this according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will arise, then do not loosen the binding until approximately three days, or longer.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, with their hands on their stomach.
And place a full, oiled, wool-filled pad beneath the support, and take care at all times of night and day that the shape of the broken limb is not impaired, or that the ligament is softened; then correct all of this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we previously described, so that the food is subtle at first until the tightening has begun; and then it is necessary to increase the amount of food.
Because, according to the custom of supporting and bandaging the limb, it is to be bound tightly for forty days; and then it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath administered, and medicated with ointments that correct the condition.
And if the fracture is severely damaged, then you do not release the ligament and splints from it for a month or even two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two "foci" (or, perhaps, "sockets"); one of which is small, and it is the one that sits near the said elbow, and the other is large and it is the one situated below the small one, in the lower part.
And frequently the larger "focus" is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently they are both broken together.
And when the smaller "focus" is broken above, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the "focus" is broken below, then the fracture is serious, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller "focus" above, then it is suitable for the physician, in order for its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease until it is healed.
But if the large "focus" is what is broken, then it is necessary that a more forceful extension be applied.
And if both "focuses" are broken together, then it is necessary that you apply the pressure much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the shape of your hand for the restoration of it and the extension of it spread out over a cushion, and the palm of the hand should be at the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the thumb should be lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be resting on a padded bed.
And there should be a cushion in front of him at his height, so that the patient does not experience trouble.
And then the attendant extends the arm of the lower part with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant extends the upper part similarly.
And then the physician manipulates the bone until it is returned to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are obstructions, then you must endeavor to return every piece to its place with your skill.
And if there appears a separated obstruction that prevents the bone from mending and you are not hopeful about restoring it, then you should perform on it and remove it in the manner we have described above.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of that.
And if you encounter a suppurating abscess at the beginning of your restoration, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of roasted oil and white wax, which should be moderate in its consistency and subtlety, and tightly bind the cloth over it with fine strips until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a prepared poultice of powdered moly with egg white, and then place strips; and let the strip placed over the fracture be the thickest and strongest.
And know that the name of the splints for the arm is used in the major arrangements, or that is, a fracture in one compartment or in two compartments together.
And then apply your strips to the fracture site with as much strength and vigor as possible, and if the strips are placed above or below the fracture, then place the strips a little more loosely, just as the recollection of it was done earlier in the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be very damp cloths, and not hard or rough, and the bandage used to bind them should be of a medium consistency, neither too thin nor too thick, as we have already described.
And consider the limb and its ligaments for a few days. And if any cause arises that necessitates correction, such as inflammation occurring on the limb, then you should soak the limb in hot water until that inflammation subsides.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandages.
And if the constriction has softened and the bone has rejoined and appears as it should, then rectify everything with your skill.
And further consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive constriction, for then it is necessary to soften that a little and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then bind it tightly again.
However, if the patient does not have any of the causes we have mentioned, then it should not be untied for about 20 days or thereabouts.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own power of active movement, and make the patient sleep on their side.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or a very slight one is restored in 28 days, and all this is according to the disposition of the body's constitution and the strength of the healing process.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures of the wrist and the joints of the fingers often occur, and they are not always caused by accidents but often by overuse.
And when a fracture or overuse injury occurs to the hand, then the patient should be placed lying down and the hand should be placed on the extended arm in a straight line.
And then the attendant extends the broken bones, and the doctor binds them together until they are properly aligned.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if not a boil is developing; and then place a splint over the area according to its size, and you should already have wrapped it in linen.
However, if the fracture is in the parts closer to the palm of the hand, then make a bandage-like shape, and tell the patient to grip it with their injured hand; afterwards, wrap it firmly with a long bandage.
And let the splint be of leather with linen over it so that the leather touches the palm; and wrap it as needed.
However, if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint over it and another splint under the palm of the hand, so that the hand is open and straight; then apply a bandage, as the hand is wrapped back towards the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as needed; and afterward, it should be wrapped with the palm, and if you wish, place a small, straight splint on it to keep the fracture elevated and prevent movement.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the pinky finger, then it should be bandaged and bound with the finger adjacent to it; or all the fingers should be bound together, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and afterwards, the placement of a hot abscess and its opposition – with your operation – with what is necessary when any of these conditions occur, as described in the account of it.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is recognised by the feeling that it has shifted forward and to a more superior position.
And the restoration of it is done by tightening a bandage over the fracture and another bandage beneath the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and on their face.
And then the attendant extends all the bandage to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is near the root of the thigh, then the bandage should be near the knee in the direction of the hip joint, so that the extension takes place in that direction, and the other bandage is below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the bandage should be applied near the knee, and the extension should be in the directions below.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both their hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is joined with a good union; then it is necessary to apply a poultice and a bandage, otherwise the limb will develop an abscess. But if an abscess develops there, then you leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: you must wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, in the lower directions, and bring the ends of the splint from the highest point on both parts, and then cover the leg and thigh with any excess bandage material.
And then place splints on the thigh over the fractured area, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is consolidated.
Then begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture area with three or four wraps, and as you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, your bandage should become smaller, lighter, and softer.
And then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and hold both ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not shift from its position.
And then leave the bandage on it, so that no ulceration, abscess, or inflammation, or similar ailments occur in the limb; and if any of these occur, then remove it carefully and correct what occurred immediately, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding, then you must push it back in as much as possible; and if not, leave it alone and treat the surrounding area of the affected spot until it heals.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh or other similar injuries are held together with splints, as we have said in the treatment of the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this way, the patient does not develop limping; and if it is restored alone, or if the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one that has always limped does so.
And know that the thigh is splinted for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all of this depends on the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap (patella).
Know that a fracture of the kneecap is often severe, but it sometimes results in a lot of abrasion/friction.
However, if it is a fracture, then it is merely a crack or hole in its parts, with or without a wound, and this is all according to the feeling/sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what is broken in its parts is bound together with ligaments until it is joined and connected as much as possible by means of adjustment and ease and manufacture.
And then apply a plaster and place it over that round shape, if needed; and compress it firmly with suitable tightening.
And now consider all the treatments we have described in other fractures, similarly, as with abscesses and similar conditions, to counteract any accident with what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is slender, and is called the shinbone.
And the treatment for any type of fracture affecting these two leg bones is similar to the treatment for arm fractures; therefore, the method of restoration is the same.
And if both bones are broken at once, the leg is deformed in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed forward.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you find that one beneath, and it is among those things that are not hidden from you.
And then apply extension, straightening, and bandaging to the splints, according to that same method.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive, with many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And if there is any need for surgery on the arm in connection with the leg, and after you have set the splints and completed your procedure, then you will take pieces of wood from a tree, which are placed under the splints which are placed in bandages and likewise made of palm branches; choose those of a small thickness and not at all large, and make their length according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the lowest point.
And then wrap each splint in two layers of cloth according to its length, and place one on the leg on one side, and the other on the other side, extending from the knee down to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places: at the two ends and the middle.  For with this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to either the right or left side, and is kept in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, matching the length of the leg, has been placed on it so that the leg is held in place, preventing movement.
And this is especially necessary to do in cases of fracture, and when there is a wound properly.
And from now on, check the leg daily and monitor it closely, looking for signs of abscess, inflammation, or other similar problems. If any such condition arises, apply what is needed until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel and bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel bone itself is not common, but sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the foot.  Fractures of the toes are uncommon, and they mostly just experience friction or bruising from their position.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot, and those bones are displaced, the patient should place their foot flat on the ground, as if walking.
Then place the other foot on whatever is raising those bones, and gently press and push them until they return to their proper places.
Then apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small, flat board with two padded ends under the sole of the foot.
Then firmly bind that area with downward pressure, since you have wrapped it with cloth and other materials as needed.
And after three or four days, then loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones rejoined, or broken, or dislocated, and the procedure for those cases is as you know.
But if any bones are broken, then set them, and rejoin them in the same way as I have described to you in the setting of the fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, slightly wider than the finger itself; and then place the board described earlier under the foot, and bind it with downward pressure.
But if the broken bones are two, three, or more, then you place a splint of appropriate size over each finger, wrapped in linen; and secure the board at the bottom of the foot, and place one end of the board outside the belly of the foot, to hold it securely.
And it is important that you do not forget that you must consider all the points discussed regarding other fractures and accidents, and prepare yourself for any accident with the necessary means.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the hip joint, the hip bone, and the penis in men.
When the hip joint of a woman is fractured, then make a splint, carefully shaped; then gently incline it towards the back a little, and keep it held behind.
And then the midwife fills the joint with wool, shaping it into the joint's shape, and the woman should be laid, gradually raising her back, until the fractured joint and bone are realigned.
Then place pillows on her back, and when she wants to urinate, the wool will be removed easily and she will urinate; and reposition and return it to the initial method; then return to her original pillows.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and insert a reed cannula into its opening; then place the entire bladder inside the joint, and gently blow air into the cannula until the bladder is inflated inside the joint, for this will help the fracture to mend.
And then fill it with wool and leave it for several days as previously stated, until healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and setting what we have described for a fractured hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these immediate fractures and those that heal slowly, especially for someone who has studied and used and understands this my book's words; for this fracture signifies to you, in the most opportune ways, the proper method of restoration and tightening, so understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis, take a bird's wing, place it on the penis; then place a cloth bandage over that and wrap it around.  Let it remain there for three days or thereabouts, and it will heal.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of the bones when there is a plague.
It is appropriate for someone who suffers a fracture with plague, especially if the bone is large, like the bone of the thigh or the shin, or similar to those, that you bleed them (perform phlebotomy) immediately if the conditions for phlebotomy are as we have stated above.
And if the plague causes blood to flow, then you should apply a constricting bandage to stop it, and if there's nothing else available, sprinkle powdered crushed rock on it.
And then begin to restore the fracture on the same day, and do not delay this, unless there is an abscess developing.
But if a severe abscess develops, then you should leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it in the third and fourth days, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominently protruding from the skin, then you must carefully reduce it and hold it in place with your hands using gentle force and little stretching.
However, if you are not prepared to gently manipulate and reposition it with your hands, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the extent of the injury; and therefore, it is necessary for the physician to use three or four of them, depending on the quantity needed for the treatment of any kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and of little thickness, so that it is not pressed or bent on the affected area during the operation.
And it should have a sharp tip, with edges on its ends; and it should have a thick middle part, tapering towards the ends.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem,"  which is a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sharp end be placed raised above the prominent bone edge.
And you push it in one go until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some equation (or, more literally, "with some consistency").
Therefore, strive to align the fractured ends, and if the broken end is fragile, and does not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone until the instrument has the power to do so.
But if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with what is suitable for bone cutters, as we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws, in whatever way is possible for you.
And then smooth out anything rough or with small scales that remains on the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and then do what you believe will greatly benefit the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and especially if it is summer, and do not put a plaster or any oily substance on the wound, so that it does not cause suppuration and corruption.
And then apply splints at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered, so that you cut the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to compress the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish doctors have done this, and caused death or a corrosive or wasting illness in their patients, which is to say gangrene. And if your constriction is slight, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear any of the bad accidents that we have recounted, and you find pain in the area, then you should not put splints on it, but rather make a bandage of hard cloths instead of splints and bind it tightly with these.
And when approximately one or two days after the wound has begun to fester, a scab has formed on it, then you remove the cloth you had placed over it with wine, and then apply some ointments, with which, according to our custom, we medicate wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And you should loosen the bandage and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is healed and closed, and the limb should be prepared in such a way that the pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound has passed many days and is not healed nor is the scab detached from it, then you should know that there are obstacles to healing, for which it is necessary to examine the wound with probing. And of those obstacles, those that are separable should be removed and extracted; those that are not separable and are causing the limb pain, force you to cut them, according to what is possible for you.
And if pus or other kinds of corruption or putrefaction occur in the wound, that is, inflammation, then you should apply to that part whatever is appropriate for the treatment described in the relevant chapter of the cure.
And in this, where my discourse should end and you should apply your skill: when a large bone is fractured, support the limb as you would support the thigh bone and similar to large limb bones, and do not attempt to pull or remove it, because often death results from that.  Leave it until it can set, for it will often fall into place on its own after about 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if it is needed, but otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of swelling that occurs in the area of a broken bone.
Often this swelling occurs in the area of healing from a fracture, especially when near joints, and it disfigures the shape of the limb; and it greatly hinders the limb's natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, cerate, and similar things; use some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or sour wine, and place this mixture on the swelling over a bandage, and bind it tightly.
And leave the binding in place for several days without loosening it; and then loosen it and apply another, until the swelling is gone; or apply a lead sheet firmly over it, for lead has the property of reducing any swelling in the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already become hard and dense and it is necessary to remove it forcibly, then cut it through the most prominent part, and remove the excess or cut it with sharp instruments until it is gone; and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural state.
When a broken bone is set, and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, this is not due to a single cause, but rather multiple factors: one being the excessive loosening or improper binding; or an excessive tightening of the ligaments, obstructing the flow of nutrients to the limb; or excessive application of poultices; or unnecessary movement at the wrong time; or a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the treatment for this is to give the patient nourishment and to make their body gentle until the blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce cheerfulness and joy into their life.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water poultices until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and the necessary operation is prevented.
When the limb, already treated, shows signs of crookedness and protrusion of the broken bone and swelling, and this causes deformity of the limb, but nonetheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the assertion of someone claiming that the limb is newly broken.
But many foolish physicians and bone setters have done this in our land; and this practice is greatly to be condemned, leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which soothing herbs are steeped, such as marshmallow leaves and root, melilot, and similar ones; and a softening poultice should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of marshmallow root should be made by mixing it with henbane seed or beeswax oil, and applied as a poultice; or fatty figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar medicinal ingredients known for their healing properties.
And sometimes it is dissolved with persistent gentle friction using damp hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in all directions at all hours.
But if the crookedness has progressed and become firm, hardened, and resistant to treatment, then it is necessary to stop the pus, dissolve the continuation of the bone, and cut away any excess swelling from the bone itself using sharp instruments, and administer relief carefully and diligently.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, preventing movement, distorting the shape of the limb, and causing the patient great pain and injury.
Therefore, when a dislocation occurs, it is essential to act quickly to reduce it, and not delay the reduction, for if delayed, the joint becomes inflamed and hardened, making dislocation reduction difficult.
And therefore, it is not appropriate to move or stretch it during inflammation, for it often causes spasms and prolonged pain.
But when this occurs, you should apply leeches to the inflamed area of the patient, and it should be left until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be soaked in warm water and oil, and then reduced with ease.
And every limb is thus healed, from which the recollection will return to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation, following the precedent in fractures; from the most superior part of the body to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So, if both jaws are dislocated, and the dislocation is done in one of two ways: either they are displaced from their places by slight movement, and are slightly softened; or they are dislocated by complete dislocation, so much so that they are softened at the joint point, to the point where the patient is drooling and unable to hold their mouth shut, and their tongue is wrapped up in their mouth.
However, when the dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and severe, then a gentle reduction must be administered immediately, and not delayed at all.
And let the attendant hold the patient's head, and let the physician place the index finger of one hand into the site of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside to assist; and command the patient to move their jaw and pull it from each side; and the physician should grasp the jaw and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil, so that the reduction of both jaws is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And thus, when they return and are grasped, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over them cloths with a cerot (plaster), made of wax and rubbed oil; and then be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; let their food be soft and easy to consume, until the pain subsides and the jaws are healed, then let them eat whatever they wish.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not exert pressure on opening the mouth for eating, drinking, or breathing, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, often causes fevers and persistent sweating, and the patient's stomach is very easily upset, and there is a lot of vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is a sign of death; and often the one who experiences this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated inwardly by its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is evident to the touch.
And the restoration is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then press the area firmly with your palm, for it will return.
And then apply a poultice and powders, and compress that.
However, the extremity that is connected to the elbow and continues from it is not dislocated in a single day; therefore, it must be reduced and bound according to what we have said and according to your equipment.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and a bandage, and command the patient to maintain quietness and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated, but in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the upper part and the other at the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and this is done hard; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and comes out at the lower parts towards the upper part, and especially in those whose flesh is sparse, it is easily dislocated; but in those with a lot of flesh, it is difficult to get it out.
And often, men suffer a blow or injury, and their muscle becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and is swollen because it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you should examine this thoroughly, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts towards the clavicle; so that you distinguish between the dislocated and healthy muscle, for you find a clear difference between them; and you find a hollow at the head of the muscle, and below the clavicle, near the head of the muscle, approximately like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor can they move it at all.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a mild reduction of this dislocation is possible when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And its reduction is that the physician raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; then place your fingers under the patient's clavicle, and raise the joint forcefully back into its place, and the physician raises the patient's hand and extends it upwards; then place it underneath, as it will return easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a long time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient must be bathed in warm water and be given a poultice that softens and smooths, as if made of the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should lie on their back, and a wool pad that is firm yet loose be placed under their clavicle.  Then the physician places their heel on the pad and lifts the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand so it does not move downwards.  Another physician should hold the patient's head so that it does not move down, for the dislocation will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this way:  Have a man present who is taller than the patient, and have him stand on the affected side.  He should place his arm under the patient's clavicle and lift the clavicle upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, while another person pulls the patient's hand away from the lower part of their torso. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with the patient to weigh them down, so the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another way: a long, roughly cylindrical post, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is placed on the ground beneath the patient.  Over the post's top are placed heavy cloths, and the patient stands in a line with the post.  The patient's hand is then extended downwards, and the patient's body is pulled strongly to the opposite side, and the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a post measuring approximately two arms in length and four fingers in width, and its thickness should be slightly wider than the width of the clavicle in this shape:
And over the rounded top, heavy cloths should be tied so that the post does not injure the patient.
And then place it under the patient's collarbone, and extend the arm or hand downward toward the post, securing the post to the support, arm, and hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a stepladder according to the width, the hand extended downwards, and the other side of the body suspended from the opposite side, so that the joint quickly returns to its place.
And when the reduction is complete, in any way possible, then you should place wool padding under the patient's collarbone; then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder with wine and egg white over the entire muscle, wrapping it securely, and then firmly bind below the collarbone with a proper bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be bound to the collarbone.  Leave it undisturbed for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light, until the limb is strengthened, for this is the best way for their recovery.
And then the diet should be gradually increased, after seven or five days, and exercise should be introduced; and if it is then firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint has been dislocated many times due to moisture or another cause, then cauterization with three holes should be administered, as described previously in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and bandage are reapplied several times, but it is still not firm, and the arm cannot be raised above the shoulder, then understand that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are now cut, stretched, or softened, and know that the joint will never return to its proper position.
For dislocations of the foot and ankle or the back, reduction is achieved by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area and treated until healed.
And if after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until the heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original natural state.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of elbow dislocations.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so in the front than in the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident under the sight or the touch, depending on how it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hand, this is clearly evident to you, for the joint is hollow and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you treat it, and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it becomes acutely inflamed, for if it becomes acutely inflamed, the reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed; and more so when it is on the back part, for it is worse than all other types of dislocations, and more painful, and often results in death from it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that his hand is extended with two hands, and his arm is stretched out, and the physician's hands are on the elbow and underneath it, and he presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then it returns by bending the hand with his hand, until he strikes the elbow with the base of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer forceful extension and strong pressure.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension so that it is not pulled away from the extension.
And then the arm is turned in every direction, as it is wrapped in a long doubled cloth over his hand, or bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoints it with some oil to aid in lubricating the joint with ease; and then he presses the joint with forceful pressure until it returns.
And then, after the reduction, a compress containing astringent and egg white should be applied, it should be bound tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the neck and left there for several days.
And then it is released; and if the joint is now properly formed, then remove the bandage and leave it alone.
And if you see the joint is not sufficiently tightened, then you reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then release it.
But if, during the reduction of the joint, the condition worsens, and the movement of the injured part is restricted, then apply moist heat, gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place a soft, oily substance over the joint; and then bind it, and leave it on the affected area day and night. And then remove it, and place it in a bath; and when it is soaked, then rub the joint with moderate friction, and then reapply the oil-based substance, once, and the second, and the third time. with intervals of bathing, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a warm, damp cow's hide over the affected area and bind it tightly; and do this several times, for this is moist and restores the area to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the malassen, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
Dislocations of the hand sometimes occur, and the reduction of its dislocation is relatively easy; the opposite is true for other joints. However, speed is required in its reduction when it is dislocated, before the area becomes inflamed or a harmful abscess forms there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table, and the assistant extends the hand, and the physician places their hand or palm on the area of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its proper place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already been turned toward the back of the hand, in which case the patient places the back of their hand on a table to facilitate the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is toward the back of the hand, then the position of the patient's hand must be on the table in such a way that the physician's hand can be placed over that joint area.
And if it returns quickly, that is good; but if not, then gently apply a bandage to prevent inflammation and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, it will not tolerate nor be possible to effect the reduction after several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains in its twisted position, and the patient doesn't feel any cause for this, but rather that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are severed or torn, and in this case, there is no remedy but to cauterize the affected area with a cautery iron, and apply a strong pressure bandage.
Therefore, once the injury has been reduced, place the bandage we described on it, and allow it to remain applied and compressed for five days. Afterward, it should be removed and left alone, and the patient's hand should be exercised.
But if the movement of the injury is obstructed and you experience some weightiness, then you should alleviate this with warm water and some friction several times, until it is eased.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in any direction.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated either to the side of the palm or to its underside, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation away using your fingers until it returns to its place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it over the side to which it was dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend and extend it until it is properly aligned in its socket.
And when night comes, bandage it according to that description, and do not cease unbandaging it during the day and exercising it with movement, and bandage it at night; and do this for a few days until it is straightened.
And do the same if it is dislocated to the underside of the hand, and bandage it to that side and do your first procedure until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated to the other parts of the body.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation occurs in any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and the affected vertebra remains significantly displaced from its place, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowels are expelled involuntarily, and cannot be retained; and often some limbs of the patient, or two legs or two arms, or one of those, are paralyzed, or a vertebra remains out of place, for many times it is displaced, and often the displacement occurs at multiple points.
Therefore, this is already displaced in the posterior parts and is called a sprain.
But the treatment for this is that you must consider if the sprain occurred due to childhood injuries, for then in that case there is no cure, nor is the complete healing of it possible.
However, for sprains that occur due to accidents, or impacts, or similar causes, the ancients have described detailed methods of treatment, from which the most beneficial can be gleaned. But I have abbreviated this, avoiding the lengthy discussion, due to my proximity to the intention of my explanation, with which I have now presented and structured this information using various tools, through which I will demonstrate and expand upon this.
And of those, only the one that occurs on the back specifically, with what I recommend, is curable.
And it is that the patient be laid on their face, near a wall, and be supported beneath them with a soft material so that their foot is not disturbed.
And then a straight piece of wood should be placed, fixed into the ground at the edge of the wall near the head, and another piece of wood similarly placed at the other edge of the wall beneath the patient's feet.
And one attendant should hold the first piece of wood, and it should not be fixed in the ground; and another attendant should hold the other piece in a similar fashion.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head, and firmly tie it to the bolster (or similar support).  Extend the end of the bandage to the wooden support near the head and tie it there; then tighten the other bandage around the patient's hips and legs; and finally, tie all the bandages to the other wooden support beneath the patient's feet.
And now, each attendant should stretch the bandage towards the wooden support, and the two supports and the bandages should remain in their respective positions, though they should not be fixed as we have described.
And now the physician places their palms on the spine with strength until it returns to its proper position, or places a table beneath it, and then adjusts the patient's feet on the table until the spine returns to its proper position.
But if it doesn't return in this way, then take a table whose width or length is approximately three arms' length, and carve a recess in the wall mentioned, near the patient, into which the end of the table will fit. And then place the middle of the table upon the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, turn the mechanism that is held in your hand; and you shall place on the ground, behind the patient's head, two pieces of wood, each one arm's length long, and behind the patient's feet, two similar pieces of wood; and the distance between each piece of wood shall be a palm; and already a hole has been made in each piece of wood through which the mechanism can be passed, and each shall be firmly secured to the ground so that they do not move.
And a round piece of wood, on which the mechanism is placed, should be turned in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at the end of that is a hole in which the piece of wood is fitted, the length of which is a palm, along with the mechanism; and in the other two pieces of wood is a similar hole.
And then tighten the bindings you have placed around the patient's feet in the hole behind their head, and the bindings you have placed around their thighs in the mechanism behind their feet.
And then, next to each mechanism, a helper should turn the mechanism with their hand, while the physician aligns the vertebra as we have described.
And then, once the vertebra is returned and the area is aligned, you should apply a thick plaster of egg white, then some strips of cloth; then place a wooden splint over the plaster, whose width should be about three fingers, and its length sufficient to cover the area of the swelling, and extend over the affected part of the vertebra.
And then bind it with the bandage as described, and provide the patient with subtle nourishment until they are healed.
And if any lingering issue remains in the area at the end of healing, then medicines that soften and soothe should be administered, with the method of the table (as previously mentioned), for a long time; and a lead plate should also be applied to that area.
And sometimes, a protrusion occurs in the lower vertebrae of the spine, caused by that dislocation;  and it is that bone that is protruding, so you should not use this remedy on it, for it will likely cause your death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint can only be dislocated, and what happens to other joints with slight movement and concavity does not happen to them.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and less so forwards or backwards.
A sign of an inward dislocation is that when you adjust the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the groin, and the area where the groin is swollen is hot and inflamed, because the head of the thigh bone has already reached that spot.
The sign of a dislocation outwards is that the symptoms are opposite to this one.
A sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient extends their leg to its fullest, but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if forced to go forward cannot do so without pain in the front.  The patient's urine is retained and their groin is inflamed, and they experience difficulty walking on their heel.
A sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can the patient bend their knee to reach their groin, and the affected leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is swollen, and the head of the thigh bone is in that area.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations requires you to consider if the dislocation is old and longstanding, having already been present for a while, then you administer a reduction;  if it remains in the same position after reduction, then the treatment is not complete, and no presumption should be made about its success.
However, for one whose dislocation is recent, and is one of the four types, you must work to return the joint, extending it both inwards and outwards, and gently to the right and left, for if it returns easily, it requires no further treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the patient's leg from the lower part, either with the patient's own hands or with a bandage wrapped around the leg above the knee;  another assistant should extend the patient's upper hand so that the patient's hand is under their armpit;  then tighten a strong bandage around the root of the thigh, and a third assistant should hold the end of the bandage.
And the extension should be either from the inside of the knee to the groin area, or from the back to the back area (buttocks).
And apply this extension in all directions until the patient is lifted off the ground by their body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common procedure for all four (types).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; but if not, then it is necessary for all cases to consider the specific treatment.
In the specific reduction of a dislocation, when it is dislocated and the dislocation is on the inner side, the patient should be laid on their right side; then place the bandages over the root of the thigh, in the area between the thigh's head and the area below the groin; and then extend the bandage from the groin area up to the highest part of the groin.
And then another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, and firmly hold the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with strong pulling, for that will return it to its place.
And this type is easier to cure, with which this limb is reduced, than the other types.
But if you are excused from and do not comply with this method of treatment entirely, then it is necessary to tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers' width, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by about two fingers' width.
And then the patient should be suspended from the top of a pole, and that should be indoors; and the distance from the ground should be about two arms' length.
And then command a strong young man to grasp the head of the thigh, and let another young man, holding the patient, and the other young man pulling the thigh with strength, push; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when there is a dislocation outwards, it is necessary for the patient to lie on a pillow, as we have previously recounted regarding a bent knee; and let the bandage be tight around the affected leg and on the patient's feet.
Then two poles should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at the head; and then poles should be placed fitted in the middle of a hill upon which damp cloths are wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the pole is between their thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend in their direction, and the physician, with his hand, guides the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the anterior parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same procedure on a hill; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected limb, and then grasps it with his other hand, and with this grasping, he extends it downwards towards the side of the injured area.
And if the dislocation is in the posterior parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, but rather elevated above the ground; it is necessary that they be placed on a hard surface, just as it is necessary for the extraction if their shoulder is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about their lying on two pillows; and they are on their face and their ligaments are stretched, just as we have said, not much.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a cloth over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is complete, as is necessary – and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient grips their leg and extends it without excuse, then know that the limb, as is necessary, is returned – and then you align the two sides and apply a bandage and tighten it with a bandage so that the shoulder is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then remove the bandage and the splint, and compare one leg with the other.
Now, if you see those equalities (or congruencies) in appearance, then know that the dislocation has already been fixed, and then allow the patient to go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you return and bandage that, and tighten it according to your original method of tightening, and leave it for three days.
And then remove it, and have the patient walk with support for a few days, until it is strong again.
Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards,  but not backwards, and not forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that if you tell the patient to bend their leg at the thigh, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these cases is to have the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg as far as possible; grasp their leg close to the knee, and place the patient's foot between your feet; then grip the patient's knee with your palms, holding them between your fingers firmly on the knee, and another assistant should push down on the foot until the knee returns to its proper place.
And the sign of its reduction is that their leg will bend at the thigh with a smooth, effortless movement.
And then bandage that area and bind the leg to the thigh with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days until they are healed and strong.
But if the reduction is beyond your ability, then administer strong traction with the supports which were previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes dislodged by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and the dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the heel swells on the side towards which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for the small displacement is a gentle repositioning; it should be extended easily with the hands and held until it returns to its place.
But in treating a complete dislocation, you should have the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable assistant stand behind the patient's legs, in the middle of the affected area.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from the highest part, and with your left hand, from the lower part of the foot, in the area of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left hand, twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot of the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two attempts using this method, and if you see the foot swollen, that is good; and if not, then repeat the procedure, because that one [the foot] returns.
However, if you are unable to reduce it with what we have described, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a tightly fixed piece of wood on the ground between their thighs, and you have already covered them with a cloth so that the patient does not injure themselves.
And then the attendant holds the patient's thighs, and another attendant extends the leg with their hands or a bandage tied around the leg's joint; and then each attendant pulls against the other's pull, and the piece of wood is stretched between the patient's thighs, holding that part, so that the patient's body is not pulled in directions below their extension.
And then the physician reduces the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh in the lower areas, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore, when the dislocation returns and the success of the reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and bandage the foot to the leg.
And you must be careful or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage on that area is not too forceful and injures it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from movement for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be re-displaced and corrupted, and that he will not receive a cure.
And if a boil occurs there, then you must apply to it what was previously described in our narrative, in another location for treatment and poultice, until it is gone.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
He who is dislocated must be gently straightened without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some bones of the foot, then you must have the patient place their foot on a flat surface on the ground or a table, and be positioned as if they were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what you believe is the dislocated joint, and then press down gently on it with your foot until it returns to its proper place, and you see that it is now straightened and no longer appears out of position.
And then place a board under the sole of the foot, that supports the entire foot, with two ends, and then secure it with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then loosen it and allow the patient to rest from walking for many days, until the joint is healed and the return to normal movement is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations that occur with a blow, or a fracture, or both.
When any of these causes occur, and you exert yourself in the treatment and restoration, many times death ensues.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such a condition, but only those skilled in the art, with long experience, compassionate and patient, slow, neither rash nor presumptuous; and who, at the beginning, should use only soothing medicines for the inflammation, leaving the patient to divine judgment, unless there is hope of recovery from the sickness, and some prospect of relief from the disease. Then endeavor to perform the reduction as soon as possible, before the inflammation becomes severe.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that reduces inflammation and treats the injury with the appropriate desiccant ointments.
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat their separation, and do in this case what we have said in simple illnesses of similar previous cases.
And, according to your ability, avoid intrusion into the paths of fear, as my teaching has guided you; for this is more beneficial to you from your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more salutary for your true self in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be praised. Amen.
